[Saturday, May 20, 1899.
Eegietered at the General Past Office, Sydney, for Transmission by Post as a Newspaper,


The Ways of Many Waters
Collects the clashing ballads and verses of sailor-men’s
and wharf-men’s life which E. J. Brady has contributed
to The Bulletin and the Sunday Times, Sydney. It is
the first attempt on the part of an Australian verse-
writer to chant the life on the 'sea and the margins of
the sea ; though there is one horse-poem—the story of
“ How Jack Bowlin Steered ‘ Jones.’ ” The volume is
bound in buckram, with gilt top, in a size that will go
into a pocket without squeezing ; and the price is 55.,
post-free.
Roderic Quinn’s selection of verses,
The Hidden Tide,
is issued in a substantial booklet, and special care has
been taken to make the typography attractive. The
edition is limited to 500 numbered copies on small
paper at Is. 9d. posted, and 15 copies on large paper
at 10s. 6d. (posted 11s.). The large-paper edition
contains an unpublished poem in the author’s holo-
graph.
In the volume of
Fair Girls and Gray Horses
Will Ogilvie has given what Victor Daley calls “the
most charming book of verses yet issued in Aus-
tralia.” The second edition is considerably enlarged
and improved, and the publishers have been informed
by a happy lover that “it makes the beautifullest
present.” The price is 4s. 6d. post free.

THE BED PAGE.
"My Lady Sleeps."
BLEEP, ANGBY BEAUTY!
Sleep, angry beauty, sleep and fear not me 1
For who a sleeping lion dares provoke ?
It shall suffioe me here to sit and see
Those lips shut up that never kindly spoke:
What sight can more content a lover’s mind
Than beauty seeming harmless, if not kind ?
My words have charmed her, for secure she sleeps,
Though guilty much of wrong done to my love;
And in her slumber, see ! she closed-eye weeps:
Dreams often more than waking passions move.
Plead, Sleep, my cause, and make her soft like
thee:
That she in peace may wake and pity me.
T. Campion.
LINES.
On seeing my wife and two children sleeping in the
same chamber.
And has the earth lost its so spacious round,
The sky its blue circumference above,
That In this little chamber there is found
Both earth and heaven—my universe of love I
All that my God can give me, or remove,
Here sleeping, save myself,' in mimic death.
Sweet that in this small compass I behove
To live their living, and to breath their breath !
Almost I wish that, with one common sigh,
We might resign all mundane care and strife,
And seek together that transcendent sky,
Where Father, Mother, Children, Husband, Wife,
Together pant in everlasting life.
Thomas Hood.
MY LADY SLEEP.
In cool, gray cloisters walks my Lady Sleep,
Telling her smooth beads slowly, one by one ;
Along the wall the stealthy shadows creep ;
Night holds the world in thrall, and day is done.
Sometimes, while winds sigh soft above her head,
Down the long garden path my Lady strays,
And, kneeling by the pansies’ purple bed,
Counts the small faces on the moonlit haze.
Sometimes she lies upon the silver sands,
Following the sea-birds as they wheel and dip ;
Or idly -clasps, in still persistent hands,
The shining grains that through her fingers slip.
Or paces long, with flowing locks all wet.
Where the low thunder booms forevermore,
And the great waves no man hath numbered yet,
Roll, one by one, to break upon the shore.
Sometimes she counts the brightening twilight
stars,
The daisies smiling in the meadow grass,
The slow kine trailing through the pasture bars,
The white sheep loitering in the mountain pass.
But evermore her hands are cool and calm —
Her quiet voice is ever hushed and low;
And evermore her tranquil lips breathe balm,
And silent as a dream her garments flow.
She comes, she goes—whence, whither—who can
tell?
Angels of God, do ye her secret keep ?
Know ye the talisman, the sign, the spell,
The mystic password of my Lady Sleep ?
Julia C. R. Dorr.
At the Hotel Grenoble.
When the Poet of England Lay Nigh Unto Death.
After the last reporter had gone, buttoning his
overcoat as he faced the stormy night, the clerk at
the desk sat down wearily. The group that re-
mained sitting on the bench over by the wall
where the bulletin hung would not worry him.
Very little hope had these latter found in the news
from the sick-room. They muttered rather than
talked, like men who had exhausted their subject
and had nothing but fragments to offer. Molder,
thin and sallow, was himself a poet. Hollo, the
story-writer, with great robustness of body and
brusque ways, turned to the poet and said :
“ The bigger a doctor the more of a mystery-
monger. That bulletin tells nothing.”
“ I’m thinking of Kipling, not the doctor,” said
Molder —“ the things that Kipling has left unsaid :
the youth of him and the glory of him that will
not die with him.” Molder’s voice died away—not
the faint dying away of a failing thought, but as
if it was gradually withdrawing into the cavern of
I Contemplation where it was continuing to resound
to deeper purpose.
“I’m going,” said the sharp-voiced, sharp-faced,
spectacled-boy at the story-teller’s elbow. He had
haunted the corridor since the poet’s illness was
first announced. “I shall dread taking up the
papers in the morning.”
He was gone.
The story-teller looked about. He had seen it
all so often the last three nights—the lowered
lights, the dimness, the porter sauntering in and
leaning heavily about, the telegraph-messenger
entering and departing after his ticket was signed
—the messenger who added one more bead to the
rosary of love for the poet who lay wrestling the
Dark Angel on the floor above. All the “ color ”
and “ atmosphere ” that were in it, he had taken
in and labelled —perhaps for future use—as Kip-
ling would have done. He stood up with a
recurrence of his old thought:
“ I have watched him from his first beginnings
m India —heard of him queerly from there when
he was sub-editing, when he was in the Swin-
burne, Bret Harte, Edgar Allan Poe parody
stage, and beginning to tell his Indian stories in
the manliest prose of the century. Just think of
it—all that swarming, colorful life of India —a
hundred years of it, the native side, the garrison
side, nine or ten generations of English soldiers
and civilians,, and nobody to tell about it at all till
Kipling took thought and raised the veil. Beal,
too, by thunder; haunting words, haunting pic-
tures, but an idealism of courage and devotion
lighting it all up—-the regimental idea. That’s in
his blood. It was after he went back to England
that the regiment became something bigger. He
fell in with the Jingos and wrote their gospel: he
has been writing it ever since—first in ringing
slang, and now in verse that has the immortalities
about it. But if that were all I wouldn’t be here
to-night. What he preaches may matter to you,
Molder. Neither the upper thought nor the
under-thought of his verses—neither the bugle-
blowing nor the dagger-thrusting appeals to me.
The Eurasian savagery of it, the joy in bloodshed,
the frank acceptance of the mire and stench of the
shambles for the sake of the glory—that repels
me. It feeds massed vanity. For all the clear
clarion in the tone there is confusion in the
moralities of it.”
“He outgrew the regiment: he’ll outgrow the
Jingo,” said Molder, looking up, though he had
not appeared to be listening.
“Outgrow?” said Hollo, bending down and
looking into Molder’s deep eyes. Then he looked
towards the discouraging, all but hopeless, bulle-
tin.
_ “Yes,” said Molder, “ outgrow. If God spares
him he will grow as he has grown : if God takes
him, the silver and gold of him will sift them-
selves from the dross of little politics and con-
tracted aims. Why do you love him, Hollo ? ”
“Because he loves,” said the story-teller, “be-
cause he feels, and toils and tells so robustly: for
the scenes he has pictured ; the inanimate he has
animated ; the unsuspected worlds he has dis-
covered—because—well, because he has chiselled
out rough lovable men in a world of letters devoted'
to finicking women ; because he is so easily what
I, what a thousand of us, would like to be. I love
him—damn it!—l love the sum total of him.”
He pulled his soft hat further down his forehead
and was gone.
' Molder fell asleep. Messenger boys, with tele-
grams and cables, kept coming and going. They
had taken the plaoe of the throng of the day and
the crowd of the evening. The far places of the
world were calling, calling. A party of young
men and women, fresh from a ball, tne dancing-
sparkle still in their eyes, came rustling in, a dozen
or more of them. They came atiptoe, as if it were
the hushed sick-room upstairs, where the poet lay,
and not the public corridor of the hotel. They
stood mute, and sorrowful about the bulletin, and
whispered. Their eyes fell on Molder. They
stood round him and tittered.
He wakened and rubbed his eyes.
“Shadows,” he murmured to himself—“shad-
ows.”
He rose and they fell away before him. “ I
knew they were shadows ” —he said, as he walked
toward the door. He halted, for he heard a foot
on the stair. There was some one coming down.
There would be news.
Then the front door swung open and a mul-
titude, real and tangible now, came trooping in, at
their head three stalwart, red-coatedmen.
Mulvaney, Learoyd, and Ortheris.
Then a group of languid, pale women in their
finery, with world-wise smiles and rich flower-
scents in their hair, with beckonings and sidewise
glances.
Mrs. Hauksbee and her sisters of the station.
At their heels came Otis Yeere and a press of
clinking and clattering young subalterns, and then
the drinking, sallow colonels and majors.
Dark faces under coiled white turbans, men
with strange curved knives at their waists and
hate in their hearts under the servility of their
gestures, came tripping and slinking on bare or
slippered feet.
Indian Princes and Benares priests and beggars
and workers and little Hindoo babies in their
mothers’ arms. And Sepoys and Sikhs, and
Pathans, and Dacoits, and Gunga Din, the water-
bearer.
A pale soldier man, wide-eyed, collarless, with
the Dead March ringing in his ears and the
shadow of a gallows thrown across him from the
sun of morning.
A battle-maddened negro with a spear and
shield, a hero’s deed behind him and a machine-
gun bullet in his breast.
Frowsy writers of Fleet-street and a blind artist
led by a girl—not so clear or real these, for all
their commonness.
Swart men of the straits, ship-captains of the
tropics, engineers grimed and grim, contractors
and coolies.
Now sailors from the Arctic seas with the salt
spray on their oilskins and the wind still tossing
their hair. And railroad men grasping tufts of
blackened cotton waste.
At their heels pressed in a strange army of
animals, the brethren of the jungle, so real, so
strange. For a space all was silence. Then a
voice came from the stairs.
What word did Molder hear ?
What lighted up the faces of these ardently liv-
ing, colorful people that he knew so well ?
What gave the troop of young revellers j-the
people who seemed shadows to the poet’s mind—
their sudden smiles and loosed their tongues,
as they moved to the street ?
What sent the messenger-boys scudding to the
telegraph-offices ?
The word that came down from the sick cham-
ber : Life.
So Molder went home, two glad tears clinging
to his lashes.
J. I. 0. Clarke, in The Criterion.
The Brownings and Blush.
The episode of the stealing of Flush is one
of the most interesting in the new books of
Browning love-letters. Flush was Elizabeth
Barrett’s dog—her dearest possession in the
world, and when he is stolen she is disconso-
late indeed. And she writes to Robert
Browning, her lover—
Here is a distress for me, dearest! I have lost
my poor Flush— lost him ! You were a prophet
when you said “Take cure.”
This morning Arabel and I, and he with us,
went in a cab to Yere-street, where we had a
little business, and he followed us, as usual, into a
shop and out of it again, and was at my heels
when I stepped up into the carriage. Having
turned, I said “Flush,” and Arabel looked round
for Flush—there was no Flush ! He had been
caught up in that moment from tinder the wheels,
do you understand ? and the thief must have run
with him, and thrown him into a bag, perhaps.
It was such a shock to me—think of it! losing
him in a moment, so/ No wonder I looked white,
as Arabel said! So she began to comfort
me by showing me how certain it was that
I should recover him for ten pounds at most, and
we came home ever so drearily. Because Flush
doesn’t know that we can recover him, and he is
in the extremest despair all the while, poor darling
Flush, with his fretful fears, and pretty whims,
and his fancy of being near me. All this night he
will howl and lament, I know perfectly; for I
fear we shall not ransom him to-night. Henry
went down for me directly to the captain of the
banditti —who evidently knew all about it, said
Henry—and, after a little form of consideration
and enquiry, promised to let us hear something
this evening, but has not come yet. In the morh-
ing, perhaps, he will come. Henry told him that
I was resolved not to give much ; but, of course,
they will make me give what they choose. lam
not going to leave Flush at their mercy, and they
know that as well as I do. My poor Flush !
When we shall be at Pisa, dearest, we shall be
away from the London dog-stealers—it will be one
of the advantages.
* * « *
Browning—selfish man ! —expresses a per-
functory grief—
Poor Flush —how sorry I am for you, my Ba!
But you will recover him, I dare say . . . not,
perhaps, directly; the delay seems to justify their
charge at the end : poor fellow —was he no better
than the rest of us, and did all that barking and
fanciful valor spend itself on such enemies as Mr.
Kenyon and myself, leaving only blandness and
waggings of the tail for the man with the bag ? I
am sure you are grieved and frightened for our
friend and follower, that was to be, at Pisa—will
you not write a special note to tell pie when you
get him again ?
• * • •
And Elizabeth is quick to detect the false
note—
“ Our friend and follower that was to be”—is
that, then, your opinion of my poor darling Flush’s
destiny. Ah, I should not have been so quiet if I
had not known differently and better. I “ shall
not recover him directly, ” you think ! But, dear-
est, I am sure that I shall. I am learned in the
ways of the Philistines —I knew from the begin-
ning where to apply and how to persuade. The
worst is poor Flush’s fright and suffering. And
then, it is inconvenient just now to pay the ransom
for him. But we shall have time to-morrow if not
to-night. Two hours ago the chief of the Con-
federacy came to call on Henry and to tell him
that the “Society had the dog,” having done us
the honour of tracking us into Bond-street and
out of Bond-street into Vere-street where
he was kidnapped. Now he is in White-
chapel (poor Flush). And the great man
was going down there at half-past seven
to meet other great men in council and hear the
decision as to the ransom exacted, and would re-
turn with their ultimatum. Oh, the villainy of it
is excellent, and then the humiliation of having to
pay for your own vexations and anxieties ! Will
they have the insolence, now, to make me pay £lO
as they said they would ? But I must have Flush,
you know ; I can’t run any risk, and bargain and
haggle. There is a dreadful tradition in this
neighborhood of a lady who did so, having her
dog’s head sent to her in a parcel. So I say to
Henry, “Get Flush back, whatever you do,” for
Henry is angry, as he may well be, and as I should
be if I were not too afraid . . . and talks police-
officers against thieves, and finds it very hard to
attend to my instructions and be civil and re-
spectful to their captain. There he found him,
smoking a cigar in a room with pictures ! They
make some £3OOO or £4OOO a year by their honor- .
able employment. As to- Flush’s following any-
one “blandly,” never think it. He was caught
up and gagged . . . depend upon that. If he
could have bitten he would have bitten; —if he
could have yelled he would have yelled. Indeed,
on a former occasion the ingenuous thief observed
that he- “ was a difficult dog to get, he was so dis-
trustful.” They had to drag him with a string,
put him in a oab, they said, before. Poor Flush !
* * * *
Then Robert rises to the occasion—plainly,
it must be taken seriously ; but he does not
rise with a good grace. He is a little
aggrieved that his troubles should be tem-
porarily forgotten in the greater trouble of
Flush; and he lectures his lady on lofty
grounds of public morality.
I am rejoiced that poor Flush is found again,
dearest —altogether rejoiced.
And now that you probably have him by your
side, I will tell you what I should have done in
such a case, because it explains our two ways of
seeing and meeting oppression lesser or greater.
I would not have given five shillings on that
fellow’s application. I would have said —and in
entire earnestness — you are responsible for the pro-
ceedings of your gang, and you I mark—don’t talk
nonsense to me about cutting off heads or paws.
Be as sure as that I stand here and tell you, I will
spend my whole life in putting you down, the
nuisance you declare yourself and by every
imaginable means I will be the death of you and
as many of your accomplices as I can discover—
but you I have discovered and will never lose sight
of—now try my sincerity, by delaying to produce
the dog by to-morrow. And for the ten pounds—
see ! Whereupon I would give them to the first
beggar in the street. You think I should receive
Flush’s head ? Perhaps— so God allows matters to
happen L on purpose, it may be, that I should vin-
dicate him by the punishment I would exact.
Observe, Ba, this course ought not to be yours,
because it could not be—it would not suit your
other qualities. But all religion, right, and jus-
tice, with me, seem implied in such a resistance to
wickedness and refusal to multiply it a hundred-
fold—for this prompt payment of ten pounds for
a few minutes’ act of the easiest villainy,
there will be encouragement to—how many similar
acts in the course of next month ? And how will
the poor owners fare who have not money enough
for their dogs’ redemption ? I suppose the gentle-
man, properly disgusted with such obstinacy, will
threaten roasting at a slow fire to test the sincerity
of attachment! No—the world would grow too
detestable a den of thieves and oppressors that
way !
* * • •
But Elizabeth will have none of the moral
grounds : she wants Flush, and her loving
instinct makes pitiful hay of Browning’s
arguments.
Poor Flush ! ... I have not got him baok yet
—no, indeed.
I should have done it. The arohfiend, Taylor,
the man whom you are going to spend your life in
persecuting (the life that belongs to me, too !),
came last night to say that they would accept six
pounds, six guineas, with half a guinea for him-
self, considering the trouble of the mediation ; and
Papa desired Henry to refuse to pay, and not to
tell me a word about it—all of which I did not
find out till this morning. Now it is less, as the
money goes, than I expected, and I was very
vexed and angry, and wanted Henry to go at
once and conclude the business—only he wouldn’t ;
talked of Papa, and persuaded me that Taylor
would come to-day with a lower charge. He has
not come —I knew he would not come—and if
people won’t do as I choose, I go down
to-morrow morning myself and bring Flusli back
with me. Ail this time he is suffering, and I
am suffering. It may be very foolish —I do not say
it is not—or it may be “awful sin,” as Mr. Boyd
sends to assure me—but I cannot endure to run
oruel hazards about my poor Flush for the sake of
a few guineas, or even for the sake of abstract
principles of justice—l cannot. You say that I
cannot, . . . but that you would. You would !
Ah, dearest—most pattern of citizens, but you
would not— l know you better. Your theory is
far too good not to fall to pieces in practioe.' A
man may love justice intensely ; but the love of an
abstract principle is not the strongest love—now is
it ? Let us consider a little, putting poor Flush
out of the question. (You would bear, you say, to
receive his head in a paroel—it would satisfy
you to cut off Taylor’s in return.) Do
you mean to say that if the banditti came
down on us in Italy and carried me off to the
mountains, and, sending to you one of my ears, to
show you my probable fate if you did not let them
have . . how much may I venture to say lam
worth ? . . five or six scudi —(is that reasonable
at all?) . . would your answer be “Not so many
orazie ” ; and would you wait, poised upon abstract
principles, for the other ear, and the catastrophe,—
as was done in Spain not long ago? Would you,
dearest ? Because it is well to know beforehand,
perhaps. *
“ Unreasonable woman ! ” But Browning
humours her.
Dear Ba, —I wrote under the notion (as I said)
that poor Flush was safe by your side, and only
took that occasion to point out what I must still
consider the wrongness of the whole system of
giving way to, instead of opposing, such proceed-
ings. I think it lamentable weakness . . . though
I can quite understand and allow for it in you;
but Weakness it essentially is, as you know per-
fectly.
. . . You have a great answer—“ What should
I do if you were to be the victim ? ” That my
note yesterday, the second one, told you. I sacri-
fice myself ... all that belongs to me; but there
are some interests which I belong to —I have no
right, no more than inclination, in such a case, to
think of myself if your safety is concerned, and
as I could cut off a limb to save my head, so my
head should fall most willingly to redeem yours.
... I would pay every farthing I had in the
world, and shoot with my own hand the receiver
of it after a chase of fifty years—esteeming that to
be a very worthy recompense for the trouble.
But why write all this string of truisms about
the plainest thing in the world? All reformers
are met at the outset by such dissuasion from their
efforts. Better suffer the grievance and get off as
cheaply as you (can); You, Mahomet—what if
the Caaba he only a black stone ? You need only
bow your head as the others, and make any in-
ward remark you like on the blindness of the
people. You, Hampden, have you really so little .
wit as to contest payment of a paltry 20s. at such
risk?
* * * *
And presently Flush is restored, and the
incident is ended, having cast much light on
the characters of the famous correspondents.
I have not Flush yet. I am to have him to-
morrow morning.
And for the Flush-argument, dear dearest, I hold
that your theory is entirely good and undeniable.
I agree with you throughout it, praising Mahomet,
praising Hampden ... But Flush, poor Flush.
Flush who has loved me so faithfully; have I a
right to sacrifice him in his innocence ? . . . Does
not Flush’s condition assimilate to my own among
the banditti? for you agree that • you would not,
after all, leave me to the banditti —and I, exactly
on the same ground, will not leave Flush. It
seems to me that you and I are at one upon the
whole question —only that I am your Flush, and
fee is mine. You, if you were “consistent” . . .
dearest! . . . would not redeem me on my own
account. You do ever so much harm by it,
observe—you produce catastrophe on catastrophe,
just for the sake of my two ears without earrings !
Oh, I entirely agree with your principle. Evil
should be resisted that it may flee from you.
But Flush is not to be sacrificed —nor even is
Ba, it appears. So our two weaknesses may
pardon one another, yours and mine ! . . . Flush
arrived here at eight o’clock (at the very moment
with your letter, dearest!) and the first thing that
he did was to dash up to this door, and then to
drink his purple cup full of water, filled three
times over. He was not so enthusiastic about
seeing me, as I expected—he seemed bewildered
and frightened—and whenever anyone said to him
“Poor Flush, did the naughty men take you
away?” he put up his head and moaned and
yelled. He has been very unhappy certainly.
Dirty he is, and much thinner, and continually he
is drinking. * Six guineas was his ransom—and
now I have paid twenty for him to the dog-
stealers.

Bayswater, Sydney, October, 1898.
W. H. Malyon, Esq., Man. Chemist, Ballarat.
Dear Sir,—For years past (about ten), I have been
troubled w th “ Acne ” in its worst form, and have sought
advice from the best Australian and London doctors, with
only fair results. A Victorian friend of mine, some four
or five weeks ago, sent me a tin of your “ Ligmolliene,”
and speaking very highly of its wonderful curative powers.
I naturally rock same “ Cum Grano Sails, ’’ and was in-
clined not to use it ; eventually I tried your ointment
with truly marvellous results, and am now entirely cured ;
and think people who suffer from skin diseases are greatly
indebted to you for your wonderful remedy. I shall
advise ail my friends with skin troubles to use same.
Wishing “ Ligomolliene ” all the success it richly deserves,
Yours faithfully, GEORGE J. O’REGAN.
WHAT THE DOCTORS SAY
Doveton-street, Ballarat,
M.v dear Mr. Malyon,— August 21st, 1898.
With great pleasure I now sit down to record progress
with regard; to my old enemy, “Eczema Capitis,” from

sincerely, THOS. LE GAY HOLT HOUSE, M.R.C.S., L.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
jRe wireless telegraphy (8., 15/4/'99, p. 13).
About 13 years ago the men who ran Milton and
Milburn railway stations, in Maoriland, used to
be startled by having their telephonic conversa-
tions broken in on by a weird voice from nowhere.
Later on, it was discovered that the owner was
the postmaster at Outram, 30 miles away. A tele-
graph wire from Outram to Tapanui, after
travelling a few miles on the same posts as the
Outram-Mosgiel telephone wire, branched off and
ran parallel for some miles, at a distance of three-
quarters of a mile, to the Milton-Milburn tele-
phone wire. Experts said “induction,” but what
induction was and how to control or utilise it they
didn’t know, and the Outram man continued to
irrupt into Milton-Milburn conversations in a
manner productive of “ language.”
At End of a Holiday.
“Leaves and brambles from hill and hollow
Come and gather,” the children cried ;
“The sun goes down, and the night will follow,
A moonless night on the dark hillside.
All ways they wandered —the dry twigs snapping,
With laugh and prattle and song between ;
Down on the rock the waves were lapping,
The sweet swell swaying the seaweed green.
And she stood by in her white sun-bonnet,
How snowy-white on her tressy hair !
With a gold king-beetle dreaming on it
A lotus dream in the lustrous air.
Was it love, or a dove in the tall tree cooing !
Was it love, or a dove that loitered nigh ?
The eventide is the hour for wooing,
But I was silent and she was shy.
Then suddenly rose a far faint humming—
A growing noise in the evening hush,
And the talk of children homeward coming,
Laden with spoil of the gold-brown bush.
“ Leaves and brambles from hill and hollow ;
The way was tangled and tangles tire.
The sun goes down and the night will follow,
Now down on your knees and make us a fire. ’
The leaves were wet and my hopes were slender,
The brambles damp with a shower at noon ;
She bent to help, and my heart of tinder,
Ah, why did it burst to flame so soon !
“Dry leaves, dry leaves from the twilight forest,
Or bark that is sheltered, or hidden ferns ;
Dry leaves, dry leaves,” the children chorused,
And, “ the drier the leaf the redder it burns.”
The fire leapt up with a sudden glancing,
The first flame flushing her hands of snow ;
And round about went the children dancing,
Their faces lit by the rosy glow.
That fire has gone beyond all returning,
For wild winds scatter and chill rains drench :
All dust the leaves ; but a fire is burning
That wind or water shall never quench.
Leaves and brambles from hill and hollow,
And two together, and violet eyes :
The sun goes down, and Love must follow,
A quenchless fire and a flame that dies.
RODERIC QUINN.
The Days were Golden!
The clouds had each a rainbow rim,
The gracious clouds and golden ;
The stars we knew were never dim,
The songs were quaint and olden ;
With glamor-glow before our eyes
We left the land of worldly-wise,
We watched the suns of glory rise
(Ah, then the Days were golden !). •
The lips we loved were ruby-red
(Ah, sweet the murmurs olden !)
The hair was haloed round the head
(The gracious locks and golden !)
The eyes were always tender-true,
The tale they told was always new,
The wreaths of rose had naught of rue
(Ah, then the Days were golden !).
We loved the glance of flowers a-gleam,
The green that kissed the golden !
Our waking life was drowsy dream
In that dear land and olden ;
Our speech ran o’er with words of wine,
Our shade was shot with silver shine,
Our Human sank in Deeps Divine
(Ah, then the Days were golden !).
We heard the vault in rapture ring
Through many a cycle golden ;
We heard the Sons of Morning sing
In mystic chorus olden ;
We heard the voice of vale and crest,
The bird that sang beside the nest,
The bird that sang within the breast—■
(Ah —then the Days were golden !)
When heart to heart was gently leal,
When Love was fresh and golden,
When Laughter rang in silver peal,
(The peerless Laughter olden !)
When bosoms proud were in their prime,
When elust’ring Joys were in their clime,
When Life was all a rippling rhyme—
Ah —then the Days were golden !
Q.
P. LUFTIG.

IN AMERICA.
The Legend,
IT was in the summer of 1778, Wash-
ington, with his army ever active,,
determined to oppose the British
general, Sir Henry Clinton, who, havin
evacuated Philadelphia, was retreatin
through New Jersey on New York. The
forces met on 28th June at Monmouth.
La Fayette conducted the attack. The
heat was so intense that some refused to
fight, and many fell dead from exposure
to the sun’s rays. Both sides fought
desperately. As the day waned, and the
flagging spirits of La Fayette’s soldiers
were at a low ebb, a strange sight aroused
them. Marching with drum and fife
was an old man, Jeremiah Wynton, and
his son, and grandson. The old father,
with silvery flowing locks, sturdily strode
through the field. The son with ban-
daged head fifing lustily, the youth,
catching the spirit of his grandsire, rolled
out with him the soul-inspiring rattle of
the drum. Father, son, and grandson
had accompanied the troops as volunteers,
and were ever in the forefront of the
fight. The fierce light of battle was;in
their eyes, they knew no fear, and many
a wild cheer arose, as on that fateful
day Yankee Hoodie was heard as never
was heard before or since.

SATURDAY, MAY 20, 1899.
Conventional Lies of the Anti-Federal
Party.
The Bulletin has counted up 17 lies on
which the New South Wales Anti-Federal
party principally travels. That party has to
lie for a very sufficient reason. It is made
up mainly of four sections. One is the old
Cabbage-Tree mob or Jackwant party, which
objects to federating with anybody because
it believes that New South Wales is the
centre of the universe, and that the sun
rises in Sydney and sets in Dibbs’s coat-tail
pocket, and that everybody born in Botany
Bay is so much better than anybody else in
all this world, that the idea of him even
condescending to speak to his neighbors is
absurd. Another is the Sydney importing
crowd ; its mission is to discourage local in-
dustry by Freetrade, and it would rather see
the Australian nation sunk in the deep, deep
sea than run the risk of bruising the shoddy
import industry. Also, there are the Sydney
landlords, who want the capital in Sydney so
that the increase of population will send up
their private land-values, and enable them to
grab the unearned increment for their private
pockets, and to raise their rents. And there
is Sydney Daily Telegraph, whose 50 per
cent. Protection must go if Federation ar-
rives. (At present the Telegraph is carried
free all over New South Wales, and so costs
Id. only, while its Melbourne and Adelaide
and Brisbane rivals, which would otherwise
cut heavily into its business in the border
districts, pay ijd. postage, and consequently
cost l|d. And whether the New South
Wales free postage is abolished under Fede-
ration or is extended all over Australia, the
Telegraph will have to compete with its
Melbourne and Adelaide and Brisbane rivals
on even terms—they will all pay, or else all
go free—and the Freetrade organ will lose
its 50 per cent. Protection). As people with
such motives as these would gain very little
except ridicule by avowing them in all their
naked meanness, they all proclaim themselves
federalists who yearn after union with a
great yearning, and only object to the pre-
sent Bill because of its many awful defects.
And, as these defects are not there, they
have to invent them. And their 16 princi-
pal inventions are these :
* * * *
That the other provinces are begging
New South Wales to federate with them
Bom the meanest and most sordid motives.
nrov e inpp« l ! th f V^ ale !’ in Parkbs ’ s time, invited the other
out derate ’ and then dl '°PPed the subject with-

That under Federation New South Wales’s
penny town postage “ will have to go.”
There are four provinces (New South Wales Queens-
and, Tasmania, and Westralia) with penny town postage
and only two (Victoria and South Australia) witha mini!
mum postage of 2d. The assertion that the two provinces
must necessarily over-rule the four is not only a lie but a
sign of weak intellect as well. y 1 a
That New South Wales’s free newspaper
postage must go under Federation.
(New South Wales > and
hatenT Hf f T news P a P el ; Postage. The other three
naven t. bo the chances are about equal.
* * * *
That the Federal Bill was stealthily and
surreptitiously drafted by a “Secret Con-
ference.”
F £ d ? ral 0 11 r is the only Bin ever submitted to New
South Wales Parliament which was not a Secret Con-
ference Bill. Other Bills are drafted at a Secret Con-
ference of the Cabinet, and not even the most shameless
liar among the ant.i-Federal organs ever found that there
was anything wrong with the principle. This one was
prepared by a public conference, and only had one or two
small alterations made upon it afterwards at a private
meeting of Premiers. F
That the other provinces have laid a gra-
tuitous insult on Sydney by requiring that
the capital shall not be within 100 miles of
that city.
rr, B wu Houses of New South Wales Parliament demanded
that the capital should be in New South Wales—in other
words, that it should be not less than 75 miles from
Brisbane, 160 from Melbourne and Adelaide, 400 from
Hobart, or 1500 from Perth. If it is an “insult” to
stipulate that the Federal city must not be within a cer-
tain distance of an existing State capital, then the people
and Parliament of New South Wales are about the most
insulting crowd on this earth.
That the fact of the Federal capital not being
in Sydney will be the “ death-warrant ” of
that city (vide J. Want’s cablegram).
As all the provinces will be under the same tariff and
the same conditions (except that New South Wales will
have the cap it a l), this implies one of two things : Either
death-warrants of Brisbane, Sydney, Melbourne,
Hobart, Adelaide, and Perth are all signed at once, and
they are all going to be destroyed, only the destruction
o r' bve doesn’t matter-also, the New South
Wales Parliament deliberately plotted to destroy the
° th ° r capitals by insisting that none of them should
he the Federal city ; or else the Sydney people are such a
poor, mean, ignorant, cowardly, and unenterprising
crowd that they can t survive under conditions which
the vigorous and courageous people of the other cities
can face without alarm. Also, it implies that Sydney’s
death-warrant has been signed all through the last 100
years, when it wasn’t the Commonwealth capital, because
there was no Commonwealth.
* * * *
That New South VV ales is the only province
whose revenue, is improving, all the others
being in a decline, and therefore New South
Wales will have to support these “ neces-
sitous ” neighbors.
The financial situation of the six provinces for the last
three years has been as follows (Coghlan’s “Statistics of
the Seven Colonies of Australasia ”) :

The figures demonstrate that New South Wales, in the
past three years, has shown less increase of revenue than
Queensland, Westralia or Victoria, and far less in pro-
portion to population than any other province. There-
fore, when the anti-Federal party states that New South
Wales will have to help the other provinces because it- is
the only one that has an improving revenue, and that the
other provinces want to federate with New South Wales
because in their declining state their only hope lies in
getting assistance from it, and talks of them as bein"
any more “insolvent” and “necessitous” than New
South Wales, it is lying—as usual.
* * * *
That the Braddon Clause commits the
Commonwealth to the imposition of a heavy
tariffalso, that it will make it necessary
for the Commonwealth to raise four times as
much revenue as it requires.
The Braddon clause provides that, so long as the debts
are left in the hands of the States, three-fourths of each
State’s customs and excise revenue (if there is any) will
be reserved to pay interest with (the customs and excise
being the source which they all depend on to pay in-
terest). When the State debts are taken over by the
Commonwealth the three-fourths will be used by the
Commonwealth to pay the interest with. It is purely a
provision for securing the public creditor either way. The
Commonwealth may decide to pay its own expenses out of
direct taxation, if it likes, and have no customs and excise •
or to depend partly on direct taxation and have a low re-’
venue-tariff like New South Wales’s ; or to have a high
tariff—the Braddon clause only provides that if
a tariff, large, small, or middle-sized, Foreign-trade, Pro-
tectionist, or otherwise, three-fourths of the proceeds
must be ear-marked as a very inadequate provision for
interest on the public debt. At present all the customs
and excise revenue of New South Wales and South
Australia doesn’t nearly meet the interest bill, and prac-
tically all that of Queensland and Tasmania goes in in-
terest, so, vvhether the clause is left in or not, the country
must set aside more than three-fourths of the proceeds of
its tariff for interest—or else repudiate its debt and go
broke. The party that is against the Braddon clause
is the Debt Repudiation Party, and its complaint is
that the Federal Government will have to raise four
times more customs revenue than it would require- that
is to say, four times more than it would require if it let
the country's interest bill remain unpaid.
That New South Wales is the only province
with any reserve-power of taxation, and con-
sequently must bear the greatest part of any
new financial burdens.
Westralia has no land-tax, income-tax, probate-duty,
excise, inland newspaper postage, or bank-note tax.
Queensland has no land or income tax. Victoria has no
land-tax except on estates over 640 acres in area and
£2600 in value, and it charges only half Now South Wales’
receipt-stamp duty. Tasmania has a land-tax of only id.
in the P, which can be doubled without exceeding the
New South Wales figure; and it has had, for the last
three years, the biggest surplus, in proportion to popula-
tion, in Australia, therefore it has the largest margin of
spare revenue. The legend about none of the pro-
vinces except New South Wales having any margin of
taxation to fall back upon, so that their share of any
Federal burdens must be borne by New South Wales, is
the usual Ananias.
* * * *
That “ the implements and tools, as well as
the imported material, of almost every trade
would, in this colony (N.S.W.), be taxed to an
enormous degree in excess of the charges
made upon them in the others.”— Daily
Telegraph lie , sth May, ’99.
The short answer to which childish bogey is that the
Federal Constitution declares, as distinctly as words can
put it, that all import and excise taxation shall be exactly
the same all over the Commonwealth.
That New South Wales, under a uniform
Federal tariff, will pay much more than its
neighbors.
The mode of reasoning, or of unreason, employed is this:
New South Wales, with a low tariff, imports heavily ;
Victoria, Tasmania and South Australia, with a high
tariff, import comparatively little. And if the New
South Wales tariff was raised to the Victorian
level, and if New South Wales still went on im-
porting as much under the high tariff as it did under the
low one, then it would pay more customs taxation per
head than its neighbors. But if the New South Wales
tariff was raised to the Victorian level, New South Wales
would import less, or if the Victorian tariff was reduced
to the New South Wales level, Victoria would import
more. The Dibbs-M‘Laurin Government proved the for-
mer proposition when it made its experiment with a
high tariff in New South Wales a few years ago, the cus-
toms taxation per head in three provinces being as
follows:
1890 1892 1894 1895
Low Tariff High Tariff High Tariff High Tariff
N.S.W. £1 13 7 £2 3 7 £1 12 6 £1 11 3
High Tariff High Tariff High Tariff High Tariff
Vic £2 6 3 £1 16 10 £1 10 3 £1 10 2
S.A. 232 1 16 11 1 10 1 191
When Australia is all under the same tariff New South
Wales, being accustomed to rely much on imports and
very little on local production, must inevitably pay more
customs taxation than some of the other provinces for the
first year or so. But when the province gets accustomed
to its new conditions it will pay no more than its next-
door neighbor. One more year of the Dibbs-M‘Laurin
tariff would almost certainly, considering the rate of
decrease, have reduced N.S. W.’s taxation below the Vic.
level.
* * * «
That the Bill ‘ ‘ gives the right to per-
petuate the Victorian bounties.”
The Bill provides that all bounties granted by New
South Wales, Victoria, or any other State previous to
30th June, 1898, shall hold good until the amounts al-
ready voted are used up. It will, probably, take at least
two or three years to get the Federal Government started,
and by that time almost every one of the Victorian
bounties will have expired, they being very few and for a
limited amount. New South Wales’s coal bounty, being
an indirect aid given by exempting the coal industry from
certain local taxes, will, apparently, go right on—not
that it matters much. All future bounties are in the
hands of the Federal Parliament, except that every State
has an equal right to grant aids to promote mining for
metals. On which point New South Wales, having a
much more varied supply of metals than its southern
neighbors, s'eemstocome off quite as well as Victoria, and
vastly better than S. A. But however that may be, one
province’s bounties will be just as much “ perpetuated ”
as another’s.
That Federation will cost New South Wales
225. 6d. per inhabitant per annum in extra
customs and excise taxation,
New South Wales last year, before the increase of cus-
toms duties, paid £1 3s. Id. per head in customs and
excise; South Australia paid £ll3s. sd. ; Victoria, £llss.
lid. If the rates are levelled up to the Victorian figure,
which is a mere matter of conjecture, the increase at
most will be 12s. lOd. The other 9s. Bd. is the usual lie.
And New South Wales will get the money back to build
its public works with, and to square its accounts, and to
pay off its deficit, and start an old-age pension system.
That the Federal capital will be in Mel-
bourne :
The Bill distinctly enacts that it will be in New South
Wales at all events, and it can’t do more than that.
* * * *
That “ Our Rivers ” are going to be stolen
or embezzled or grabbed by other provinces
which will come along in the night, and each
take a river under its arm, and sneak away.
“Our Rivers” are mainly' the Darling, whose waters
are largely supplied from Queensland, and the Murray,
which derives half its waters from Victoria. The only
mention of them in the Federal Constitution is this
(clause 99): “The Commonwealth shall not, by any law
or regulation . . . abridge the right of a State or of the
residents therein to the reasonable use of the waters of
rivers for conservation or irrigation.” Even the most
agile liars on the river question never quote that clause,
but simply state off-hand that the rivers are going to be
run away with.
That “our trade” will be annexed by the
adjacent provinces.
The Federal Constitution provides for Freetrade inside
Australia. It abolishes the border duties by which
Queensland, South Australia, Victoria and Tasmania shut
out New South Wales products; abolishes the heavy
Queensland and Tasmanian duties on New' South Wales
coal, and the export W'ool duty by which Queensland pre-
vents its wool going to New South Wales ports. It also
abolishes the differential railway rates by which so much
of New' South Wales’s wool trade is attracted to Melbourne
when it wmuld otherwise come Sydneywards. It puts
all the provinces on the same level, and if New South
Wales trade is such a poor, weak, diseased thing that it
can’t stand free and fair competition with its neighbors
then the wretched object deserves to be lost.
That the equal State represention in the
Senate is ruinous, unprecedented and gener-
ally awful.
There are 16 Federations now existing, and 14 of them
(including Switzerland, the oldest and most successful of
the crowd) have equal State representation in the Upper
House, regardless of population. And the great inevit-
able smash that is to come never happens.
That “ the Bill gives the Senate the right to
refuse supply until it gains its own ends,
while the Federal Government can only
control the House of Representatives.”—
Daily Telegraph lie, 9th May , 1899.
If the Senate refuses supply to “gain its own ends,”
there will be a deadlock. The remedy provided for a
deadlock is a joint sitting of the tw'o Houses, where the
majority decides. The House of Representatives, being
twice as large as the Senate, can control it every time.
And the Federal Government will control the House of
Representatives. So the above is the same old lie once
more.
That it will “prevent reform of any kind
desired in N.S.W. and Victoria by putting
a veto power in the hands of the three
smaller States.”
Instead of the three smaller States (South Australia,
Tasmania and West Australia) being able to veto New'
South VVales and Victoria the four small States,(South
Australia, Tasmania, West Australia and Queensland)
can’t do it. Whore any deadlock arises between the
Representatives, where New South Wales and Victoria
hold the majority, and the Senate, where the four small
States have the majority, the final decision rests with the
two Houses sitting- and voting as one. In that joint
House the voting- power will be :
Lower House Senatorial

New South Wales... 25 . ... 6 ... 31

Queensland 9 ... 6 ... 15

Tasmania 5 ... 6 ... 11
West Australia ... 5 ... 6 ... n
Majority of nine for New South Wales and Victoria over
the four small States. Fine robust lie.
A large number of other lies are held over
through want of space. In particular, the
great Twenty-two-shillings-and-,sixpence lie
requires an article to itself.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Shocking’ the Bishop.
Bishop Goe and the purist section of Melb.
University Council are in favor of a new statute
(which, however, the Senate is not anxious to
adopt) giving an absolute majority of the Coun-
cil power to wipe out any professor who may
from this time forward chance to be guilty, in
their opinion, of publishing questionable verses,
or uttering ungodly remarks, in any public or
private capacity whatsoever. The suggested
wording of the statute says, “ Guilty of any
conduct, whether or not in the course of his
duties, calculated to injure the University,”
and this, of course, includes the lamentable
possibility of a professor getting very much and
very drunk, or being detected in a criminal
act; but the main purpose of Bishop Goe and
his disciples is to obtain a means of dismissing
any future Marshall-Hall. The learned and
gifted Prof, in question had openly expressed a
disrespect for parsons and parsonic rules of
propriety : he has likened the pale moon, peep-
ing from behind a cloud, to a giddy maiden
with indiscreet intentions, and, in a general
way of speaking, he has shocked Bishop Goe.
And to shock a Bishop, whilst one is connected
with a University, is to imperil the morals
of the rising generation, in a churchy
way of speaking. The Council being
expected to safeguard the morals of Uni-
versity students, it follows that any Pro-
essor who might appear a dangerous person in
he Bishop’s eyes would probably have to go
forth from his billet by virtue of the new
statute. The Council is bound to swear, more
or less, by the morality of the spiritual poten-
tate in garters, so the statute would be an
awful stumbling-block in the path of worldly
Profs. If the Bishop were to catch one of
them saying “ damn ! ” or doing aught in the
nature of a bad example to the young (even
though there are no young present, save a con-
traband Tottie), the offence might prove fatal
to the sinner. Therefore, the less strictly
Grundyish section of the Council, headed by
Chancellor Judge Madden, is unfavorable to
the arbitrary statute aforesaid, and the Pro-
fessors are not likely to be exposed to the
risk of sudden dismissal on a charge of “insult-
ing behavior” towards the Church party. A
Professorial outrage against decency would find
the whole Council unanimous in making the
offender’s chair too hot to hold him; but an
outrage is one thing, and the shocking of a
narrow-minded Goe is another.

The Newcastle Coal Bogey.
More financial items from that wonderful
fount of inaccuracy, the Secret Conference Tele-
graph
The Victorian Government gives a bonus of a farthing'
a ton per mile on all Victorian coal carried on its rail-
ways. The Railway Commissioners make a further re-
duction of a farthing per ton per mile for such coal.
These concessions, which can be perpetuated under
the Secret Conference Bill, are part of what Newcastle
is asked to contend against.
And they are not all.
This question has been addressed to the Anti-Conven-
tion Bill League:—“The wharfage rates at Melbourne
are 4s. 6d. per ton, at Sydney Is. 6d. per ton, at New-
castle nil. Will Melbourne have to be levelled down or
Sydney up, and how will any levelling process affect
N.S.W. interests ? ”
What guarantee is there that N.S. W. rates will not be
levelled up and Newcastle thus put at great disad-
vantage ?
Again, the possibilities of exportation will be reduced
as a consequence of the limitation, by a high tariff, of
imports.
If the miners of Newcastle want to look after their own
interests they will VOTE TO A MAN AGAINST THE
BILL. It will have A DECIDEDLY DETRIMENTAL
EFFECT ON OUR COAL TRADE, for the simple reason
that the 27 per cent, advalorem duty essential to provide
for the necessities of the other colonies would mean that
very much less goods will be imported from England and
the Continent to Australia, and FEWER SHIPS would
consequently COME HERE for our coal. The OTHER
COLONIES HAVE ALL TO GAIN AND NOTHING TO
LOSE. They can still pour their grain and cabbages into
New South Wales, but will not require any more coal to
supply their wants.
Wherein the Secret - Conference Telegraph
blights its alleged immortal soul with another
untruth, for the Vic. Railway Commissioners
don’t make -a further reduction of a Jd. per ton
per mile. It is only one farthing all the time.
The case is a very simple one, and can be con-
densed into these few propositions
(1) In N.S.W. and Victoria local coal is carried by rail at
the same charge—Jd. per ton per mile. Some people say
that the rate doesn’t pay and that the charge should be
Jd., others say it is all right. Anyhow, the loss, if any,
is borne in Victoria by the general revenue, which pays to
the railway revenue the amount of the presumed deficit.
In N.S.W. the loss, if any, is borne by the railway revenue.
But. local coal is carried at the same rate in each province.
(2) But N.S. W. coal carried by rail in Victoria pays fd.
per ton per mile. This heavy tax will probably continue
or may even increase if the provinces don’t federate. If
they do, the clause prohibiting differential railway rates
will wipe it out and give N.S.W. coal the same show as
the Victorian artiele.
(3) The N.S.W. coal-owner receives a special bonus
(Is. (id. per ton according to the Telegraph) in the shape
of exemption from wharfage dues.
(4) The Telegraph pleads (a) that the N.S.W. coal-
miner shouldn’t federate because, as it alleges, the rail-
way charges are lower in Victoria, and ( b) that he
shouldn’t federate because the wharfage charges are
lower in N.S.W. In other words the bonus which the
Vic. Government gives is a reason against Federation
because bonuses are bad; and the bonus which N.S.W.
Government gives is a reason against it also, because
bonuses are too good to be lost.
(5) The Federal Government isn’t likely to interfere
with the N.S.W. wharfage-duties in any case. Wharfage-
rates aren’t even mentioned among the things over which
the Federal Government has cognisance.
(6) And, apropos of the grain and cabbage argument, if
N.S.W. can’t compete, under interprovincial freetrade,
with the other provinces in the matter of grain and cab-
bages, what’s the matter with the miserable place ? The
Bulletin always regarded N.S.W. as a province that
could grow grain and cabbages against anybody.
In any case, the N.S.W. coal-miner is the one


man in the community who wants Federation
at any price. Queensland and Tasmania have
already practically closed their doors to him by
imposing duties of 2s. and 3s. a ton, and only
Federation can compel them to take these
duties off. Now he almost depends for his
existence on the export - trade to V ic-
toria, S.A., and Westralia (Victoria alone
takes a quarter of his exports), and if Vic. and
Westralia put on a heavy duty in order to
develop their own coal-fields, of which they
have abundance, the N.S.W. miner had better
light out for the Benevolent Asylum. And the
only thing which can make it impossible for
them to put on that duty, and which can
make Victoria abolish its extra railway
charge on N.S.W. coal, is Federation and
its inevitable accompaniment, interprovincial
Freetrade. The N.S.W. coal-miner who votes
against Federation is voting to cut his own
throat. And, if he can’t see which way his in-
terests lie, his throat deserves the worst that
can happen to it.
The Auckland Lie.
The “ Auckland lie ” of N.S.W. anti-Federal
party grows monotonous. As a sample, one
“E. Guille” (why two “l’s”?) shrieks in
Sydney Telegraph :
The loss ot the capital is nothing, say these secret con-
spirators. Voters, you know better! Auckland was once
the capital of New Zealand ; influence and chicanery
removed it to Wellington. Auckland was taxed to build
Wellington’s harbor. Trade followed the capital. WHERE
(this in a deafening roar) IS AUCKLAND NOW ?
And every statement in this yarn, save one—
that the capital was removed —is absolutely
false :
(1) Auckland, like Sydney, is at the extreme edge of
the province. Maoriland, when its union was effected,
decided to have a more central capital. (2) The
selection of the capital was referred to a committee
selected from outside the province, so as to shut out all
chance of local influence and “chicanery.” It selected
Wellington as the most central city. Wellington had
mighty little “ influence ” in those days. (3) Auckland
wasn’t taxed to build Wellington’s harbor. Wellington has
a splendid natural harbor which didn’t require building.
The harbor works, wharves, etc., were charged to the
local harborboard, and Wellington pays theinterest on them
itself. (4) And as to where Auckland —ruinedAuckland —is
now : It is the most populous city in Maoriland (Auckland
and suburbs, 57,616 people ; Christchurch and suburbs,
51,330 ; Dunedin and suburbs, 47,280 ; Wellington—the
great bloated metropolis—and suburbs, only 41,758).
Also, Auckland is the greatest shipping port in Maori-
land. It owns three times as much shipping of its own
as Wellington. And, as a manufacturing province, Auck-
land is ahead of all others, save Canterbury, and far in
front of Wellington province. And the G. PiLE’s-dog party
assures us that what happened to Auckland will likewise
happen to Sydney. (5) Also, the whole parallel is a prevari-
cation. The Maoriland capital was removed from Auck-
land. The Australian capital won’t be removed from
Sydney, for it isn’t there. So Sydney should come off
better than Auckland that down-trodden ex-capital,
which has fallen up to be by far the biggest place in
Maoriland.

Some Agricultural Troubles.
“C.C.” about N.S.W. selectors’ wrongs :
Now that the squatters are beginning to wait on the
N.S.W. Lands Dept, door-mat for still another renewal of
their leases, let me point out to the Minister that by
granting these renewals for five years he plays into the
monopolist’s hands, and that he would be doing his
friends, the selectors, a favor by making the renewals ex-
tend a few days over five years. Reason why ? A clause
in the Land Act debars selector from claiming half cost
of boundary-fence from a lessee unless the lease has five
years to run. Thus lessee nearly always escapes by a
little dodgery. Have only known one selector who was
cute enough to score. Just before last renewal of the
Central leases he hurried up, got his boundary-fence on
the leasehold line finished, and lodged two claims for con-
tribution— one dated the day before, and one on the day
of renewal. Even then the lessee pleaded that as an
hour or two of his lease had expired before the claim was
made he was not liable 1 These fencing-regulations,
in fact, have been artfully framed by squatters for
squatters. A selector takes up land on a resumed area
and fences it. Yet the adjoining squatter pays nothing
towards the boundary-fence erected by the “ little man,”
whereas if there is already a fence there belonging to the
lessee the selector has to pay for the whole of it. The
squatter’s plea, that as his tenure is uncertain it wouldn’t
be fair to make him pay his share of the selector’s fence,
is mere bluff. With a Minister who will grant renewal
for the asking, the lessee has practical fixity of tenure,
and even if the lease is thrown open for selection the
lessee gets the value of his share of the fencing returned
him. Most selectors the writer knows repudiate the
eternal Parliamentary whine about the “ poor struggling
selectors.” They don’t claim more sympathy than any
other class of straggler, and only want their honest
grievances ventilated. They say: “Administer the Act
fairly, and don’t talk of charity !”
Another N.S.W. selector’s wail :
Dear B. —N.S.W. Lands Department is a persistent liar.
It has been frequently announced in the Sydney papers
that land-agents in drought-stricken districts have been
instructed not to insist upon fines for over-due payments,
yet the local land-agent says—“ All rot! I have no
orders from head-quarters to remit the fines, and people
will have to pay them if they are only a day over-due
with the cash.” The “struggling selector” is getting
very tired of being advertised as the recipient of con-
cessions he never gets. The squatter who owns scores of
selections is doubtless the poor “ straggler ” whose fines
are to be remitted. Again, there is a selector in this
district who was fined 10 per cent, for not paying his rent,
when it was due, and although he explained to the de-
partment that he didn’t pay because the land-agent-
wouldn’t talce the rent—pending an appeal for reduction
—his fine was not remitted. The present Act may be the
best up to date, but the administration of the depart-
ment is hopelessly rotten, and will remain so as long as
the head of it continues to be a man who has no practical
knowledge of the subject.
“ Moldoon ” upon a sore subject :
N.S.W. Stock Department plays a good second to its
brother ass, the Lands. Present assessment under
Diseases in Sheep Act is Bs. per 1000. Owners up to 500
exempt, but oyer that number and under 1000, full rate.
Thus a man with 500 sheep pays nothing, but the owner
of 1001 pays Bs. for the 1000 and Bs. for the odd sheep !
Of course he only pays theoretically. As a matter of
fact, the owner who has an odd hundred or two easily
convinces himself that they will die before his assessment
is due, and soothes his conscience wdth the reflection that
all the thousands of pounds collected under the Act are
expended upon alleged inspectors who only heard of
sheep dying of anthrax on the public roads when the
newspapers reported that the drovers who skinned them
had also died. Stated at the same time in press that the
Department had collected £28,000, the whole of which
went in salaries. If the assessment-returns were correct
they would have a few thousands more to play with, but
at present the sheep statistics are even less reliable than
the figures of the Sunday Shellback when it’s enlarging
Braddon’s Blot with an inky finger. There is a peculiar
oversight in the working of the D. in S. Act which allows
of thousands of sheep escaping the assessment; but, as I
share the general opinion of stock-owners that the Act is
as obsolete as N.S.W’. Marine Board, and the money
simply wasted, I don’t intend publicly to put my finger
on the weak spot, though I am willing- privately to con-
vince The Bulletin. Anybody acquainted with the
idiotic working of our Stock Acts and Land Acts must
regret that Australians don’t follow the English fashion of
putting the “ fool of the family ” into the Church instead
of getting him a Government billet.

Some Sabbath. Paper Tips.
The Sunday Shellback again, quoting from
Dr. M'Laurin :
Supposing', for example, that the Federal Treasurer
were to keep the customs and excise revenue, all the
States would be left with serious deficiencies, which
would prevent them from meeting their engagements, and,
in fact, would prevent them from paying the interest on
their debts.
Now, this persistent and brassy person knows
perfectly well that the much-abused Braddon
clause provides for 15s. out of every 20s.
raised by customs being either returned to
the States so that they may pay the interest
on their debts, or applied by the Common-
wealth to pay the interest on these same debts.
And M’Laurin is never tired of demanding the
abolition of this arrangement. And the same
M’Laurin admits that if this arrangement is
abolished the States must default. So the Debt
Repudiation party again comes to the front,
and M’Laurin is its prophet:
During the Secret Conference of the Premiers in Mel-
bourne, the other day, they passed a new clause, which
entitled the Federal Government to give assistance to
the weaker States. That is a very fine clause.
Dr. MacLaitrin advocates Unification, and
under Unification such a clause would
be unnecessary, because by virtue of the
Unification N.S.W. and all the other pro-
vinces would go bail for the solvency of
every province and county in the continent,
just as Sydney at present time is security
for every shilling of the interest of the
money spent on the Bogan bridge at Monkey.
Under Federation it is quite proper that such
a clause should be in the Constitution, as the
Commonwealth can borrow money for the relief
of any “necessitous State” at a much lower
rate of interest than the n.s. itself ; and, of
course, the “ necessitous ” one would have to
pay the interest thereon. Seeing that Dr. Mac-
Laurin himself declares that the N.S.W.
“ surplus ” can’t be annexed to help the “ neces-
sitous State,” what has N.S.W. to complain of ?
N.S.W. Provincialist party has one long howl
that will not be quieted: “The necessitous
States will rob rich N.S.W.” To Dr. Mac-
Laurin’s credit be it said, he, at least, knocked
that idea to pieces, and declared emphatically
that under the Constitution Bill it could not be
done. At the Protestant Hall the black-labor
director said:
But where is the Federal Treasurer to get the money
(with which to assist the necessitous States)? Is he to
take it out of the N.S. Wales surplus? . . . The
fact is that the Federal Treasurer cannot take it out
of the surplus, because that is the property of this
country, and he could be stopped by the Supreme
Court. He cannot get it out of N.S.W., except by an act
of robbery, and I do not wish to accuse the other colonies
of robbery. I never did, and I will not do so now. All I
say is, he cannot get it out of the N.S.W. surplus.
The parochial party is never weary of holding
up MacLaurin as the most reliable Federal
authority in Australia. And the reliable
MacLaurin thus knocks the parochial party’s
best argument endways.
More Sunday Shellback :
SOOLING ON THE POLICE.
MUZZLING COGHLAN.
DEPRIVING CIVIL-SERVANTS OF CITIZEN RIGHTS.
These are some of the means adopted by the anxious
pro-Bill leaders to try and force the Bill on the people.
According to pro-Billites, the man who wants to
thoroughly examine the terms of the contract he is de-
sired to become a party to is nothing less than a scoun-
drel.
It is indeed astonishing how examination of the facts is
resented. The people are supposed to go like dumb,
driven cattle to the slaughter—all because" of the office-
hunger of certain individuals.
This is simply lovely. At Sydney Town Hall
some thousands of people were gathered
together to hear Reid and Barton explain
“ the terms of contract,” and a few dozen
howling ruffians, apparently paid by the Pro-
vincialist party, resented it so loudly, and
cursed and broke chairs so furiously, that most
of the thousands heard not one word of the
“terms of contract,” and their attempt to
examine the Bill was utterly wrecked. This
certainly looked as if it was the Provincialist
party that resented examination. The attempt
to remove these roaring rowdies is what the
Sunday rag calls “ sooling on the police,” the
Sabbath Shellback being the standing apolo-
gist for disorder and the sworn enemy of free-
dom of speech (the other fellow’s freedom of
speech). As to what “ depriving civil servants
of citizen rights ” means Heaven only knows.
They have the franchise. They are not allowed
to address public meetings either for or against
the Bill, but it is years since they were deprived
of that right, and the Sabbath tipster never
seriously objected when they lost it. But this
world is full of woe and confusion, and of
Sunday papers that urge the public to buy
shares in the excellent Shellback mine just
before it goes utterly broke, and there are
many things in it that are hard to understand.
Three Provincial Items.
Some slabs from Dr. M'Laurin, M.L.C.
The railways are a paying asset in this oolony (N.S.W.)
You will riot have the privilege (under Federation) of
sending your letters for a penny, as you do now.
The Bill provides for a system of bounties by which the
producer in Victoria will be put in a much more favorable
position than the producer in N.S.W.
The shortest reply to which is that not one of
these statements has a shadow of truth about
it. The Bill gives every province exactly the
same privileges in the matter of bounties. As
there are four provinces which have penny
town-postage, against two that haven’t, the
chances are 100 to 1 that the postage argument
will turn out a shameless lie. And the N.S.W.
railways, according to the latest published re-
port of the Auditor-General, are not a paying
asset, and never have been. They are nearer to
it than most of the others, but there is no
paying railway-system in the Seven Provinces,
barring Westralia’s, which is now fast getting
back to its old, non-profitable state. Will the
just and fair-minded and honorable gentlemen
of anti-Bill please try to tell the exact facts—
well, say, semi-occasional ly ?
+.
“ The Bulletin’s ” Prize Competition.
This paper is not generally addicted to prize-
competitions, but it is moved to start one by
the following statements cut from the Secret Con-
ference Telegraph’s regular anti-Federal lead-
ing article of 9th May :
One of the most misleading contentions used by its
advocates in seeking to secure for the Secret Conference
Bill the support of the wage-earners of N.S. Wales is that
a great deal of employment would be assured to them by
the building of the Federal capital. ... If the proposed
Bill finds acceptance, it is a very open question whether
the plans for a Federal capital in N.S. Wales will
ever be drawn. But assuming the improbable con-
tingency that the removal of the seat of Aus-
tralian Government from Melbourne during the present
generation is decided upon, what prospect is there that the
workers of N.S.W. will he thereby afforded, an especial
opportunity of obtaining employment ? Even if the site
decided upon were in the remotest part of our north-
western territory, there would be no bar to the workers of
other colonies reaching it and becoming applicants for em-
ployment equally with those of Sydney, save the cost of
an inexpensive sea passage. To come from Melbourne
to Sydney is a small matter to those who think that by
doing so employment could be secured. It was bitterly
complained of in Melbourne, when large contracts like
that of the erection of the Exhibition Building were in
progress, that a considerable number of N.S.W. workmen
were employed.
There is a slight difference here—not that that
matters much. Victoria paid the whole cost of
its Exhibition, and, despite its Protectionist
ideas, N.S.W. workmen—a considerable num-
ber of them—were allowed to find employment
and wages at the erection of that stupend-
ous soap-and-candle show. The Victorian
workmen grumbled certainly, but still the
Cornstalk crowd had the employment. N.S.W.
claims to practise the large, wide virtue of
Freetrade, and not to be narrow-minded like
the Protectionists of Melbourne. It won’t pay
the whole cost of building the Federal capital,
but only a shade over one-third of it, for all the
provinces will contribute. And the Freetrade
Telegraph has the inconceivable dog-mean-
ness to urge, as a reason why N.S.W. should
reject Federation, the absence of any provision
in the Bill to the effect that, while Queensland,
Victoria, Tasmania, S.A. and Westralia contri-
bute nearly two-thirds of the cost of building
the Federal city, no workmen from these pro-
vinces shall get a job at its construction. The
idea of the truthful, honest, fair-minded
gentlemen who run the Telegraph is that
N.S.W". should supply a little over one-third
of the money, and all the wages should be dis-
tributed among Sydney workmen—even N.S. W T .
workmen from places outside Sydney seem to be
barred ; while the other provinces should put up
the bulk of the cash and their people should be
warned off as lepers so far as the employment
and wages are concerned. And yet the I).T.
admits that the Victorian people, against whom
it rails so unceasingly, didn’t treat N.S.W. like
that when they had work to distribute, though
it was work for which Victoria paid the entire
bill. The Telegraph started as an advocate
of unity by demanding that all the other pro-
vinces should be regarded as politically-in-
fected spots and disqualified so far as the pos-
session of the Federal capital is concerned, but
its latest idea breaks even its own particularly
villainous record all to pieces. Wherefore The
Bulletin, as already mentioned, has been
moved to start a prize competition. After con-
sidering the length and breadth and depth and
general hoggishness of the D.T.’s latest
provincial argument, it proposes to offer a
prize to anyone who can find a provincial
argument which will be even meaner than the
one which the D.T. gentlemen have evolved.
It wants to preserve that argument as a curio-
sity. Said prize will cousist of a life-size por-
trait of a worm crawling on an ash-heap, and
will be enclosed in a wooden frame. All mean
men are cordially invited to compete, but no
prize will be awarded unless some one submits
an even lower-down specimen than the one
quoted above. Competition closes on Referen-
dum Day, as after that date the market for
purely parochial meanness will undergo a
severe slump. The worm in the picture will
be the very large, bulgy kind that grows in
damp spots in tropical Australia, and the artist
doesn’t mind enlarging it a little “ on his own ”
if a peculiarly deserving competitor presents
himself.

Another Boiler.
The recent boiler explosion at Sheppar-
ton, Victoria (two deaths, some children
scalded, girl injured, one store and one hotel
wrecked, town considerably battered, some
persons and other ■ animals scared out of their
wits, cash damage about £2000) should make
the Turner Govt, put on a spurt with the
Boilers Inspection Bill promised for next ses-
sion. As for the reason of the catastrophe, it
is the old story over again :
The owner states that the boiler was second-hand when
put in the works at the time they were built, and how old
it is cannot be conjectured. He had anticipated no dan-
ger from it.
The owner never does, somehow. His late
lamented boiler, according to the Age account
quoted above, was so venerable that the exact
extent of its venerableness was beyond the
reach of conjecture, and even an owner knows
that boilers are not immortal. But it is a great
deal cheaper, for a time at least, to have perfect
confidence in the old boiler than to buy a new
one, and the owner goes right on having confi-
dence to the bitter end.
Those who predicted* and those others who
prayed that, with the withdrawal of the anti-
ivingstoman doctors from Adelaide Hospital
ttie poor patients ” would soon crowd out the
new cemetery, have been shockingly disap-
pointed; and successive annual reports have
knocked out the rebellious doctors’ allegedly
humanitarian idea of running a rival institution
on their own. Very opportunely on the eve of
general Parliamentary election, when
Premier Kingston almost for the first time
appeared to be seriously in danger, the hospi-
tal board’s ’9B report was issued. No docu-
ment could more thoroughly prove the wisdom
of a bit of administi auun as bold and deter-
mined as any Australia has known. First of
all, the hospital is shown to be as popular
among patients as ever. In 1888 there were
2003 cases; in ’97, 2740; in ’9B no less than
3246. Next, patients had to remain a shorter
average time—the average for ’9B is the
lowest since ’Bl. The rate of mortality was
7-11 per cent.—against 9'B in ’95, 9'2 in ’96,
and 7'59 in ’97. A particular mess was going
to be made of surgical cases; to go to the
operating-table under the new' regimd was to be
much the same as going to the morgue. Yet,
as a matter of fact, in respect of 1332 patients
operated on, the rate of mortality was ‘9 per
cent., while the mortality ascribed to the
operations themselves was '4 per cent. —one in
250. The silence with which Kingston’s
enemies have read the report has been one of
the most eloquent things of the Parliamentary
election campaign.

Gov. Beauchamp, who comes to the head of
N.S.W. affairs with a little munioipal but no
really political past, is received w'ith open arms
by many of the very men pledged by their votes
in Parliament to resist such a Governor. When
policeman Henry Blake darkened the Queens-
land horizon, N.S.W. took up the question of
what sort of man her own Governor should be,
and Parkes moved an address to the Queen, of
which the following is an extract:—
In view of the great and growing interests of this
colony in connection with the Empire, we desire dutifully
to convey to your Majesty the expression of our opinion
that no person in the future should be appointed to the
office of Governor who has not had experience in the
conduct of public affairs in high political office, or in the
Imperial Parliament.
Dibbs, leader of the Opposition, supported the
motion, Wise supported it, Reid voted for it,
half the men now prominent in Parliament
voted for it. Buchanan and Tommy Walker
created a diversion because they objected to an
imported Governor of any sort, but the motion
was ultimately unanimously agreed to. Yet
here comes Beauchamp into the open arms of
all these people, as if he were the ideal after
which the nation strained.
The complaint about the alleged “ muzzling ”
of N.S.W. Statistician Coghlan with which
the parochial party is now rending the heavens,
is interesting when all the circumstances are
considered :
(1) The anti-Federal party declares that this is the
same obnoxious Bill as the one of last year.
(2) Coghlan reviewed that Bill at great length, and if it
is the same Bill as alleged, and Coghlan’s last year’s
statements were accurate as alleged, then it doesn’t
require further reviewing.
(3) The muzzle yarn is utterly false on the face of it.
The anti-Federal party, which has the gall of many thou-
sand cuttle-fish, asked that a public servant, paid by the
public, should be lent to them to dig up information
about the Bill, of which information they' would pre-
sumably suppress what didn’t suit them, and publish the
rest as an official utterance.
(4) They were told that the public servant in question
was not lendable to any clique of private persons, but
that he was as much at liberty to deal with the subject
now as he was last year—which apparently means that he
can talk or write in his spare time, like anybody else.
And this is how the N.S.W. Statistician was
“ muzzled.”
The survivors of Dr. Lang’s 1849 cargoes of
immigrants who settled down in Fortitude
Valley (Brisbane), gave a picnic, the other day,
to their friends by way of celebrating the
jubilee of their arrival. One of the doctor’s
old warriors numbers 130 descendants. Bris-
bane Courier revives proudly the memory of
the battle that Lang and his sturdj r friends
fought against the convict-transportation sys-
tem. Same Courier howls ferociously against
any modern revolutionist who ventures to
breathe a syllable against the iniquities of the
present kanaka system—a business quite as bad
and as black in its way as ever the old trans-
portation system was. The convicts, at all
events, didn’t spread leprosy among the free
population. Neither did they leave a hybrid
generation behind them to constitute a curse
and a social sore on the body of the coming
nation. But Lang can’t make things uncom-
fortable for Fat Men.any longer. He’s a Dead
Hero, and Dead Heroism can’t hit anybody in
the paunch.
