“ A.8.C.” states in S.M. Herald some reasons
why the Federal Bill should be rejected by
the free and indigestible electors of N.S. W. :
1. Because under Federation farthing jumbles will only
be obtainable at the rate of three for the penny. Let the
State children pause and digest this fact (if they can
digest the jumble, why not the fact?), and before they
barter away their birthright, earned by so many slap-
pings, read an iniquitous Bill that would enslave them
for ever.
2. Because under the proposed scheme of Federation
every woman in this colony will be compelled to pay l£d.
a year extra for her imported fashion journals. Mothers,
think of this !
3. Because in order to die comfortably in N.S.W. every
person about to shuffle off will have to contribute to the
funeral expenses of dead persons in Victoria. Clause
3047 proves that every corpse in this colony will be taxed
for the support of three corpses in Tasmania. Will you
stand it ? Then rise from the Necropolis and reject this
Abortion at the polls.
4. The Bill is so drawn that it will promote pains in
the head, dizziness, chilblains, and a heavy feeling after
meals ; and all for the benefit of the other colonies.
5. Because under the rivers’ clauses it will be im-
possible for the citizens of Sydney to let the taps in their
back yards run all day, as every pint of water in this
continent will be required to make an ornamental lake in
Melbourne for the Federal legislators to row upon. House-
holders with fixed tubs should think over this.
5. Because the Bill makes flirting criminal, and punishes
sweep-promoters with death; it will confiscate your
money in the Savings Bank, and flood the colony witn
dervishes, rinderpest, German clocks, and other foreign
commodities. Eleotors, reject it.

SUNDRY SHOWS.
SYDNEY SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.
Her Majesty's “ The Belle of New York."
Criterion. “The Three Musketeers."
E-oyal. “ The Liars.”
yceum “ A Soldier and a Man."
Tivoli. Rickards Co.
Town Hall Newbury’s Shilling Pops.
Fitzgerald’s Circus—opposite Redfern Stai ion.
MELBOURNE SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.
Princess “Forty Thieves.”
Theatre Royal “ How London Lives.”
Bijou Rickards Variety Co.
St. George's Hall Dante the Great.
Alexandra “The Sixth Commandment."
The costly musical show at Her Majesty’s
has got safely into its second week, and the pro-
bability is that it will get into a good many more
weeks before it wears out its welcome. Some
night, of course, the last pair of legs will be turned
off at the meter ; and the young hero’s last drunk
will be blown out ; and the final girl of them
all will put her brief skirt outside the door to be
polished, and go to bed ; and the bottles that re-
main from Bronson’s revelry will be shifted in the
surreptitious ash-cart; but these things are not
yet. There is no vast boom over the “ Belle,”but
it appears to be making a good, steady living.
Yet it is the kind of play which makes this paper
feel worn and crow’s-feetty and wrinkled, and as
if it was a remnant coming home from an all-night
burst when it was too old for that kind of enjoy-
ment. The “Belle” is built upon the familiar
story of the Young Man and the Old Man and
the Girl. The Young Man is drunk when the
audience makes his acquaintance, yet he looks as
if he was prematurely world-weary and all
the joy had gone out of his drunk.
He and the other gilded youths present
a dissipated shirt-front to the cold grey dawn,
like ten slim dudes bowed down by a great
weight of dollars, and trying to re-light the ashes
of a lost youth that had gone out before its proper
season. The Old Man is a lean hypocrite, with a
mouth like a dry sepulchre full of snuffle, and a
desire to paw the nice girl. The girl who has been
divorced nine times looks handsome in a hard sort
°f fashion, but, after all, the joke about the lady
who, through much marriage and divorce, has
come to be a kind of public building, doesn’t
appeal to the tremendously high order of intellect
which has always been the leading feature of this
paper. And the Salvation girl only looks her best
m her Hallelujah bonnet and her blue costume.
When she gets into the short skirt and other
frivolities of the flesh a lot of the holy charm
leaves her.
* • * *
The present authority likes Oscar Girard,
the Old Man of the play, for his dry and never-
failing humor, and, for his sake, he hopes the
“Belle” will prosper. But when the author in-
vented the public building with the nine consecu-
tive husbands he made a mistake, though the lady
who struggles with the part isn’t responsible for
his blunder. The German lunatic who wants to
kill Bronson grows in favor to some extent on
account of the particularly contorted fit which he
takes when Bronson escapes him, and Charles
Ken'ningham and Miss Belle Bucklin have bright
moments wherever the nature of the show gives
them a chance. Likewise the profusion with
which the Firm has staged this unstable trifle
strikes one like all the N.S. W. public debt falling
on his head when it is least expected. And the
writer decides to suddenly stop here lest he be be-
guiled into saying something that may sound un-
friendly.
** * *
To-nighfc (Thursday) the “ Three Musket-
eers” are due at Sydney Criterion. The new
version is the work of Bernard Espinasse and
George Rignold, and rumor has it that they have
done something great and unusual with the tale of
the four tremendous adventurers who stood the
troubled kingdom of Louis the Just on its ear.
There is so much hard riding in the story, and it
covers so much space, and rages on so many towers
and battlements, and is so full of conspiracy and
entrenchments and sudden death, that all that can
be done is to select a few of the best corpses from
the heap.. It is alleged, however, that the authors
of the Criterion version have selected them on a
new principle, in consequence whereof their
particular heap of dead has all the advantage of
novelty.
** * *
“ Lady Ursula ” was put on for only a
fortnight at Sydney Royal, and “The Liars”
follows on Saturday. Therefore whosoever has
not yet seen Mrs. Brough’s graceful figure blush-
ing in male attire, especially when Joseph Carne
compliments her on her legs, had better seize an
opportunity which will last till Friday night only.
The spectacle in question is well worth the neces-
sary expenditure of anybody’s money. And there
is one moment of immense thrill in the play when
the disguised lady, being harassed by a crowd of
gay bachelors who want her to drink, and smoke,
and go to bed, and do other unseemly things,
rushes into a duel with one of her tormentors ancl
demands satisfaction with pistols across a table.
Things get so far that another half-second will
obviously lead to the destruction of the carpet by
the spreading thereon of a saucerful of grey brains,
and half the audience gets on its feet with tense
expectancy creeping, like a spider with cold feet,
up its spine. That thrill alone is worth 6d. extra.
* * * '*
Benjamin Landeck’s new play, “ A Soldier
and a Man,” will unfurl the gory banner of war
at Sydney Lyceum on Saturday. A new leading
lady, Miss Dora de Winton, from London, and Mr.
Charles Saunders, from the same obscure spot,
will make their first Australian appearance on this
occasion, and help the banner to unfurl for all it
is worth. Until then the admonitory tale of
whisky will continue to point a moral at the
Lyceum, and Mr. O’Neill will batter his dis-
tracted wife because she asks him to come back to
the ruined ’ome where the children have had
nothing to eat for years or thereabouts. After
a second inspection of the above episode the
battering of the thin, faded, little woman’s head
began to assert itself as a tolerably sickening spec-
tacle. The beery husband gets her in chancery,
and pounds her shrieking countenance till, in the
usual order of things, she shouldn’t have a single
feature left. The adventuress who has had vitriol
poured over her is also a gruesome spectacle when
she has recovered and is leading a crushed exist-
ence in a back room with red bars all over a
countenance that will never smile again. In fact,
there is rather too much realism in “ Man’s
Enemy.”

Fitzgerald Brothers celebrated their eighth
week in Sydney with another change of pro-
gramme, which went off with the same happy fizz
and bang as its predecessors. Mahomet, the
“Talking Horse,” who must be getting rather an
aged horse now; Vita Provini, a remarkable
child juggler and balancer; the acrobatic Atlas
Brothers, and Rosina Aquinaldo, an alleged
Cuban lady contortionist, whose bonelessness is
calculated to haunt a man in his dreams, are the
principal new items. To-morrow (Friday) Tom
Tom, Fitzs’ clever clown, takes a benefit, in view
of his early departure for England. Thomas
Thomas is of English origin, and is going back to
stand on his comical head in the land where all
the happy recollections of his childhood are
centred.
At Sydney Opera House the “Two
Scamps ” have gone the way of all scamps and
been succeeded by “ The Alhambra Girl,” a three-
act musical farce, adorned with many morals. In
the course of this frantic upheaval, the respectable
household of Mr. Arthur Glover (Mr. W. J.
Montgomery), is. reduced to something between
sheol and a lunatic asylum by the arrival of the
Alhambra girl, a lurid Flossie in scarlet robes,
with Mr. Glover’s handkerchief. The complica-
tions are awful, and by the end of the second act
the master of the house has five women, including
the Flossie, fainting in his drawing-room all at
once. Miss Marion Lewis, as Sophie, here sings a
song of love and fidelity, and is usually called back
twice by an enthusiastic audience. The third act
is devoted to clearing things up, and local hits. A
topical song by Mr. T. E. Fisher, as Joshua Gilt-
edge, is usually good for several encores.
Mr. Arthur Denham, disguised as one Jobson,
also pleases the people by explaining that
his marred visage was due to the anger of his
beloved, the same lady who sings of love and
fidelity, and who has declared that she would fix
thereon the birth-marks Lord Beauchamp made
the borrowed poetry about. As the Alhambra
girl, Miss Phillips acts well enough, and her song,
“A Little Bit of String, ” is well done; but her
last vocal effort is reminiscent of the five pipers
who simultaneously played six different tunes in
the same wee parlor. If it be a success to amuse
the audience and make it enjoy its night out, the
“Alhambra Girl,” despite its wrecky condition
as a work of literature, is successful enough, in
spite of the fact that Miss Marion Medway, as the
Mother-in-law, is over-painted and over-drawls
her part. Miss K. Scott and Miss Hilda Fraser,
as Mrs. Glover and Lillie Sydney respectively, are
neat and look at home in their characters. It would
be an improvement if Mr. W. J. Montgomery spoke
louder and not quite so fast; but he looks such a
picture of despair that this scribe, who has been
m trouble over women all his life, feels quite a
brotherly love for him.

At Melb. Princess’s to-day (Thursday)
there is to happen a “farewell complimentary
matinee, tendered to Mr. Julius Knight, by
Messrs. Williamson and Musgrove, at the close
of his long and memorable engagement in Aus-
tralia, extending over two years and three months.
The programme selected for the occasion will con-
sist of ‘ The Sign of the Cross, ’ in which Mr.
Knight will be seen for the last time as Marcus,
the character with which his name will be ever most
indelibly associated in this country.” The fore-
going quotation from the advt. may be taken as
the text for a bilious sermon on the great financial
success of the “ Knight-Ferrar co.” (now so-called)
and the origin thereof. Julius Knight, Miss Fer-
rar, Miss Page, Gaston Mervale, Hawtrey and
co., started their Australian campaign in Mel-
bourne with “The Prisoner of Zenda,” a deserving
play which undoubtedly showed all of them at
their best as actors. Knight, who made his first
appearance in a red-brown suit and an unbecom-
ing straw-hat, was neither glorified by Melbourne’s
womenfolk, nor held up to special adoration by
the management. He was accepted as a worthy
leading-man, with a presence somewhat more im-
portant and not visibly older than that of the J.K.
who played second-fiddle to Clifton Alderson at
the Royal in days of yore. The Firm didn’t
star ” Knight for the obvious reason that George
Coppin had not * 4 starred 99 him when he cam© hero
as a member of the Laura Villiers co.
* * * •
“ Prisoner of Zenda ” excited no
enthusiasm in Melbourne. The play was a
little too good, anyhow, for the average
theatre - going fool, whilst the critical people
couldn’t get away from the thought that Mrs.
Potter and Bellew would have been more
“romantic ” and picturesque than Julius and Miss
Ferrar in the two leading parts. “Prisoner of
Zenda” failing to boom, the Firm tried “Two
Little Vagabonds,” and here Miss Ferrar and
Julius Knight were at their worst as the lugu-
brious heavy parents of a long-lost child, the boy
Dick and his consumptive pal, Wally, being the
chief features of the show. Up to this time there
was no apparent probability of Julius Knight’s
engagement proving memorable. When he
passed on from Melbourne to Sydney his
departure was a merely casual event. When
he reappeared at the Princess’s he was Marcus
Superbus in that clap - trap, mock - religious
melodrama, “The Sign of the Cross.” Audio!
the women adored Marcus for his gaudy profli-
gacy, his fine bare arms and his impressive, padded
legs, and in his professional and social capacities
i which are_ practically one capacity for an actor)
iulius Knight stood about 15ft. higher than be-
fore. The lamentable “ Sign of the Cross ” made
a new man of J ulius Knight, and from that time
up to now Melbourne people in general have never
thought of the Julius who played the hero in a
first-class stage story 27 months ago, and seemed
hardly big enough for the part. He takes away
with him an Australian reputation, not earned by
the best work he has done here, but achieved
through the influence of his Roman legs. Nine-
teen women out of twenty consider that Mr.
Knight’s limbs were the two grandest bits of acting
they ever saw.
** * *
“ The Forty Thieves ” pantomime extrava-
fanza, which was a Xmas show in Sydney, will
e produced at the Princess’s on Saturday (20th)
with a slightly different cast. An imposing him-
perial tableau, entitled Britannia and Her Sons,
will be added for the first time to the original
spectacle, and the ballet will include sixty dear
little souls in raiment of surpassing beauty.
** * *
Bland Holt, at the Royal, has also ar-
ranged to put up a new attraction on Saturday,
when Australia will get its positively first experi-
ence of “ How London Lives.” We have
had dramas “Lost in London,” also “Lights
of London,” and “Alone in London,” and
we are acquainted with the girl who
escapes along a telegraph-wire “When London
Sleeps.” This time the metropolis is going to
disclose its latest modes of living, on a scale of
exceptional splendor and awfulness. Meanwhile,
“ Woman and Wine ” continues to supply a public
want, and the steeplechase grows reckless in its old
stage. Last Saturday, one of the rank outsiders
fed heavily. Likewise the jockey, who slips from
the “dead-bird’s ’’saddle, was so late in slipping
that he had to let himself drop with a bang. The
fury of Walter Baker and the surrounding punters
seemed selfish under the circumstances, for they
were not the only persons who suffered through
the jockey’s misconduct. He felt rather hurt him-
self.
** * *
The Bijou is full every night since Cin-
que valli began to roll his head around the cannon-
ball, consequently the festive little marvel presents
the samp list of juggles as in the beginning, and
keeps his changes of programme in reserve. But
he changes his silk fleshings and bodice several
times a week, and each suit looks more expensive
than the one he juggled in previously. The other
night Cinquevalli broke his artistic silence by
dropping a few words re an uproarious admirer in
one of the private boxes. As an apology for being
unable to perform the famous billiard-ball-balanc-
ing feat, he smiled sadly at the noisy stranger and
said, “ Sometimes the silliest little thing puts me
out.” Which was Cinquevallian.
** * *
Melb. Town Hall was almost crowded last
Saturday afternoon, when Marshall Hall shook
his baton at 55 instrumentalists and set them going
on Schubert’s melodious unfinished Symphony.
The programme was calculated to attract many
people, and it fetched them accordingly. Good
music, treated as Prof. Hall and co. treat it, will be
always well patronised if it is not a little too
good for this world. Weber’s “ Oberon ” overture,
“The Dowie Dens o’ Yarrow,” by Hamish
MacCunn, the Schubert work aforesaid, Mar-
shall-Hall’s “Study on Tennyson’s ‘Maud’”
(dramatically sung by Madame Wiedermann), and
Brahms’ pianoforte Concerto in D minor, kept the
multitude reasonably happy for about two hours
and a half. Of these five numbers the Concerto
scored the biggest success, on the strength of its
all-round effect, and the particularly interesting
perforniance of Edouard Scharf, who took the diffi-
cult piano parts from memory.
** * *
Next Saturday afternoon the second of
Allan’s ballad concerts will be given at the Town
Hall, and in the evening Walter Bentley is to try
his “Mirth, Song, and Story ” entertainment. At
St. George’s Hall the course of Dante the Great
runs smooth, and the fact that the elegant
magician and his show are all right has become so
generally known that some of his tricks get a
round of applause before they begin. At the
Alexandra the Grattan Riggs season ends this
week, and on Saturday old friend Dan Barry will
pop up again, after a long absence, with “ The
Sixth Commandment.”
Q. Chief Justice Griffith, when interviewed
by a Northern newspaper man, declined at first
to say anything about Federation; but, being
pressed for at least a few words, put his views
into three —“ Go for it! ”
Roderic Quinn’s book of verses, “ The
Hidden Tide ” (Is. 9d. post free from Bulletin
office) has received uncommonly good critical
notices everywhere, and has evidently nestled
close to the hearts of Australian poetry-lovers.
By the way, what is probably Quinn's finest story
—“Margaret”—will appear in next Bulletin
Xmas number.
The Secret Conference Telegraph (so-
called on account of the Secret Conference of
directors, at which it was stealthily decided to
oppose Federation) tells the public that, under
Federation, N.S.W.’s Id. town-postage “must
go.” Why? There are four provinces—N.S.W.,
Tasmania, Queensland, and W. A.—with Id. town-
postage, and why will they give in to the two
provinces which have 2d. postage? Also, there
are three provinces with free newspaper-postage ;
why is it certain that they will give in to the other
three? Will the Australian press kindly rise up
and yell that deafening interrogation into the
shameless ear of the King-street organ till it gets
an answer ?

“Man’s Enemy”—another cursed derrink-homily—
defeats its purpose in the usual way by showing that the
really bad man of the play “ never touches,” because
it he did he wouldn’t be able to be half so wicked ;
whereas the man who does drink is a real good sort of
chap, who, if he’d been teetotal, might have been as bad
as t’other fellow. The episode of the good girl who
won’t marry the reformed boozer—not because the
Staunton curse might descend to her children—which
would have some sense in it—but because his adventuress
wife isn’t dead, is a biting commentary on the materialism
of modern Christianity. If there’s anything the ortho-
dox Christian is strong upon it is the small importance
of Death in the scheme of things—a mere nothing in
comparison with sin and crime. Yet to Death alone—
not to sin or crime—is eriven the power of severing the
marriage-tie. The sin of being dead is the only sin a
wife can commit that entitles a Christian husband to a
divorce.
Nowadays the good old word, “benefit," is always
dropped in favor of “complimentary testimonial
matinee,” or something equivalent. Old-ti'me stars used
to stipulate in their contracts for monthly or quarterly
“ bens.,’’ and they always saw to it that bumper houses
resulted. Half the benefl jiares of the present day also
work up their own “complimentary matinees” —but not
in the outspoken way of the bluff old-timer.
Nellie Stewart has to work hard for her salary at Drury
Lane. Matinees on Monday, Wednesday, Thursday and
Saturday.
At the auction of Leon Caron’s effects the other day in
Sydney, two violins were put up. One instrument,
worth about £20, went for something over £4. For the
other, a violin of delicious tone which has been in Caron’s
hands over 20 years, there was no bid beyond a playful
suggestion of “ three bob.”
Melbourne people remark that Peggy Pryde’s last
nights at the Bijou were advertised as the last nights in
Australia—and now the little woman is back again at
Sydney Tivoli. Why these business tarradiddles? An
established importer of “ stars ” makes a mistake in not
teaching the public to put implicit faith in “ farewell"
announcements. One of these days Rickards will adver-
tise the “absolutely last” appearance of a celebrity—
Cinquevalli, perhaps—who is actually leaving the city for
good, and many who wouldn’t otherwise have missed the
show on any account will take it for granted that the
artist is coming back once, anyhow, and will wait for the
chance that never comes off.

laide Royal.

An anti-Federal lie which should make
the Sydney Telegraph’s financial editor snudder
at the awful arithmetic of his paper appeared in
that organ last Tuesday:—
The ABSOLUTE MAJORITY amendment made at the
Premiers’ Conference is not so simple as it looks.
It provides that amendments in dispute between the
two Houses shall only be deemed to be carried if at the
joint sitting an absolute majority of the total number of
members of both Houses support them.
Consequently, if parties are almost equally divided, a
few members on the weaker side can nullify the provision
by staying away from the sitting.
If they do so, a majority of the total membership of
both Houses cannot be obtained.
There will probably be 109 members in the two
Federal Houses, though the actual number makes
no difference. When they sit together to decide
a deadlock it will require an absolute majority-
55 votes —to carry the proposition, whatever it is.
If the majority is only the smallest one possible,
then there will be 55 votes for and 54 against.
And the above lunatic gravely alleges that if “a
few members on the weaker side ” —a few of the
54 that is—stay away, the 55 will therefore cease
to be a majority of the total membership of
109, and the provision will be “nullified.” Any
person blessed with even the rudiments of arith-
metic would reckon that, even if all the weaker
side —the 54- stayed away, the fact that 55 is a
majority of 109 would still remain. It is founded
on the solid rock of the multiplication-table, and
the staying away of the entire universe couldn’t
alter it. Isn’t the Telegraph at all ashamed
of the exhibition it is making of itself? *

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
Maoriland, according to a rather dilatory bit of
cable intelligence, has a surplus for the year ended 31st
March, 1899, of £274,717, and a credit-balance of £221,144
brought forward from last year makes the total surplus
£495,861. Most of this will probably be transferred to
the Public Works Fund for railway-building and other
purposes, for Maoriland is the only one of the Seven Pro-
vinces that has no old deficit to wipe off. But, all the
same, the past year was a most lamentable one, so far as
the public debt was concerned. The public incubus
increased from £44,963,424 to £46,938,006 —an addition of
£1,974,582. This isn’t quite so bad as it looks, for £500,000
of the money went to provide funds for the State Land
Bank to lend out on freehold security, and £499,000 was
for the purchase of estates for closer settlement —still, it
is bad enough. And, in view of these things, the Tory
howl for a reduction of taxation is a very ill-timed
hypocrisy.
The Maoriland returns and some recent news
from W.A. makes it possible to show how five of the
Seven Provinces are getting on so far as the financial year
has gone :
Surplus.
Queensland (lOmonths) .. £686,991
Maoriland (12 „ ) .. 274,717
Deficit.
Sou !h Australia ( 9 „ ) .. ±10,637
Westralia (10 „ ) .. 107,248
N.S. Wales (10 „ ) .. 574,724
Except in the case of Maoriland these are, of course, only
interim reports. The N.S.W. deficit will almost certainly
be much smaller, and the Queensland surplus very much
smaller at the end of the financial year.
» • * *
Up to 31st March, 1899, the old-age pensions
granted in Maoriland amounted to £128,273 per annum.
As the bulk of the claims are now in, this probably
means that the annual liability, when all the patriarchs
have been overhauled and sorted out, won’t exceed
£150,000 per annum for the present.
* * * •
At 30th June, 1898, the public debt of Westralia
was £8,947,954, or ±52 6s. 9d. per inhabitant. At 31st
Dec. it was £10,188,175, or £6O 11s. lid. per inhabitant.
In ’9l Westralia owed £3O ss. Bd. per inhabitant, and was
the least-indebted province of the Seven. Now it occupies
a middle place, having piled up a larger debt, in propor-
tion to population, than Victoria, N.S.W., or Tasmania.
In another year, at the present rate, it will have also left
Maoriland behind.

The municipality of South Singleton (N.S.W.)
estimates that its receipts from all sources for the present
year will be £514, and expenditure £857. And nobody
looked surprised or shocked, or suggested that the rates
should be increased in order to make ends meet. Appa-
rently South Singleton is quite satisfied to scoot along its
cheery road to insolvency.

£5,973,670. ’

The A.M.P. Society issues its balance-sheet (the
fiftieth) for the year ending 31st December, 1898, and a
very prosperous-looking document it is in every respect.
Some recent years’ revenue items pan out as follows :
Premiums in- . ,
eluding con- Interest Total Added
sideration for &c. Income. „ tO ,
Annuities. Funds.
1893 .. £1,272,171 £669,779 £1,941,950 £626,138
1894 .. 1,270,835 699,654 1,970,489 480,664
1895 .. 1,311,585 698,259 2,009,844 727,372

1897 .. 1,374,296 706,270 2,080,566 751,039

The A.M.P.'s accumulated pile of wealth now' amounts to
£15,179,049, which should be sufficiently stupendous to
satisfy anybody. It provides £36,245 for depreciation of
securities and wear and tear of things in general, and dis-
tributes £479,742 in bonuses, as compared with £445,235
a year ago. The expenses (£l3 18s. 7d. per cent, on
premiums or £8 19s. lOd. per cent, on total receipts) are
heavier than they have been any year since 1892 and show
a considerable increase on last year’s figures, but the
A.M.P. is still by far the most cheaply-conducted life
office in Australasia. The statement of accounts is clearer
and more complete than that of any other life office in
these provinces, and the list of assets shows no dubious
item or items to v'hich, on the evidence now before the
court, any objection can be taken. Though why the
A.M.P. doesn’t issue a detailed catalogue of assets after
the fashion of the Ne.w York Life 00. and thus put things
beyond question it is difficult to say. One good feature is
that the great pile of mortgage investments has diminished
a little, and a possibly good feature is the increase of
£580,000 in the holding of Government securities. It
would be an undeniably good feature if it wasn’t that
£505,000 of the increase is caused by an investment in the
rather dubious securities of Sandgroper Land.
One thing which is rather regrettable is that
the reserve for possible depreciation of securities has been
allovved to run down from £288,000 a year ago to £266,000.
The sum looks large, but it isn’t much over 3 per cent, of
the mortgages, or l)/> per cent, of the total investments.
Also, it is noticeable that the interest earnings have made
another considerable drop—a trouble that all the as-
surance offices have had to face of late years. The rate
realised in the last six years has been :— *
1893 £5 13 6 per cent. 1896 £5 0 5 per cent.
1894 511 10 „ 1897 417 0 „
1895 5 5 2 „ 1898 414 10 „
One of the standing arguments for the A.M.P. Society
when the annual comparison with its hated rival, the
Maoriland Government Department, is published in the
S.M. Herald, has been the extra interest rate earned by
the private office, hut considering that the A.M.P. is now
down to £4 14s. lOd. against the State office’s £4145., there
is so little margin left that this branch of the row ought
to cease somewhere about now. The steady drop in
interest shows that the A.M.P. w r as w'ell-advised w'hen it
took time by the forelock and reduced its estimates to 3Vf>
per cent.
Despite the increased expenses, however, and
the reduced interest, the A.M.P. Society is a very great
and prosperous affair, and few concerns of its kind in this
world can compare with it. Also its prosperity seems
to become more aggressively healthy ever year.
And just on account of its prosperity and its
great size, the question of State Life Assurance
and State Audit becomes more and more pressing
in Australia. The population of these provinces is so
small, and it has got so enormous a proportion of its
eggs in the A.M.P.'s big basket, that if anything went
wrong with that institution the consequences would be
too shuddersome to be seriously thought of. In fact, the
A.M.P. is growing so large that, if anything very bad
happened to it, Australia would feel like closing up alto-
gether. So big a pile ought to be surrounded by every
safeguard that the State can give it.

The Colonial Mutual Life Assurance Society
issues its report for 1898. The revenue figures compare
with some previous returns as follows :
Premiums , , . .. , ,

1894 305,530 .. 86,953 .. 892,483 .. 131,328
1895 303,983 .. 89,703 .. 393,686 .. 130,207
1896 311,558 .. 81,147 .. 392,705 .. 117,137
1897 312,450 .. 84,562 .. 897,012 .. 112,370
1898 322,768 .. 90,130 .. 412,898 .. 108,314
Last year’s income is therefore the best the Colonial
Mutual has had up to date, both premium and interest
receipts showing a large increase. The total funds are
now £2,261,656.
In other respects most of the objections which
The Bulletin took to the Colonial Mutual’s accounts last
year still hold good, though in a modified form only. The
C.M., like some other Australasian offices, has hitherto
based its policy-contracts on the assumption that its
funds, during the currency of its existing policies, will
earn 4 per cent, interest. It has either to keep on earn-
ing the 4 per cent., or make provision, by setting aside a
large sum of money out of its profits, to reduce the as-
sumed interest to, say, 31 per cent., or else be insolvent —
in which respects it is just like any other office. The
Colonial Mutual last year earned £4 Is. 7d. per cent, on
its total average funds, which is a very narrow margin,
though it is better than the figures for 1898, when the in-
terest earned on the average funds was only £4 Os. lOd.
per cent. No other office of any size or standing in Aus-
tralia considers itself safe with so narrow a margin to
provide for a possible further drop in interest rates.
Under these circumstances, the Colonial Mutual manage-
ment has done one eminently wise thing. It has decided
to increase its premium rates so as to enable it to place
its business on the basis of a3J per cent, estimate. Pre-
sumably this can only refer to new business, the contracts
with old policy-holders being a fixed quantity. The new
arrangement will make the institution much more secure,
still it doesn’t leave so large a margin of safety even on
the new business as most other offices think desirable, and
apparently the old business remains just about where
it was.
Some figures on this subject are as follows :
Interest

actually which es-

Do. Old business ..417 400 17
The Mutual Life Association is reducing its estimates of
interest to £3 10s., reckoning that the present margin of
14s. 9d. is too narrow, and the National Mutual lately set
aside £50,000 with a view to a similar reduction. Among
the large Australasian assurance offices the Colonial
Mutual, even with its 3J per cent, estimate for new
policies, has the smallest margin, and the best that can be
said for it is that the Widows’ Fund, which is getting into
a rather bad way in this respect, has almost as small a
margin. As regards the old policies the Colonial Mutual
has a better margin than it had last year (it was then only
10d.), but a remarkably small one none the less.
The trouble is all owing to the fact that
the Colonial Mutual earns less interest on its
funds than any other of the large Australasian
assurance offices. If this arises through the fact
that a large portion of its business lies abroad in
countries where interest rates are low, then the thing
can’t be helped ; in that case it is only necessary to re-
mark in passing that the extension of the business to
England, Scotland, Ireland, the Cape of Good Hope,
Natal and the Transvaal, if it produces such consequences,
is a pretty bad disaster for the Australian policy-holders.
Under these circumstances the Colonial Mutual, if it
wants to make itself anything like as safe a concern for
its policy-holders as the A.M.P. or M.L. State Depart-
ment, needs, not to reduce its estimates on new policies
to 3i per cent., but to reduce its estimates on all policies
to 3 per cent. The large sum of money which that
would require would knock the bottom out of bonuses
for a while to come. And if the small interest earn-
ings arise through the fact that losses and deprecia-
tion of assets haven’t been fully provided for, it will re-
quire a writing-off of about £300,060 to bring the interest
earnings up to even the modest figure shown by the
Widow’s Fund. Presumably the management will face
this question at the approaching quinquennial investiga-
tion and put its institution on a more satisfactory footing.
But it looks like a pretty large question even now, though
the decision to increase premium rates certainly removes a
part of the difficulty.
On another point the Colonial Mutual shows a
marked improvement. Expenses are a little over £l9 4s.
per cent, on total income, or about £24 12s. per cent, on
premiums, and though these figures are very high—much
higher than they ought to be—still they are about £1
per cent, less than last year’s outgoings. The balance-
sheet still fails to distinguish between accrued interest
and interest in arrear, or to show separately property
acquired by foreclosure, but this habit of reticence seems
to be incurable. To sum up—things look a little better
with the C.M. all round, but they need to look a great
deal better yet before they will be quite satisfactory.
The Australian Mutual Fire Insurance Society
had a good time during the year that ended on 31st
March, 1899, and realised a net profit of £17,530. A
combination of good luck and good management has pro-
duced the following handsome show of profits :
1892 . £15,546 1896 .. £15,087
1893 .. 15,377 1897 .. 15,851
1894 .. 17,068 1898 .. 17,647
1895 .. 15,668 1899 .. 17,530
With last year’s balance the sum available is £18,709.
The shareholders receive 8 per cent, dividend, £2OOO.
Twenty per cent, of the premiums (£8109) is divided as a
bonus between shareholders and policy-holders ; policy-
holders get a rebate of £4055, and the same amount is
divided among shareholders, so the latter get the gor-
geous return of over 24 per cent, per annum. Another
£2500 is added to the reserve fund making it £lOl,OOO.
This is the largest fire insurance reserve in Australia, the
Australian Alliance (Melbourne) with £75,000 being a poor
second best —though the two Maoriland giants leave both
these records so far behind that the best Australian co.
doesn’t get within cannon-shot of them. The Australian
Mutual Fire also adds £5OOO to contingency fund,
making it £14,000, so the total reserves are now
£115,000. The balance-sheet is painfully reticent about
the nature of the investments, the great bulk of them
being mentioned in one line as “ Investments, city free-
hold property and mortgages, £108,89Q.” Still there is
no evidence against the absolute soundness of these secu-
rities, and, by reason of its high record in all other re-
spects, the co. is hereby discharged without a speck on
its character.
• • • •
M'Lanrin stated in his anti-Federal speech that
Reid’s estimate of £500,000 as the cost of the Federal
Govt, was too low, and that he put it at £750,000. Then
he proceeded, a little later on, to say that N.S.W. would
have to contribute £1,631,000 in customs duties towards
that £750,000, and out of her contribution she would get
back £612,000 under the clause which provides for the
return of three-fourths of the customs revenue to the
State—£6l2,ooo being quite obviously three-fourths of
£1,631,000. A statement like that amounts almost to
genius.
* * * *
A considerable sensation in Sydney commercial
circles is the embarassment of a large cash grocery busi-
ness, noted for its big Govt, contracts. Several of the
middlemen will be hit for large sums if the firm seeks the
Insolvency Court. The disaster, no doubt largely
attributable to bad management, was in no small measure
the result of a family row.
B.H. Block 10 issues what the directors intro-
duce as “ the best report for many years.” The net profit
for the half-year was £50,723 ; this is inclusive of some-
thing (probably about £15,000) received for zinc tailing's
which were produced In former years. Block 10 has,
however, for eight years past consistently followed a
policy of equalising divs. as far as possible, and so only
£20,000 has been distributed among shareholders—less
by 15000, that is, than was paid in the corresponding
half of ’97-8, when the profit was only £20,546. It isn’t at
all safe, therefore, to accept Block 10’s div.-announce-
ments as a guide to the state of things at the mine. Of
the surplus of £30,000 odd a trifle goes to the depreciation
fund, £15,000 is used as the basis of a reserve-fund
(and these directors don’t mind saying how it is in-
vested—in Victorian Government 3 per cents.) and
the balance goes forward. The reserve fund is to be
added to, and is to be employed, “ should occasion arise, to
erect additional works for the further treatment of by-
products or to equalise dividends.” At the end of the
half-year (March 31) the co. had, apparently apart from
the reserve, £28,085 cash in hand and other liquid assets,
which made a total of £69,468 ; the only doubtful line was
“estimated surpluses to be received, £734o”—and, as
things have turned out, that must be more than all right.
And the total liabilities were £6027. With the mine look-
ing well, with the mill doing more work as well as a little
better work, and with a good metal-market, the directors’
observation may pass that this is the best report issued by
the co. for years.
* * • •
Lewis Lloyd, owner of Burraga mine, Bathurst
district (N.S.W.), left for England last week. The mine
goes on as usual making a big monthly profit from a com-
paratively small output. Before going-, Lloyd conde-
scended to accept another very substantial deposit for
buying option. Both deposit and principal are much
larger than hitherto received and agreed upon. Well
known that during the last 12 years several deposits have
been put up and forfeited on this mine and each time the
price has gone up. The last was paid and partially for-
feited by the representatives of the Anglo-Australian Co.
—why, it is hard to guess. The deal was proposed early
last year when the copper-market was well" up, the price
of the mine was low, and, having a splendid record of over
20 years, its acquisition would have gone far to save the
intending buyers from their present embarrassment.
They, too, unless writer is misinformed, admit their
serious mistake by recent overtures to Lloyd to take the
mine at a much higher price than that at which it has
now been bonded.

Carcoar, N.S.W. : “The Gallymont tributors
have cleaned up for a month’s work, 300 tons for 12dwt.
per ton. Expenses incurred at start will be a heavy charge
on first div., but for the next few months they expect to
reap a rich harvest.” This is really tew much ! Many
thousands in cash given for the property, tens of thou-
sands spent in plant, all under the active personal super-
intendence of the able and experienced Eissler. The
first crushing a horrible disappointment, the next worse,
and finally the mine shut down on the advice of a mining
engineer sent specially from London to decide what was
best to do ; and now, when Eissler has left Australia in
great distress over his failure, and they who once swore
by him are swearing at him, tributors are expecting to
reap a rich harvest out of the mine. A little while ago it
was stated that the Anglo-Exploration Co. was going to
console the Gallymont shareholders by the free gift of
another mine—Mt. Boppy—a custom worked with Wes-
tralian “ cats ” to such an extent that offers of this sort
became regarded as adding insult to injury. But the Ex-
ploration Co. denies that it thinks of giving all Mt. Boppy
to the Gallymonters.

News-wire from Blayney: “The copper-mine
has re-started work. The water-jackets have arrived and
are being erected. By mistake, a stamper-battery was
forwarded instead of a furnace, and but for this the fur-
nace would be erected.” The first is not true, and the
latter would be a good joke if it were. The news-man has
probably mistaken a Roots combined engine and blower,
weighing some 30 tons, for a stamp-mill—not a difficult
thing to do after having a few drinks at Blayney. Writer
has been there.
» * * *
Oobar notes. —Cobar’s main supply of mining
water ran out early in January, and no rain of consequence
has fallen since. The big copper mine now gets its supply
from Cobar Gold Mines, and pays handsomely for it. The
town tank for domestic purposes will be dry in a week or
so, and the G.M. Co. consents to give the people some
water at double the Government’s charges. “ It’s an ill
wind that blows no one good.” By the way, Cobar
Gold Mines aforesaid will need at least four months
more to get ready for a start on the large scale.
Arrangements for providing funds for the Chesney’s big
plant are expected to be disclosed at the co.’s coming
half-yearly meeting at Melb. The Great Western is
slowiy erecting an awkward toy-cyanide plant.—Mount
Drysdale and Eldorado cos. are erecting a cyanide plant
to treat their tailings—two heaps, each about 7000 tons of
tolerably-rich materials. There are three Huntington
mills on the spot to create more sand when rain falls. The
mine water has been found admirably suited for cyanida-
tion, and, as there is plenty of it, big things are expected
of the plant. Water in Drysdale main shaft makes quick
enough to keep the plant in full operation.
* * * *
Lake George, N.S.W. , March and April returns
show a production of over 11,000 tons, for 180 tons of
copper, 8360 z. gold, and over 30,0000 z. silver—ore aver-
aging in copper between l£ per cent, and 1| per cent. ;
gold, a shade over ljdwt., and silver a little over 2£oz.
Value per ton on price of metals 375. 9d. for March and
355. sd. for April. Costs for March, mining, handling,
smelting, development, and general expenses, 15s. 6d.
per ton ; estimated cost of transport and realisation, Bs.
7d. For April, 15s. 7d. and 7s. 7d. per ton respectively.
Accepting these figures of costs and values as accurate,
the profit shown for the two months is a fraction under
13s. a ton—a very good thing where production is large,
and could be doubled if warranted by stability of the
copper-market; but it is obvious, with copper at the
average price of the last five years, the Lake George, as
the Yanks would say, is a very “ close proposition.” Time
shareholders knew something definite re transport and
realisation charges, always given in the form of estimates.
All very well for the time being, but that is the last ever
heard of them, from which it may be inferred the esti-
mates are a little too modest; though 7s. 7d. and Bs. 7d.
on each ton of ore seem enormous. Imagine one-half the
total of handling, development, smelting, manag-
ing, and general expenses being absorbed in sending away
matte and realising on it? Would there not be a saving
in refining at the mine, or at the nearest coalfield ?
» * * *
The South Tharsis Copper Co., Mt. Lyell,
have lately entered into a contract with the Austral Otis
Engineering Co., South Melbourne, to supply a complete
crushing and concentrating plant for treatment of their
ores, which are mostly low grade. The plant is to be a
very complete one and comprises compound steam-engine
and boiler of 100 horse-power, 3 rock-breakers, 2 sets Roger
rolls, 8 4-compartment eccentric jiggs, 4 improved buddies
and Otis circular vanner, with the necessary trommels,
&c., for sizing and classifying the ore. The plant
has a capacity of 100 tons per day, with provision
for 200 tons. The Otis Co. are also supplying a self-acting
tramway from the mine to the mill; also, a pumping plant
to supply the mill with water from the dam.

Be the boom in dredges. Vic. Minister Best,
who has been visiting M.L., finds that 75 per cent, of the
dredges working on the Molyneux River are paying. They
cost from £4OOO to £lO,OOO each, the latter class being up
to latest date. A well-written description of the dredges
working on this river has been furnished Sydney Daily
Telegraph by D. K. Inglis, but it is sadly lacking in detail
and averages. Low capitalization is admitted—there is
no flood of vendors’ shares—and N.S.W. speculators are
asked to bear this well in mind. “ From 25 to 30oz. may be
looked on as the normal return for an average week’s
work, but at times, with a good bit of stripping, the
return may run away to between 600 oz. and 700 oz.” The
total expenses do not, it is claimed, exceed £5O a week.
But same article indicates occasional accidents when the
dredges would not work for days and weeks, while exes
would be about the same, if not more, on account thereof.

For some months, Melb. public have been read-
ing of the sale in London of the Crown Lyell copper-mine,
Tas., and shares fluctuated with the news pars. Then
came a hitch, for which particular beans were given the
directors. Finally, things were fixed up—everything
right hut the "deposit of £lOOO, and this was only a matter
of Baillieu, who had come to the rescue when things were
a bit mixed, cabling to London. Alas !—now announced
that negotiations have been abandoned, Bull having
decided to cry off. Little wonder, after all the haggling
and humbugging. The co. will now have to work out its
own salvation, and, when writer thinks of the time and
big money needed to get a copper-mine into the pro-
ducing stage, it has no picnic before it.
» * * *
Raspberry Land is enjoying a mild tin-boom.
Most of the crowd seem to favor lode-mining, a branch of
the industry that has so far proved a ghastly failure ; but
there is evidence that new blood, and perhaps better
management, may yet make something out of the tin
lodes. The inueh-talked-of Anchor is, at any rate, being
worked with better results, and promises to come up to
within decent range of what the less idiotic of its backers
claimed for it at first. The fancy locality for present-day
boomsters is Ben Lomond—a field of many alleged
“ lodes,” but small and patchy. “ Teddy ” Gaunt and a
following have the Rex Hill show on tribute from the
N.Z. Co., and have also a flotation option.

• * * •
The worst feature of many big Anglo-Australian
mining co’s is that the controlling interest is held by
financial co’s, often consisting of a mere handful of Fat-
men, so that the really important business is done outside
the companies’ meetings altogether. This is what is the
matter with Lake View Consols, and probably explains
the extraordinary financial policy of that concern. And
to kick against it seems about as useless on the other side
as it would be here. One venturesome shareholder, who
wanted the money earned to be distributed, has, he pub-
lishes, made a discovery that a day or so before the last
L. V. Consols meeting 34 persons had one share each trans-
ferred and registered to them, that these people attended
the meeting and voted (the voting was by show of hands),
and that the whole lot of them were at the time clerks
either of the L.V. Consols or of the boss London and Globe
Finance Corporation. No denial could be got from the
office; and, at latest, an injunction was being talked
about.

* * * *
The N.S.W. Railway Commissioners have been
again asked for, and have again refused “workmen’s”
tickets for workwomen. Why the man earning, say, 40s.
to 60s. a week should have a cheap fare while a shop-girl
with from 10s. to 20s. weekly must pay full rates is a
mystery to outsiders.

■ Lord Beauchamp, the new N.S.-Wales
Governor, was canny enough to say, on reaching
Albany, that at present he was not in a position to
express his views regarding Federation. Yet
Corkran, private secretary, handed the press the
following astounding message from Lord Beau-
champ to the people of N.S. Wales. It is in
verse, being an adaptation of a verse of Rudyard
Kipling’s “The Song of the Cities ”
Greet,—Your birthstain have you turned to good,
Forcing strong wills perverse to steadfastness.
The first flush of the tropics in your blood,
And at your feet success.—Beauchamp.
Strangely enough, this appears in S.M. Herald,
but not in Daily Teleuraph. What on earth
coula ever have induced the young gentleman to
thus “put his foot in it” by leading off with so
direct a reference to the One Forbidden Subject—
to the fact that the N.S.W. community is a pump-
kin, a large and succulent pumpkin, grown on a
particularly nasty dung-heap—is, and will pro-
bably ever remain, the profoundest of mysteries.
SEE MISUNDERSTOOD.
HIM: “ Woman! I did not deceive you! Before
we were married I told you I had a bad heart.”
HER: “ You did, Reginald—you did! But, as
heaven is my hope, I thought you meant heart-
disease!”
That clumsy and inexplicable birth-stain
reference of Lord Beauchamp’s roundaboutly recalls
Lady Jersey’s immortal “Who are these people
and whence have they sprung?” Most people
come to grief if, when they feel gushy, they
attempt to convey their over-wrought emotions in
poetry. If the unsophisticated young Earl, who
has come out here sans a qualified bear-leader, had
consulted (say) the experienced Capt. Wallington,
he could easily have ascertained that the denizens
of Botany Bay aren’t a bit proud of having risen
superior to the condition of poor grandpa, who
w:is lagged a long time ago. In fact, they aren’t
taking any reference, direct or constructive, to
grandpa aforesaid.

.N.S.W. church circles are disturbed about
the doctrinal and other ecclesiastical opinions
ascribed, rightly or wrongly, to the young Gover-
nor with the wavy hair and perfect teeth. A few
aggressive elc-ios, at a recent meeting, urged that
he deserved to be kept at arms’-length until he
purged hunself from suspicion. But it was ruled
that all should attend the levee and await the
future. I o stay away in a body from a Governor’s
levee would no doubt be an extreme step, but the
step was once taken by the R.C. Church in N.S.W.
JNo -‘YC'm cleric or lay, attended Gov. Denison’s
levee in 1809. The Gov. had in a lecture crossed
K.C. temper, the word went round, and the levee
was deserted. The action was intended to stun
the Gov. and the public. But the Gov. said he
didn t care, and good old S.M. Herald next
morning printed among those present the names
d n ru' 0 lay and cleric, of the
K.C. Church. Lady Denison, writing home about
it, said the reporter must have been asleep, or
composed his list of names before the levee started.
But Sydney gossip knew better. The Herald
merely meant to spoil the public effect of the R.C.
stratagem.

South Australia’s new Governor, Lord
Tennyson, has already had one compliment paid
him : the ‘Tennyson sausage” has arrived. In
this instance the compliment is a peculiarly
delicate) one, as every sausage is popularly
supposed to be some unhappy canine’s “In
Memoriam.” Gov. Buxton had a brand of beer
named after him, and as His present Ex.’s
illustrious sire wrote “ Grossing the Bar,” the
precedent is sure to be followed.
Not long ago, a man was sen-
tenced at Bendigo (Vic.) to three years’
hard for stealing some old shirts from
a lawn - tennis club - room. In same
Court, a few months back, a person
who had systematically robbed his em-
ployers for nearly two years got 18
months, and a petition is now being
extensively signed to procure his re-
lease, after serving but a few months
of his sentence. Moral —well, there are
several morals to this.

A lone Perth (W.A.) citizen was
recently absent from town for a few
days on a fishing expedition, and on
his return to his diggings was aston-
ished to find everything upside-down-
all his private papers, letters, etc., lying
scattered on the floor. On asking for
an explanation, he was told by his
friends, “Oh ! we thought you had com-
mitted suicide.” He has since been
wondering whether they were disap-
pointed.

« * *
“T.D.”: Quite the thing now
ladies in evening garb to ride home
from theatres atop of Sydney ’buses.
The staircase ’bus adds materially to
the inconvenience of being a man.
In bland weather he has to make
shift on the side-rail or creep inside
while fair damsels usurp his ac-
customed place, but he has to ride
outside when the stormy winds are
blowing ; else stand inside, with the
roof battering his head, and endure
the cold glare of the seated women
who regard him as an intruder.
The ancient and. abominable hurdy-gurdy
has been under a cloud in Sydney since the recent
arrival of a large orchestral organ of modern type.
The latter is a revelation in its way. People
generally supposed to detest any form of music
other than “ How-doo-dee ” (or something like
that) cluster and gape while the street instrument
turns off Handel’s “Hallelujah,” Mascagni’s In-
termezzo, and other such trifles. Writer, by the
way, is surprised to find that the “ Hallelujah ” is
just as good music outside a threepenny bar as it
is in a cathedral.

tin swimming pars, seem to indi-

men. If true, it shows
that the usual fool femi-
nine knows nothing 1 on a
subject of which she
should know all; but it
is only consistent with
woman’s inconsistency
that she knows less about
man than about almost
any other animal. Even
lacking the experience,
common sense should tell
her that the more hairy
a man, the nearer he is
to the brute, physically;
consequently, so long as
a woman served his pur-
pose he would not care
if she hadn’t a rag to her
back, or was all rags. It
is the smooth - skinned
man who reveres, loves,
and perennially pampers
woman as if she were
superior clay. He buys
her diamonds, poodles,
and bicycles, and will
walk himself, that she
may ride. The very hairy
man is simply a talking
gorilla. [Plausible, but

sive. Some of the world’s

been, and are, hairy men.
‘‘Perce” confounds phy-

tal development. Even
were his proposition true, it doesn’t

“Doctor”: N.S.W. State Chil-
dren’s Relief Board allows up to 7s.
6d. a week for the keep of children
boarded out to foster-parents. If
boarded out to their own parents
the maximum is 2s. 6d., “to pre-
vent imposition.” The depart-
ment has an army of inspectors
who, if they are worth their salt,
ought to make imposition impos-
sible. Either the former amount
is excessive, or the latter insuffi-
cient, e'xcept on the not un-
natural assumption that foster-
parents may be expected to starve
the children in order to feed themselves, while
the natural ones will starve themselves to feed
their children. Looked at from any point of view
the arrangement is a disgrace to civilisation.
* » * *
Sydney Guv’ment House has had several
thousands expended on it since the retirement of
Lord Hampden, to fit it for the reception of his
unmarried successor. This public money has been
paid out without the advice of Parliament or re-
gard to any particular section of taxpayers.
Lxpenses connected with the vice-regal reception
have been ordered in the same fashion, and in
other ways vice-regal life in N. S.W. is assured of
having its wants lavishly attended to. The public,
instead of getting a tighter grip on vice-regal
expenditure as the years go by, seems to be more
and more losing grip. Young, Denison, Fitzroy,
and had to furnish the family apartments
at Govt. House. They had to pay for their own
pleasure-travelling about the country. Parlia-
ment voted nothing for these expenses. One of
the bitterest of Parliamentary discussions arose
through James Martin (afterwards Chief-Justice)
charging the Govt, of the day with having pur-
chased a necessary piece of furniture for Lady
Young’s bedroom. But now, everything is bought
which a Governor likes to ask for. Ministry vies
with Ministry as to which will the more lavishly
pour the taxpayers’ money at the vice-regal feet.
* * « *
Brassey has once more “fallen off.”
Victorias tottersome Governor has long since
dropped off all his worldly possessions except the
mangle, and he is now reduced to dropping off
the same things a second time. So the unstable
prop of the State publicly fell from his horse in
Melbourne the other day, and banged his head
against a hard world. The newspapers charitably
try to disguise the affair by saying that the horse
was “ frightened by a passing pedestrian,” but the
public knows better. It is the old man’s con-
stitutional habit of dropping off breaking out
again. When Brassey is dead he will probably
fall up out of his grave the very first opportunity.
Lven death couldn’t quench the ancient’s yearning
to fall off.

Major-General French’s proclamation
shunting Tommy Atkins’creditors from the mat
of the pay-office is merely a revival, on soldiers’
behalf, of a venerable N.S.W. Civil service regu-
lation, which, in turn, was a survival of the
Phillip, King, and Hunter ordinance that police
and convict warders were not to be garnisheed or
imprisoned for debt. Caveat creditor was the
maxim in such cases.

THE IMPERIAL
SENTIMENT.
1ST TIRED CITIZEN: “ What dif-

THE ENTHUSIAST : “ Difference, you fool, differ-

“ The success of Marconi's lineless tele-
graphing across the Channel,” writes The Bul-
letin’s London correspondent, “ will, without
doubt, have far-reaching effects. Since I inter-
viewed him for The Bulletin nearly two years
ago, the young inventor has much improved the
working of his system. The telegraphing between
Dover and Boulogne was perfect though slow, the
maximum rate of speed attained not exceeding 15
words a minute. The short and long electrical
waves thrown off by the transmitter to register
‘ dots ’ and ‘ dashes ’ at the receiving station are no
longer allowed to spread left and right. They are
concentrated similarly to the directed rays of a
bull’s-eye lantern. The message passes into the
air from an 80-foot wired pole at South Foreland
takes a direct and restricted air-path across the
channel to the Chalet 1’ Artois, where a similar
pole stands, which collects the symbols and passes
them to the receiver. Move the receiverso feet to
the right or left and it will miss the message. What
quotations for a slump in cables and copper ? ”
* * • •
With wireless telegraphy and messages at
60,000 words a minute, telegraph-operators begin
to fear that their occupation will soon be gone. A
decade ago the youth who took to telegraphy
deemed himself “on to a good thing,” but the
majority have never got further than £2 10s. or
£3 a week, and the Civil Service Boards have de-
termined the fate of many others. Also, the
clicking instrument plays havoc with the opera-
tors’ nerves, and it is pitiful to note the subter-
fuges of the man, whose wrist has failed him,
hanging on to his billet as long as possible.

The Church of England is now trying to
keep pace with the times. One Melb. church has
taken the initiative and sent out a set of soul-
drummers who canvass the city in search of pro-
spective church members. The average bottle-oh
is mild and unassuming compared with these sal-
vation “commercials” who assail the front door,
shove their boots in the slightest opening, and talk
unceasingly until the home-father fires them down
the steps.

“I FEAR NO FOE.” —LORD BEAUCHAMP, THE NEW GOVERNOR OF N.S.W., “HAS NO INTENTION WHATEVER OF GETTING MARRIED."
The protracted haggle as to the seaworthi-
ness of the ancient soft-wood ship Republic is
more or less a repetition of recent history. The
question really at issue is whether you can be
dead-sure as to the soundness of a ship until you
have seen her, stripped of copper, in the dock.
To examine and pass a suspected ship without
docking and stripping her is like trying to listen
to a man’s heart-sounds through a thick suit and
a frieze overcoat. And all the blather in the
world won’t alter that fact.

More about that escape from Hobart
„ ao ] .
n)enth two pnsoners escaped-walked out of
Hobart’s old ramshackle gaol. The way out stared the
poor devils in the face every day, and gave them no rest
till they went it. They were away for a month, and
never left the neighborhood; and yet the police couldn’t
find them. One met a policeman in a public-house bar,
and “passed him the time o’ day, ’ and when, a week
after, he was arrested, he wd he spenUa day
without saluting a constable. But when the men were
caught (with the help of private people) the valiant-
police set about showing their enormous zeal. The new
commissioner from the poorhouse summoned a publican
for “harboring” one of the fugitives. The escaped one
was starving, and one night thrust his head through the
side-door and said— Pitch us a boh, Mick, to get some-
thin’feat.” And Mick tossed him a half-crown and told
him to go and give himself up. The two had made boots
together in their youth. Mick himself reported the in-
cident to the police, vet when Williams was caught the
publican was summoned for not running after the
escaped one and finding and securing him, and carrying
him before a justice. Mick weighs 24 stone, more or less,
in his socks, and couldn’t run if a hungry African lion
was on his track. “Going to plead K'/'d ty was the
question put to the huge one s lawyer. No fear! was
the reply of lawyer Propsting, “ we’re going to fight-
and, look here, we’re going to put the Attorney-General,
the Chief Commissioner, you, the two detectives, and
half the city force in the box, and show who’s to blame
for Williams not being caught.” The prosecution was
withdrawn in such haste that it nearly broke its neck
getting away.

Ihecareer of the Slattery anti-Papal troupe
has evoked one presumably vituperative pamphlet
from Holy Rome and filled one municipal council
with the ghosts of religious bitternesses. I he
Prali ran town-clerk had refused the town-hall for
a second Slattery lecture, and good Orange citizens
assembled in force at next council meeting to show
their disapproval of what they recognised as an
attempt on the part of the Scarlet Woman to sup-
press the glorious Terewth. For a while the chain-
ber was an edifying spectacle of warring bigotries,
whereby the great disinterested multitude learned
for the first time of the existence of the Slattery
combination. But, beyond this, the Yellow Pup
has refused to be stirred to a palpable fury, and
the season is a comparative failure.

Ihe notice published by Gen. Trench m a
recent N.S.W. Govt. Gazette, warning trades-
people against allowing soldiers to run lip bills, as
the Military Dept, will not in future hand over
any of the said sojers’ pay to creditors, brings
things somewhere near the practice in England,
where, according to law, a creditor cannot recover
more than Is. from an Imperial warrior. Imme-
diately a regiment arrives in a city at ’Ome,
several parties, consisting of a non-commissioned
officer or two and a trumpeter, are sent out to
p arade the streets and “cry down credit,” the
procedure being for said trumpeter to blow many
blasts until be gets a crowd together, when the
n.c.o. reads a formidable-looking document, eon-
sisting of much “whereas,” and “hereby,” and
“ hereinbefore,” and warning “all whom it may
concern” that aforesaid regiment will not be
responsible for debts contracted by any of its
members.

. .
JN .b. W. .Presbyterians donning armour for
their centenary thanksgiving of 1900 proclaim that
they must realise on that occasion 100,000 guineas.
A big sum, but with true business instinct they
earmark the first receipts with a sum of £l5OO for
an organising secretary. Communities with less
skill for money-making would have searched for
a secretary who would do the work for nothing, or
mere out-of-pocket expenses. The canny Scots,
however, know human nature better. They want
to rake in big money. They consequently tell the
man at the handle of the rake that they’ll give him
a cabinet minister’s salary. Whether or not they
get the 100,000 guineas, they start about it in the
right way.
YOU AND I
VYe will not heed the grey old loveless seers
Who write of tragic loves and seas of tears, •
Who sing “Joy comes not more through all the years.”
We will rejoice that God has let us stay,
Just you and I, dear heart, for but one day,
. ~ , ’ ... ~ ’
To laugh and love and cull white blooms of May.
To smell red roses where the gold bee sips,
To watch the stream where sunset’s glory dips,
o fk i . t
To hear soft laughter from sweet children s lips.
We have no wondrous mission, you and I,
No subtleties to weave as moments fly—
We are content to live, and love, and die.
NELLIE BRUTON.

A WOMAN’S LETTER
Sydney, May 16, 1899.
Mr Dear Mtee—
The pity of it! To think that, after all—all the
body-guard of aunt, sister, cousins, first-aides,
and nurses—Sonny has gone and got into
mischief. This enervated, aggravated, desecrated,
birth-marked city wonders if Beauchamp’s first
pill is a sample. The inherited colonial constitu-
tion will never swallow another with the coating
of “ Your birth-stain have you turned to good ! ”
Somebody in Authority must really point an
arrow at the ways our green young Governor
mustn’i go. Or has anybody been “pulling his
leg? But there—the shackles are so heavy on
me that my pen cannot do justice to this painful
subject.
Only, in passing, a window in Hunter-street
rather anticipated things last week with a burst
°f red-and-white chokers (not to be tied under
mi? ear )’ the “ Beauchamp ” necktie.
Ihe Beauchamp birth-stain is red and white,
don’t you know.
Apropos of absolutely nothing, a bride, who
belongs to a hoary Australian family, received a
dreadful wedding-present lately. At first glance,
it was an artless tablecloth of lovely linen. Bor
border, some exquisite embroidery worked out a
pattern that looked like the famous Egg and Dart
Everybody went into raptures over it. Then the
pure-merino bridegroom came along, and noticed
that the eggs were rather queer. He looked
closer, and discovered a wicked composition of
broad-arrows and. hangman’s loops. Luckily his
manhood stepped in, and he explained that some
woman who wanted to marry him must have done
this.
But, After all, what is Family ? What is Glory ?
What is Anything ? The story of Fairfax and the
Boots shipwrecks me on these spikey reflections.
r ° tlier day a bold, bad, blasphemous man went
into banners shop and said, “I am Mr. Fairfax
of the S.M. Herard, don’t you know. I want a
ready -made suit. . . . Also, a couple of hats,
and i am going up country and want a
pair of new boots. . . . Enter ’em all to
my account.” And no bolt came down from
the Herald to burn him up ! The ghastly
protanity of a Fairfax in a ready-made suit never
struck anyone in all that stylish establishment.
Ihe blasphemer got all he wanted, and ordered
the boots to be sent home. Outside the gates of
the family mansion lurked “ Mr. Fairfax ” as the
parcel-delivery drew nigh with the boots. He
seized them with eye, muttered words
to tne enect that he was going up country that
minute, and had been waiting there till those boots
came home, and made off round the corner. And
the poor benighted parcel-delivery saw nothing
incongruous in that —in a Fairfax who waited out-
side his gate for his new boots, and couldn’t go up
country without them ! Considering the splendor
of the gaudy young vision in knee-breeches and
burnished leggings,.that sometimes rises from a
certain editorial chair, this sacrilegious story must
be forgotten as soon as heard
It will take the new Governor some time to live
down the effects of that cablegram from Albany.
People in N.S.W. are terribly sensitive concerning
the birthstam ”so prettily alluded to by Kipling,
Beauchamp and co. It is well-known that the
deadliest revenge a vindictive person in Sydney
can take on some very high-toned families is to
paint a broad arrow on the gate-post during the
night. It is even said that one girl in Sydney who
is gaining a poor living by her own exertions ought
to possess much valuable city property; but to
Erove her claim she would have to acknowledge
erself of convict descent, and so she prefers to
struggle. There are weak spots in the story
apparent to the interested observer, but the inner
meaning may stand all the same.
Were Kipling to visit Sydney during the present
month it is almost certain that the last reception
of that man would be worse than the first. Before
the publication of Earl Beauchamp’s fatal messa,ere,
few people were familiar with “ The Song of the
Cities,” and the aspersion therein cast on the
Beautiful Harbor. Now Kipling’s “name is
mud ” to a vast section of the community, along
with somebody else’s. It is amusing to find His
Ex. now so pointedly remarking that he will
express no opinion concerning matters Australian
until he gets to know more about the country.
The burnt child dreads the fire. However, there is
one beneficent aspect to the Beauchamp blunder—
anything that may tend to stem the tide of
Kiplingmania comes as a temporary blessing.
The Fresh Air League Ball will be the Earl’s
first Australian hop. It comes off at the Town
Hall on Tuesday, 23rd May. The transcendental
pill, and all the little pillars of Society, promise
to make this the gayest ball of the season up to
date. By the way, why don’t the tickets say what
time the ball begins ? Is it to be so fashionably
and unmentionably late as all that?
His Ex. and suite will also shine on the Agri-
cultural Society’s ball—another brilliant affair to
be. This is a fixture for Wednesday, 31st May,
at the Town Hall.
The Jewish Ball —in aid of the Moses Monte-
fiore Home—follows next night, Thursday, Ist
June, at Paddington Town Hall.
By the way, it is just possible that Lord Beau-
champ and suite may shine on Madame Buhrow’s
dramatic entertainment at the Palace Theatre next
Thursday. A vice-regal telegram—in reply to the
committee—says that His Ex. will attend if not
too tired after the reception ceremonies.
For two or three years past some of the candi-
dates at the University public examinations have
been deaf mutes, but this year another innovation
is to take place. Several inmates of the Blind
Asylum, in imitation of Helen Keller, will present
themselves for examination, on June sth, at the
University. Of course, they cannot read the
, or wrl . ta tlu ; lr answers, but a number of
graduates, whom the authorities feel that thev
MV;n tr f L St ’ f re to be employed, as amanuenses.
h,, v 111 tne , re .be as many amanuenses as candidates?)
Ihe work is to be undertaken for love, not for
pay. It would certainly be much easier to do the
work outright than to write answers at another’s
bidding, especially if they happened to be the
wrong ones.
Prof. MaeCallum’s lecture on Madame de Stael
under the auspices of the Womanhood Suffrage
League on the 10th, attracted an overflowing
attendance. A member of the League (Miss
Meares, MA.) proposed a vote of thanks to the
lecturer and was seconded by another lady, who
remarked that she had successfully established a
oranch of the League at some country centre.
Whereupon an unmannerly man arose and said he
did not know what that had to do with Prof.
MacCallum s lecture, but he supposed it was use:
less to expect logic from the New Woman : also, he
could not see what the Prof.’s lecture on French
literature had to do with the League at all. Some
in the audience began to wonder if he could see
his way home.
A delightful “ young ” dance was the North
Sydney Rowing Club’s Ball at the local School of
Arts last I riday. The usual lining of chaperons
melted down to two or three ornamental figure-
heads who did duty for the whole big bevy of
pretty girls. Colored umbrellas shaded the lights
with a superfluous cunning considering that nearly
everyone in skirts was called Miss. Among lots
of charming girlish frocks a white chiffon struck
everybody with admiration of its three-tiered
skirt, early-Victorian bodice, and wide square
collar of indigestible-apple-green velvet. Pretty
Miss Lyne was in amber tulle with fragile-looking
rucmngs; Miss Randal Carey in sky-blue satin:
in short, there was not a dissonant note in any-
body s f rocking.
One of the ‘ ‘ Points ” is in a flutter owing to the
fate of two fascinating damsels and their still
more alluring mother. About a year ago the head
of the family died, and the three gave up house-
keeping and went to board. One daughter made
a wealthy marriage the other day, another is to do
ditto in six months, while the widow herself is
said to be engaged to an eligible suitor. All the
partners in folly are (or were) fellow-boarders.
But the saddest part of it is that the boarding-
house is already quite full, so there is really no
use in publishing this story.
The Pleyel concerts started their new season last
Wednesday at Y.M.C.A. Hall. Never wildly
crowded, these chaste concerts still support the
good old wheeze, the “’igher the Art the Fewer
the Few.” (A Cockney version is, “the
igher the Art the ’orrider the ’armony.”)
And the Pleyel audience is a thing you
can pick out from a crowd—long-haired, foreign,
and bent on nothing but a flow of soul, and
the best music it can get. Signor Alba was
Wednesday’s new feature. He turned out to
be a big bearded man with a voice like a trombone
—a great voice, the largest and longest we have had
here for some time. It rushed down underground
passages with smiling vigor and sent up new notes
from the lower regions unbroken by their dizzy
journey. An eccentricity of time and a formally-
announced cold stole something from the Signor’s
effects. Also, the beard was a slight deterrent to
a city that likes a lot of lip with its love-songs.
Signor de Beaupuis played Chopin and De Beau-
puis with just “ the little less ” that “ how much
it is ” ! This variable musician can play like an
archangel when he chooses, but on Wednesday he
was merely a Signor. By the way, two musicians
in this co. are so remarkably alike that quite a
phenomenon is thrown in with the concert, for one
is beautiful and one isn’t, and yet you can’t tell one
from t’other. As for the highly-cultured quar-
tette itself, whatever it does is well done, as
Schubert and Mendelssohn both testified this
particular evening.
There was also music made at the Railway
Institute last week. It was a benefit evening for a
sick railway-man’s family, and was entirely suc-
cessful financially. The big hall was filled. But
the audience was overfilled. Six comic songs in
succession, with five verses to each song, shed a
gloom on the evening. Miss Inez Bensusan re-
cited for twenty minutes to front rows only.
The back seats had to feast on her scarlet tulle
blouse. Nugent Robertson’s play was quite the
most cultivated plant in the bear-garden. A slight,
subtle little episode about a man who didn’t know
he loved the other woman till the husband of yet
another woman died and showed him that he
didn’t want her. Miss Mercy Murray played the
truly-loved woman gracefully, but rather wailfully
for a chosen one. A.N.R.’s acting would have
driven Wilson Barrett to epilepsy. He neither
posed, strutted, nor ranted. For an amateur it
was sensationally natural. He carried all his pre-
cepts into execution, even to the reading aloud of
an announcement of a death from a daily paper.
Your true professional always eloeutes when he
reads anything, whether it’s a love-letter or a
tailor’s bill.
The Press Bazaar still has its meetings. The
Daily Telegraph has withdrawn. The Evening
News regrets that a business agreement precludes
it. The Sunday Times think the Press should
have a show of its own six months later, and has
also gone back on the movement which started, by
the way, with the same journal. Now remain of
the papers, definitely (others may join later, or
assist), the S.M. Herald, the Chinese Herald,
the Freeman’s Journal, the Dawn, and the
Sydney Mail, a union that ought to beget some-
thing astonishing.
