Quite the newest idea in brooches has been in-
troduced to Sydney by a returned traveller. In
America everyone” now wears the coat-of-arms
ot her native city made in enamel or mosaic, and
lined at the back with gold. Sydney girls who
Tf, 6 born ln Halifax or Montreal are very proud
of the pretty brooches which have been sent them.
Velvet blouses are now made in America a la
Can bald 1, and a complete set of gold studs and
sleeve-links adds a finish to this rather severe type
of costume.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
Brassey s new aide, Lord Hindlip, arrived
sate and sound, and was publicly uncorked
on the first available opportunity. He arrived
when he was most wanted, too ; for two of Uncle
ioms other aides, Cecil and Pakenham, were
laid-up with accidents just before he put in an
appearance leaving all the ‘ work ’ (as they call it)
to be done by long Dicky. Up to the present, the
D. V. Association has not offered Hindlip a recep-
tion, nor has there been a Band of Hope demon-
stration to welcome him; but it is already the
correct thing for men to ask for ‘Bass’s, please,’
at the fashionable hotels.
“ Adelaide martyrs begin to suspect that Gov.
lennyson is but a shade more sprightly than his
predecessor. So far, he hasn’t tooled his family
out in an elderly one-hoss shay after the manner
ot Buxton, but he’s hung a millstone round the
neck of his career in the shape of a married, olive-
branched aide.
“ The group of .Melb. enthusiasts interested in
packing little Fritz Mueller off to Germany
might as well have the common sense to
take the ‘Young Vic.’ ticket off the lad’s back.
It s all right to provide him with the money
necessary for a first-class musical education, but
there is really no excuse for representing this par-
ticular boy-pianist as a future credit to Australia.
He has a German name and a German face, and
he s going to Germany, and if he achieves any dis-
tinction. it will be as German as the sausages.
Australia will not get a show in the matter. By
the way, talking of boy-pianists, what glory has
Victoria gained from the exportation of Percy
Grainger ! Ihe last time Percy’s name figured in
print he was said to be growing tired of the piano.
“ The 11-year-old Fritz (who seems likely, with-
out the assistance of any golden curls or fatal beauty,
to get a big send-off from Melbourne), was ‘ dis-
covered it is the fashion to be discovered, nowa.
days—-b y Mrs. Felix Levien. The musical exploress
didn t get much thanks for her discovery in the first
instance. Papa Mueller is tutor in Madame Wieder-
mann s family, but when Mrs. L. tried to convince
Madame of the nipper’s talent her eloquence fell
flat. Then she appealed to Marshall-Hall, with-
out success, for the Prof, ’s liver was in a cynical
mood, and, after remarking that the Conserva-
torium of Music wasn’t opened for babies, he sug-
gested that Fritz should be put to bed for an
indefinite period. Miss Burkitt, a former disciple
of Herr Pabst (who brought Percy Grainger out),
took the little fellow in hand and tutored him
gratis for two years, and her method has been
warmly commended by Herr Friedenthal, who
accepted the boy as a genius at first hear-
mg> and gave him several lessons last month.
Mrs. Levien, Mrs. David Syme, and other cham-
pions of the infant-pianist and sonata-composer
are anxious to pack him and his mother off to
Berlin in time for the August exams., so that he
may enter the big training-school there at half-
fees. The mother has influential relatives in
Berlin, and will be able to push her boy into
notice whilst she is looking after his young
health. This fact is privately circulated in order
to dispel the idea that Fritz might as well be
despatched by himself.

“ A dull, blue-nosed crowd came away from the
cold-muttony ’Varsity sports (9th May), sadder
and more snuffiesome than it went. In the
enclosure a few youths occasionally got into sacks,
jumped or cycled vaguely, most of them clad
hideously in utilitarian underwear. Among the
spectators, a tall, dark somebody looked spruce
and seasonable in green-tartan skirt, short, close-
fitting blue jacket, and velvet hat with pink roses
under the brim. The same belle, who trains her
glossy, wavy da,rk hair over her ears, shines at
evening shivoos in cunningly simple white frock,
trimmed with tiny longitudinal tucks, a twist of
purple ribbon-velvet round the waist, and cluster
of violets in the low-cut seamless bodice.
“A certain politician evidently really believes
in the levelling-up of the masses. One of his
housemaids is to be married shortly, and he intends
the ceremony to take place in a style equal to that
of his recently-wed daughter’s. He himself is
providing the wedding-feast, and his wife the
bride’s outfit and accessories. The members of
his family and their friends are going to wait on
the bridal-party at the wedding-breakfast, after
which the happy couple will depart to a fashion-
able hotel for a fortnight’s jaunt. What exalted
sphere will receive them on their return to every-
day life nobody knows ; but the ceremony is being
looked forward to as an immense joke by the
friends of the politician concerned. All the same,
if employers expended a little more sympathy on
their dependents, the eternal servant-question
would be well-nigh solved.
“ The Vic. marriage-market may be looking up,
but the educational world still seems very slack.

Nellie Stewart explains to London inter-
viewer that when playing pantomime-boy she seeks
to extinguish her eloquent waist with padding. If
this'sort of she-boy impersonation became general,
male-kind would have no further use for its eye-
sight. But man need not despair : the sex has
discovered that P.D. corsets not only preserve but
improve the grace of their form divine. The
wearers of P.D.’s never grow old.*

In a recent Windsor Magazine a tale, by
L. T. Meade, tells how one of the characters is to
go from Sydney to Albany (overland) to inspect
some mine. He would probably take the cable-
tram or a ferry-boat. The tale itself is more
poisonous than a “ Deadwood. ” One of the part-
ners in a big mining swindle turns “dog,” and
leaves Sydney for England to give his evidence.
The other partners buy a ship, which they sink
in mid-ooean in view of the mail-steamer, which,
of course, rescues the crew. The captain of the
derelict has a dog, which he is always beating.
The informer interferes to protect the dog, and is
bitten by the animal. He exhibits symptoms of
poisoning, and is finally saved by the doctor, who
discovers that one of the dog’s teeth was false and
filled with poison. Next!

PERSONAL ITEMS.
Robt. Gray, Queensland’s Railway Com-
missioner, started as a railway porter, and boasts
of it. So says Brisbane Worker.
The late James Service was a Spiritualist
in the days when the spook business was less
understood. Like Alfred Deakin, he seemed to
renounce it.
Of the 78 members of the Vic. Leg.
Assembly of ’7l only three retain seats, and 54 of
the 78 are dead. Of the Leg. Councillors of the
same year, but three are now alive, and only one
of the trio now holds a seat.
“ General ” Booth was met on Launceston
(Tas.) wharf and welcomed by the Mayor, a pub-
lican ; and when Booth, in answering the address
of welcome, gave the pubs, particular Sheol, the
Mayor’s face was a study indeed.
“ Our three-quarter-Portuguese Premier
stands on just the same level as any savage who
can neither comprehend nor appreciate the
triumphs of civilisation.” — Infuriated Tory paper
(the Critic) on Premier Kingston.
King O’Malley has taken his rejection for
Encounter Bay (S.A.) very philosophically ; he
has returned to the life-assurance business. It
was, by the way, on a corner of his late district
that the whisky ship Loch Sloy went to pieces.
Windsor Magazine prints a portrait of
Vic. Postmaster-General Duffy, the high-kicking
expert, and labels it “Sir Charles Gavan Duffy.”
Theaccompanying letterpress supplies the startling
information that the subject of the portrait is 89
years of age.
The N.S. W. anti-Federalists seek to make
capital of the fact that Mr. Braddon, a Sydney
resident, said to be a nephew of Premier Brad-
don, of “Blot” celebrity, opposes the Common-
wealth Bill. In matters of finance, however,
Mine Uncle usually knows a lot more than his
nephew.
A link between the old “ Imperial” times
and the present passed away the other day at her
residence, “Lisgar,” Liverpool-street, Darling-
hurst, in the octogenarian Mrs. Scott, widow of
Capt. Scott, formerly P.M. at the old Sydney
Central Police-court. Mrs. Scott came to N.S.W.
as a child.
“A Humorous Prince Satirises Syco-
phancy.” Thus the cable-headings re Duke of
York’s remark that, considering the number of
inquiries about his recent slight indisposition, he
might almost have been a Kipling. But, what
sycophancy in assuming that any “satire” was
intended !
Dr. MacLaurin, arcii-anti-Federalist, is 64
years of age. He spent 13 years as navy surgeon,
chiefly in the Mediterranean, and has been 30
years a Sydney resident. It is within the last 10
years—since Dibbs raised him to the Leg. Council
—that his political abilities, especially on financial
questions, disclosed themselves.

J imes Wilson, of Walgett. But, all the same,

well on to <£lo,ooo. A Parliamentary return
showed that Mr. C. G. Heydon alone drew, for 52
days’ work at Gunnedah and Sydney, <£llo7 14s.
The return stated that 17 other days had yet to be
paid for, and that other amounts were then out-
standing.
“ Oily ” and “ Dreary ” Q. Premier Dick-
son may be (writes a Bulletin correspondent),
but he owns the only private art collection in
Queensland worthy the name, and hi 3 savings are
reported to go entirely towards valuable additions.
It is not a collection of oleographs or vegetable
studies, but comprises pictures of merit—though
the choice is, of course, dictated by personal taste.
Surely an evidence of a mind with something
more than mere dreariness to recommend it. Ada
to this that Dickson is invariably courteous to
even the humblest messenger—a labored courtesy,
perhaps; still it’s there.

Apropos the cabled fact that the Arch
bishop of Canterbury and bishops and many mem-
bers of both Houses of the Imperial Parliament,
as well as leading Nonconformists, intend to pro-
test against the Sunday publication of the London
Daily Telegraph and Daily Mail. When little
alphabetical Bailey, of Sydney, first visited
London, he called upon Stead and intro-
duced himself as “the originator of Sunday
journalism in Australia.” “You say you started
the first Australian Sunday paper?” said the
Maiden Tribute man. “Yes,” proudly answered
Bailey. “Then, sir,” exclaimed Stead, “you are
an enemy of the human race.”
Rumors that Archbishop Carr, of Mel-
bourne, was to be given a cardinal’s hat must
have been mere ignorant say-so. Australian
R.C.’s must be hungry for pomps if they want
two cardinals, for England has a solitary cardinal
(Vaughan); Catholic Ireland has only one;
America has contented itself with Card. Gibbons
(but Archbishop Ireland is to be elevated); and
France, an essentially R.C. country, possesses
only two. The “ hat ” is, perhaps wisely, re-
served as a rule for Italian prelates. Of the pre-
sent College, far below its limit of 70 members, 24
cardinals reside at the Vatican, and the remain-
der are mostly distributed over Italy. A more
likely man than Dr. Carr for the coveted head-
gear is Archbishop Redwood, of M.L.
People begin to say that if a certain
Governor doesn’t leave off drinking, Parliament
in his province will be opened some day by an
Excellency in the horrors. And that Excellency
will be trying to brush imaginary cockroaches off
the Speech from the Throne, and will thereby
bring the vice-regal office into even worse con-
tempt than it is in at present.
Anglican Bishop Green, of Armidale, re-
cently took lessons in ploughing. Believing that
a sound body should go with a sound mind, he
doffed coat and gaiters, and strode over the fur-
rows for the better part of a month. But many a
man who stalks abroad to find a sound body to
match his mind should stay at home and inquire
whether it isn’t his mind that wants levering.
Bobkeb Smith, who went ’Oine Bishop
and came back Archbishop, received the latter
title, not because he is Primate of Australia, but
because he is boss-bishop of N.S. W. The deter-
mination of the Synod was that “ the metropoli-
tan of each ecclesiastical province now in existence
shall be designated Archbishop.” So that any
other Australian metropolitan bishop is entitled
to ask Lambeth Conference to give him rank as
Archbishop.
“General” Booth was asked, the other
day, how it came that he had abjured the military
cap and taken to a pot-hat. Explained in reply
that the expansion of the Army’s concerns de-
manded a bigger tile, as they couldn’t all be
crowded under the little cap of previous days. . .
The change is significant. It indicates unerr-
ingly the transformation of the one-time prole-
tarian apostle into a propertied prince among
devil-dodgers.
Significant that that awful failure, Theo-
dore Unmack, started the anti-Federation cam-
paign in the Land of the Leper. Theodore
Unmack is the slow, stupid German who once
held Cabinet rank under Griffith, but was per-
emptorily dropped by M‘llwraith at the Coalition.
He is an aged and sad and disappointed man, who
was kicked out of the Ministry and Parliament so
suddenly that he never quite recovered his bal-
ance. As a politician he was mainly distinguished
for his advocacy of “ten bob a week ” as a fair
wage for the average artisan. Theodore is a fine
advt. for Federation. Any cause that he objects
to must be democratic.
The row started by Madden, M.L.A., re
the method in which David Syine acquired his
Mt. Maeedon property 30 long years ago, and the
alleged exorbitant price which Govt, paid for
it when it bought the place for His Ex.’s summer
residence about a generation back, still simmers.
Judge Williams has been appointed a Royal Com-
mission to look back into the buried years and
find out if the Age autocrat really did the square
thing or not. But supposing any Commission
appointed by the Vic. Govt, presumed to find
that D. Syme had not done the square thing—
well, would the man who owns Victoria take it in
a peaceful spirit, or would he rush out and get
measured for a new Government forthwith ? Can
any Vic. Govt, afford to find that D. Syme ever
did anything wrong ?
Strange that whilst Graham Berry’s little
shop in the suburbs was continually thrown in his
teeth after he had taken his grip in politics, the
smaller shop of his opponent, “ Jimmy ” Service,
was forgotten. But Service began in a very small
grocery on Emerald Hill, now South Melbourne,
and ran it into a big business, making there the
foundation of his fortune. The queer-looking,
prison-like, bluestone house ho then built still
stands in what is now a laborers’ neighborhood.
It was thought to be a palace in “ Jimmy’s ” day.
Berry made the mistake of dropping his grocery,
but the pawky Service stuck to business, and it
grew with him, and was good to him in his old
days. Berry lived to be Victoria’s biggest
politician, Service lias died its biggest grocer.

(Sketched at “Gen.” Booth’s Melbourne meetings.)
Died atTodrak, non igenarian Presbyterian
pastor John Gardner, for half an ordinary life-
time at Qtieetiscliff. He leapt into a day’s fame
by an astounding utterance in Melb. Presbyterian
Assembly. The Sabbath question was on. The
Liberal school ridiculed the oldsters. One of the
new lights said lie supposed Mr. Gardner would
not take a lutli on the Sabbath. Gardner quickly
interjected “ I would not! ” Of course the news-
papers gibed at him, but he disdained to explain
that he only referred to a dip in the briny, which
he regarded as amusement.
Madame Rudalyski is in a bad way at
Pentridge. Almost stone-deaf, and with badly-
failing sight, the ordeal of the trial and the death-
sentence has turned the once-prepossessing woman
into a physical wreck. She is now unable to per-
form J;he ordinary duties of looking after her cell,
and last week in carrying out her bedclothes —
they don’t have much in Pentridge, either—she
collapsed and fell. At present it iooks very likely
that Radalyski will be released long before her 10
years a.re served—if not by the State on the ground
of ill-health, then by the Finisher of all sentences.
Tim Quinlan, Westralian M.L.A., recently
made a Chevalier of the Papal Order of St. Syl-
vester, was lately “ elevated ’ to that dignity at
Perth R.C. Cathedral. A local report states
that —
The uniform worn on the occasion was of a gorgeous
nature —comprising a large capello, adorned with a cock-
ade and feathers, scarlet jacket with epaulets, cream-
colored trousers with a gold stripe, high boots, and gilt
spurs. There is also the medal of the order, and a large
gold and enamelled medal suspended by a gold necklet,
and a magnificently burnished sword and scabbard, with
pearl and gold mountings. During the ceremony, the
Bishop having presented the sword, the recipient thrice
cleft the air with it, and, after the spurs had been ad-
justed, he was dubbed knight by the presiding pontiff.
There were also the prayers usual on such an occasion.
One good turn deserves another. It was the
self-same Tim who headed, with .£5O, the list of
subscriptions for a gift to the Bishop on the latter
setting forth upon the journey to Rome from
which he returned with Tim’s ehevaliership.
The “ mystic faith-healer, “Dr. Edwards,”
from Australia, recently arrested in Paris for
alleged fraud, is, it seems, identical with a man
well known on this continent. Arriving in Mel-
bourne in 1885, he was engaged as glove-fitter by
n leading softgoods firm, his name then being
Ldouardo Geisinardo. He was so clever at his
business that the glove-operations of his employers
grew by leaps and bounds. Then he took a shop
of his own, where his effective personality drew a
large business : a luxuriance of wavy hair, dark,
piercing eyes, and splendid Italian features
attracted the fair sex of Melbourne to his Glove
Boudoir.” Then he disappeared suddenly, and
was arrested at Albury for fraudulent insolvency.
He disgorged the money found on him, and pro-
mised settlement of his debts. Another dis-
appearance, and an “electric healer,” Prof.
Richard by name, shone in Melbourne. He was
visited by a patient, who discovered him to be
Geismardo, the glove-man, and, as he had proved
a debt in insolvency, he claimed the intervention
of the assignee. Richard fled again, and came to
Sydney, where he ;.:ave exhibitions of his “ healing
power by electricity.” He has now, it wquld
seem, bleached his jet black hair. The man who
now represents himself as St. Paul arisen from
the dead is no other than the clever Italian
glove-fitter.

Aboriginalities.
“Bunty Tom,” from way back
Dear Bulletin.— Sydney Evening News oritio, com-
menting on Brady’s “ Ways of Many Waters,” and com-
paring Brady’s work with Kipling’s -in fashion—takes
occasion to observe “ Kipling, who can only be said to
have touched at one or two Australian cities, has in a few
lines given a picture of inland Australia which not one
of the many Australian poets ... has been able to do .
“ We read of the English skylark,
Of the spring in the English lanes :
But we screamed with the painted lories
As we rode on the dusty plains.
Every old bushman recognises the picture and wonders
how the devil the fellow wrote it.”
If the infallible NEWs-mun’s tin-joss had written :
I suppose the News man would have “ recognised the
picture” and it would have stirred the bush instinct in
him to the depths; and the second picture would have
been about as typically-Australian as the first. The
writer has “ ridden on the dusty plains ’’—which Kipling
never saw—but he has met no lories. If he had, he
couldn’t have screamed with them, because the lory is
not a screaming parrot. He is a comparatively quiet,
cold-country bird, and his habitat is rather the “ chilly-
mountains” than the “ dusty plains.” Have shot lories
in the timber—between Appin and Bulli, about St
George’s Basin, in Burragorang, all along the Blue Moun-
tains west to Bathurst, around Blayney, and elsewhere
but cannot remember meeting them in the fiat oountry.
They may have been there ; perhaps the News man has
seen them—if he knows the difference between a galah
and a lory but I haven t, and none of the old bushmen
I have interrogated on the subject have, either.

An English magazine prints a “ paper ”
by an alleged Australian about “Fossiking” (sic),
in which a swag is made to consist of a rug and a
tea-kettle / Rigged with a rug and coddled with a
kettle ! O, maligned “ Maria ” ! One illustra-
tion shows “ Prospectors ” slashing away with
picks in rooky ground, while a “ Rush for Gold ”
ought to have been labelled “Departure of the
Israelites from Egypt.” If English publishing-
firms (in this case a rich concern—Cassell’s) want
Australian stuff, why don’t they go to real Aus-
tralians for it ? Heaven knows there are plenty
of them on the job.

“ E.N.” : Have heard of an extraordinary
freak of nature in the shape of an aboriginal man
minus any apparent head, and having a small hole
about where the base of the neck should be,
through which he took in food and drink. The
tribe to whioh he belonged repeatedly tried to
make a head grow on him ; until, at last, exasper-
ated at failure, coupled with certain visitations of
bad luck which were attributed to their ill-formed
tribesman, they resolved to hold a religious cor
robboree at which the headless man should be
sacrificed on a sacred rock situated at the foot of
what is now called Lennox Head, about 12 miles
from Richmond River Heads. A great gathering
took place on the day appointed, and after certain
leligious exercises had been gone through, the
headless man was hurled over the cliff so that he
tell upon the sacred rock far below—and was
smashed up instead of suddenly developing a
head, as the tribe had anticipated. Great rejoic-
ings, however, followed, the curse being now sup-
posed to be removed from the tribe. Some of
those who came to the Richmond River district in
the early days knew this human monstrosity and
often gave it food—or said they did.
* * * *
“ Yarrum ” on cruelty in the bush :
At a dairyman’s place recently came upon something
that made me weak. Bov was skinning calf on one side
of a gate, and cow (mother) bellowing and moaning
piteously on other. Fellow cut off a leg for pig, one for
the dogs, another for the house ; and threw balance over
the gate, with the remark to the cow—“ Here you !
You ought to get some of it.” Most of our dairy fanners
practically offer their sons a premium for cruelty by
agreeing that “ the boys” shall get all the calf-skins for
pocket-money.” Result—calf can’t look a bit “off-
color ” without being forthwith “ knocked on the head,”
and the hide whipped off. Specially the case before a
race meeting. At “ encampment ”or “show time,” also,
skinned poddies can be seen about the paddocks as thick
as mushrooms after rain.

Dear Bulletin. —lf “Steele Rudd”
means (8., 29/4/’99) that he has found the young
of the native bear in the mother’s teat, he surprises
me. Have found them in the pouch, and in utero
often; but in the teat, never. Has “Dad,” or
“Dave, ’ or “ Sal,” or “old Casey ” been pulling
“ S.R.’s ” leg? Prof. Caldwell, Caius Coll., Cam.,
is the greatest authority on the native bear—
tracing it through seven generative stages—and he
(Proe. Linn. Soc.) says nothing about finding the
young in the mother’s teat. I feel like St. Thomas
the Apostle.—M.

Dear Bulletin.— ln answer to “E.C.K.,”
the tiger-snake, as its specific name ( curtus) im-
plies, is a short snake, about 3ft. 6in. being its
average length, though in rare instances it may
attain oft. Varies in color from silvery-grey to
coppery-brown, and appears in seven different
colors, though its unique scale-arrangement (body-
scales in 18 rows anteriorly, and 19 posteriorly
with anal and sub-caudal shields entire) is persis-
tent. The T.S. is ovo-viviparous, and highly
venomous. Typical or best-known form, brown-
banded. The T.S. doesn’t “crush”; only the
python family do this. Snakes in the Lower
Macquarie reed-beds are not plentiful; and dia-
mond snakes don’t occur there, nor anywhere west
of the Dividing Range. Carpet and diamond
snakes never attain a length of 16ft. ; 12ft. would
be abnormal.—H. J. M'Cooet.

Bimble : I have a kangaroo of curious
tastes. Caught him when the drought was at its
worst—not a blade of grass to be seen. Fed him
on bread-and-water for a time, but he will eat
meat (raw or cooked), oatmeal porridge, sardines
dried apples, boiled rice, almost anything that
comes along, in fact. He is a whale on treacle-
pudding, and can pick a bone as clean as a dbg
can. If it doesn’t soon rain I shall have to
destroy him, as he is becoming rather an expensive
boarder.

“Ballarat”: In answer to “D.B.” (B.
29/4/’99), the best corrective of Barcoo vomit is
the avoidance of fatty foods. “ The Vagabond ”
has left it on record that the fryingpan has killed
more bushinen than the rum-bottle. Fat is a
strong component of all out-back diet, except
‘whitey.” Even “brownie” has fat as a chief
ingredient. The Filipinos live in a far deadlier
climate than the Barcoo-men, -but, by living on
rice, fish, and fruit, escape this vomiting sickness,
whereas the invading Americans were afflicted
badly therewith. Writer has kept sea-sickness at
bay by eating nothing - , the day before sailing, but
soda-biscuits (bile corrective) and fruit, and giving
a wide berth to ship-stews when on board.
x * * * *
“ K.D.L.” : “ D.B.” (B., 24/4/’99) errs in
calling that way-back malady “Barcoo vomit.”
It has always been known as the “ Belyando,”
“ Barcoo ” being the name for sores on the hands
caused by bites of flies fresh off carrion. The
“ Belyando ” was supposed to be caused by the
emanations from rank grass, or from “ penny-
royal ” and other strong-smelling herbs. Best
remedy is a table-spoonful of Lea and Perrins’,
taken immediately after meals.

“F.” : Heard a swaggy offer to bet drinks
he could make a fly empty a box of matches. The
bet was made, and swaggy had ’em easy. Cateh-
lng a fly, he held it by the wings between his fore-
finger and thumb, and allowed its legs to touch
the head of a match in a round box of vestas, when
the insect clung to it (the match), and, the opera-
tor lifting his hand, the fly pulled the match out.
In this way the box was emptied.
E.D.” : The majestic he-goat never re-
ceives sufficient credit for his extraordinary
intelligence. Served my time as a small boy >
in a township that was simply a goat-run
carrying about 250 animals to the acre, and
could fill pages with anecdotes demonstrat-
ing the superior mental attributes of goats.
Was the proud possessor of a large, adult
male doctored and set apart as a saddle-
hack, and that goat, after long experience
as a garden thief, developed an amazing
diplomacy. He would prise out the bottom
of a paling, enter the garden and eat his fill,
and after leaving would carefully replace the
paling, butting it into position. Experience
taught him that the paling would be secured
again if the opening were left obvious, and
that would have involved the trouble of
making another break when next he visited
that particular cabbage patch. At first he
was trapped a few times through over-
indulgence, beings so blown out that he
could not negotiate the opening; but he
was not such a goat as to fail to profit by
this adventure, and afterwards always modi-
fied his appetite to suit the available aper-
ture. Saw him more than once trying the opening
after a debauch ; if the fence fitted him tightly he
left; if there was still some slack he had another
go at the vegetables. He was then 15 years old,
and would have been alive yet, I believe, but for
a disagreement he had about a carrot with one of
the elephants in a travelling circus.

“ J.A.P.” : Apropos “ Swaggie’s ” lament
in recent Bulletin re the Westralian working-
man denouncing Asiatics, yet employing them
whenever a penny can be saved thereby. At
Cooktown, a few weeks ago, a large party of
Southern miners, bound for Papua, abused the
local residents for voting against the Labor-candi-
date. But they, the miners aforesaid, hired
Chinese to carry their swags to the wharf, at
Chinese prices, while the white carriers looked on.
After all, isn’t the doctrine of cheapness uni-
versal?

“L.S.”: Was camped once with a party
of bullockies, one of whom, a silent man, took no
part in the tough yarns going round until all the
rest had lied themselves to a standstill. Then,
however, he took a turn. “ I have had good
‘hides’ in my time. In fact, I owned the only
ones that ever buried themselves ‘snigging.’
Whenever a tough job was on I was always called
upon, and one day old M‘Fadden sent for me to
shift an ironbark tree from a ridge he was going
to build on. So I yokes-up 18; slings on best
‘jewellery’; hooks on to tree half-way up;
straightens ’em out nicely ; calls ’em to me ; gee’s
them off, an’ down they goes to it. All of a
sudden I hears a tremendous rumbling and rolling,
and remembers no more until I finds myself half-
a-mile away, half-buried in pebbles. You see,
that tree had such a big tap-root that them bul-
locks had pulled that there ridge right over and
buried themselves to all eternity.”

“Basalt”: Be deaths from thirst (B.
4/3/’99). Last year a blind Myall, two gins, and a
child at breast started to go from the Leichhardt to
the Clancurry. They did not get water where they
expected on their way and the blind man died,
the gins being found further on by some stockmen
—one dead, the other dying. The child also died
after arrival at station.

“ 5.,7. ” on the scenery-advt curse :
He “ the monotony of dull, drab, Australian scenery.”
The sign -painter is now abroad, and Australian scenery is
fast being cured of its monotony. From Broken Hill to
Balmain ; from Annidale to Albury—everywhere you go
—not dead-green but raw-blue and flaring-reds, relieved
with white or yellow letters, give you such prods in the
eye that you are glad to turn to the dull drabs and greens
for relief. The foreshores of Sydney harbor have been
brutalised by the soulless shopkeeper, who is now hard at
work dauhing-over the wfiiole country : one firm I know
of has no less than six men on the road with paint-pots.
Up to now I have not heard of Mt. Kosiuscko and the
Jenolan Caves being “beautified,” but I believe
Premier Reid has only to have his attention called to the
matter, and he will put on an army of men to paint all
N.S. Wales red, blue and yellow, and obtain a big surplus
from the revenue derived by letting out the country in
advertising blocks. Australian scenery is being de-
monotonised with a vengeance.

“ Backblocks In reply to “ Centralian ”

“ Burragorang” on aboriginal mourning:—
I remember, when 8 years old, seeing a black gin, whose
eldest son had died, chop her head into the bone, With
an iron tomahawk, in six or eight places, so that the
blood ran down in rivulets, and then besmear her head
with a mixture of white pipeclay, matting the hair
together in six big tallow-candle-like divisions, ail the
time keeping up a weird wail. For a month afterwards
she went about in this ghastly headgear. In eases of
'STJESg
completely cover the head with a mass of plastic
pipeclay, which clay skull-cap was worn day and
night for a moon, and was then removed with much
pain and loss of hair. This mourning-cap was preserved,
and carried from place to place under cover; and to it
unquestionably attached a deep and undehned sanctity.
It was the holy of holies-something altogether too
mysterious and sacred to be spoken of, much less defiled
by touch. In case of tribal war, to obtain this holy skull-
cap was the enemy’s ambition, and to defile this sacred
emblem before the eyes of the vanquished was revenge
sublime to the dusky warriors of 60 .years ago. These
sacred skull-caps were very rare in N.S.W. ; and to-day
not one is to be he found in any Australian Museum. In
the British Museum (of course !) there is one from N.s.
Wales, and another is in the Berlin Museum. Can any
Bulletin reader let me know if any of these pipe-clay
mourning-caps can be met with in Australia? And, if
so, where ?

“Via”: “ C.R.” (B. 6/5/’99) errs when
lie says that youthful Q. murderer also slaughtered
Eugene Dubrocca (the French, not Austrian) re-
cluse. Aforesaid youth was 50 miles away the
day Dubrocca was murdered ; writer, 500yds.
Beyond reasonable doubt, Dubrocca s murderer
got away in a French vessel that sailed same
night, for Guam. Revenge was the motive ; the
victim had been dogged for years, and, through
fright suffered from intermittent aphasia at the
sight of any stranger. I had his disjointed his-
tory at first hand ; told the police, and gave trie
clue following day. Three days after they tried
to stop the ship. After the youthful Deeming
was hanged, every possible and impossible crime
in S.Q. was laid at his door by a bungling force.
Some of the police even now think he had a finger
in the Gatton pie.

station near Yuelba, died and was buried close to
his own hut. His wife and three daughters (aged
13 ) 15 a ,nd 17), having no other means of support,
decided t G finish the contract themselves. They
gged away at it till, between hard toil, sun-
S-*«»*,*•»»«-*•>»**■«*
is in the blood of the bushman and bushwoman,
they all went mad together. Then the eldest girl,
apparently the worst case of the lot, fled into the
bush. She W as found after several days, still
a jj ve but na ked, partly eaten by ants, and howl-
ing a wild a^nal the tl 4s, an ’ d she died
? oon a her the discovery. The body was taken
home to the hut where the other three demented
frameworks _ the mother and two sisters-were
doing their little howl at nothing in particular.
There is a most beautiful postscript to the daily-
aC£Jounts of this horror-a postscript
which, if it is quite true, ranks high as a
case of Thrift It is to the effect that there was
money due to the four insane wrecks on their ring-
barking contract, but the manager didn’t give it
to them because, in their deranged condition,
they didn’t know the value of cash. They might
have wasted in frivolity, or spent it on large,
garish feather-hats unsuited to their station. So
the f our lunatics just went on starving and ring-
barking—they were sane enough to ring-bark but
too mad to be allowed to spend money—till the
adventure of the nude sister who was partly eaten
by ants drew attention to them.

commandant, was about to leave for New
Zealand hi February to spend a six Weeks’
holiday, Colonel ISingham, officer command-
ing the artillery, took exception'to Colonel
Hoad, assistant adjutant-general, being ap-

that he was senior to that, officer. To pre-

fake orders from Colonel Hoad during his
(Sir Charles’s) absence. This action,’ how-
ever, was declared by the Minister of De-
fence ; to be a violation of the regulations,
.which' provide that leave of absendh foi‘
more than three weeks cannot be
granted without . the approval of the

THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, arid opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. 11. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems. *

Business, Robbery, etc.
In Mt. Gambier their lives a man who was once
young and ambitious and a butcher. When he set up for
himself his previous employers lay low for him. There
was a meat-famine in the town, but a drover was ex-
pected with 100 head of cattle, and the p.e’s conspired
with others to run up the bids when the beasts were
auctioned and “ let in ” the guileless youth for the lot at
a fancy price. But the g.y. got wind of it, rode a day’s
journey to meet the drover, and bought the mob off-
hand. Then he subsidised the man to take the cattle
into town and auction them on his (the youth’s) behalf in
the usual way. When the sale came on the conspirators
gaily ran the bidding up until their last bid was at least
twice the value of the stock. But the “chump,” who
had also been bidding recklessly for a time, would go no
further, and the take-downs found themselves in the
soup. No language could do justice to their feelings
when, upon going to the agent’s office to pay, they found
that the spoil wasn’t going to the drover but to their in-
tended victim.
* * * *
A N.S.W. man died recently, leaving a matter
of £2OO cash behind him, the bulk of it in the Savings
Bank, the rest in another bank. His brother, going
across from Melbourne to prove the estate and collect the
money, was advised to put this very simple job in the
hands of a solicitor. Result, two bills from the indus-
trious shark—two long documents setting forth three
dozen items, or so, and culminating in a grand total of 18
guineas to pay : 18 guineas for transferring the dead
man’s bank credits to his brother in Victoria. The
charges included such lines as Is. 6d. for parchment, but
the necessary tape was thrown in gratis.
• * * *
A new development in Sydney is the soi-discint
employer who advertises several clerkships as vacant, and
adds “ Enclose refs, and stamps for reply.” The detec-
tives might look the gentleman up.
* * * *
The clever American ! This is the saving
machinery: “Dictated D.8.J., typed F.S.” As D.B.J.
and F.S. are soulless 30-bob-per-week retainers, what
easier than to make each swear black is white and “cleri-
cal error” when the “ boss’s ” interest is involved ?
* * * *
Letter received by a Sydney firm : “ Dear Sirs—
I have just received yours of sth inst., to say that you
have received my letter saying that I had not received the
parcel, but I have since received it safe.”
* * • *
This should be about a seven years’ record in
going broke. In ’9l adjudicated insolvent; in ’93 com-
pounded with creditors; same year, adjudicated insol-
vent ; in ’97 compounded again ; ’9B, adjudicated in-
solvent. The hero, a breezy person who used to encourage
the belief that he was a baron at the least, is now described
merely as an agent; he used to talk airily of family
estates in foreign parts, too, but even that bubble'now is
pricked. The S.A. courts have been favoured with his
business lately ; but his troubles began elsewhere, years
ago.
* * » *
Melb. express delivery-vans now carry tail-
board terriers to snap at would-be parcel-thieves. A
swagger bank-clerk, running for Flinders-street station,
the other day dodged between a delivery-cart and a cab.
Suddenly a foxy nose shot out, grabbed the swell’s
Alpine hat, and retreated into the van. During process
of recovery from the driver, the bank-clerk lost his temper
and his train. '
* * * *
A certain Govt, department decided to dispose
of a quantity of second-hand building material, and in-
structions were accordingly given to have a sale effected,
terms cash. One “ lot ” was knocked down to a man
from a distant suburb, who dropped in to the auction too
late to hear the terms announced, and unprepared to
comply with them, as it happened. Not to get prompt
delivery of his purchase would mean considerable trouble
and some expense to him, and on representing this to the
auctioneer, to whom he was well and favorably known,
and promising to square up when next he came to town,
he was cheerfully allowed to take the things away. This
did not meet with' the approval of the representative
of Red Tape, who was in attendance for the occasion, and

lorty years ago Chilian flour played an im-
portant part in Sydney market. There was a scarcity of
°, nce > anf l several Chilian ships were expected. In
se days the captains of the ships were usually part
owners—with complete control of their cargoes. There
were little syndicates then—mostly of chapel men. It fell
ic on a. Sunday. A hymn was being sung when a young
man entered the church and, approaching one of the
aeacons, whispered something in his ear and then left.
Ihis deaoon went to the sitting of the other deacon-said
omething to him and both departed. The hymn was over
ana the minister had begun his sermon, when the two
deacons re-entered. One took his seat the other
marched up to the pulpit and handed a note to the
minister, who paused in his sermon to read it. Having
read it, the rev. gentleman gave a pleasing nod to the
deacon, who immediately left the building. The congre-
gation wondered, and it was not known for years what
cnat dote contained. It was revealed bv chance and was
something like this : “ Chilian vessel sighted. If I take a
steamer and run out to her I will be ahead of the pilot and
get her to dodge about till midnight, so as not to trade on
the .Lord s Day. Will then buy at best price and get
captain to sign contract. Give a nod and I’ll know that
as usual you will be good for one-third of the venture.”
And the rev. gentleman nodded.

nous foot-passengers were humping “bluey” to Berrigan

gammg a rise in the road they saw a number of picks and

m . l *® away. A brilliant idea then struck Paddy : “ Off
with yer coats an’ git to it!” he ordered, pointing to the

confidence in Patrick’s resourcefulness, obeyed without
questioning, and Paddy began strutting before them with

wares, and Patrick bought largely in clothes, pipes, and
tobacco and gave the hawker an order on the paymaster
ot the line. “Do your men want anything ? ” asked the

anything you want ! ” “ Are they O.K. ?” whispered the
‘‘Oh, yes,” said Patrick sagely ; “ they’re all

or lers on their wages in payment. That hawker and the

’lhe Commercial Would (London) writes re-
ln? Colonial Mutual Life Assurance Society :
We always confidently anticipate a good year from this
vigorously-conducted Mutual Society. The circumstance,
therefore, that the figures for 1897 show no falling away
from the steady and substantial progress which is inse-
parably connected with the Society’s past career occasions
us no surprise. As a matter of fact, the Society earns its
success by deserving it, and, that much being conceded,
the best possible has been said for the continuance of the
large measure of public support which it has hitherto
fallen to its fortunate lot to receive.” Pleasant reading.*

dropped suddenly and emphatically, and fat

“F.C.” on the troubles of certain bank-
olerks :
. Half-an-hour in a suburban branch of Melb. Sav-
ings Bank the other morning much enlightened me
as to why clerks in such establishments are worried-
looking and prematurely grey and bald. A perspiring
middle-aged woman, with three small youngsters, bustled
in. One infant immediately climbed on to the only
vacant chair, another “occupied” the small writing
table, while the third was planted by his mother on the
counter. Said mother next pounced on a towel supposed
to tig for the exclusive use of the clerks, and having
therewith wiped her own placid brow, proceeded to
remove the outer stratum of dirt from the hands of the
counter-seated infant. After having a wipe at the other
two offspring, she blandly enquired of the cashier—
“ You haven’t a brush and comb handy, have you
Mister?” “No,” answered that paralysed youno- man’
grimly ; “ it’s not usual for depositors to perform their
toilets in the bank.” Then he turned to another female
depositor, who, wishing to withdraw £3 10s., had carefully
filled up a cheque for £lOOO, following the exact wording
even to the name, of the sample posted up for general
instruction. The cashier sighed wearily. “My dear
madam, you don’t want to draw £lOOO. And you know
your name isn’t John Smith. Come now.” It would save
a lot of time if some of the customers could he taught the
meaning of the word “endorse,” and that it isn’t
necessary to cover the back of a cheque with address
occupation, and whether married or single. Curious
that the ordinary housewife should be so anxious to
proclaim that she is a married woman. With a sympa-
thetic or apparently-interested clerk women grow quite
confidential, telling him all about their goings and
comings and families, and sicknesses and deaths in said
families, even unto the third generation. An exception
however, is the real business woman, who runs some
small concern on her “ own,” or one who attends races
regularly (there are several in Melbourne living on that
game). These are closer than an oyster and as cute as
a monte-man. But they are few and far between.

The Bookfellow this month takes another
leap in size ; and the contents are more varied
than previously. There are articles on the French
poet, Baudelaire, and the Australian one, Mary
Foott, with portraits; a notice of artist Tom
Roberts, with portrait and reproductions of his
work ; the Quatrain forms the subject of numerous
quotations ; the Brownings’ love-letters and E. J.
Brady’s book of verses are reviewed at some
length; Victor Daley contributes a satire in
verse ; and there are numerous illustrations and
portraits. Prizes are offered for French transla-
tion and an English quatrain ; and altogether the
number is well worth its modest price of 3d. post
free from Bulletin office.

A Dread Foo.
In uncivilised countries if a man makes an
enemy he has to be constantly on the qui vive
night and day lest his foe come upon him un-
awares and kill him. In this enlightened land ot
ours the law looks out that such things do not occur.
There are some things, however, that neither
civilization nor the law can prevent. Even to-
day an inoffensive citizen may be set upon sud-
denly and almost without warning by some dread
disease. Influenza is especially prominent at this
time of the year, and it sets N upon its victims in an
alarmingly sudden manner. It is in reality an
aggravated form of cold, supplemented by a
general weakness of the whole system, and may be
cured by Bile Beans at a cost of but Is. lyd. per
box. These 'Beans can be obtained at all chemists,
or direct from the Australian depfit, 39 Pitt-street,
Sydney. Six large boxes for 6s. Ordinary colds,
it is well-known, are caused by the congestion of
blood, which, as a rule, is most prominent in the
head or throat. Influenza can be traced to the
same cause, and irregular or insufficient liver
action is in most cases responsible for both.
When the liver, through any derangement of the
digestive organism, ceases to act in a proper man-
ner, a certain amount of bile finds its way into
the blood, and at the same time the blood created
from food consumed is of a decidedly poor quality.
In this manner the blood becomes congested, the
circulation sluggish, and a cold results. The cir-
culation of the blood now being much slower than
usual, the entire system is affected. The nervous
organism becomes unstrung, the appetite poor,
and the rest disturbed. The body is extremely
susceptible to chills, and the slightest draught
causes a violent attack of coughing or sneezing.
Many so-called cures for influenza fail to get at
the root of the trouble, and only afford temporary
relief. A medicine that will keep the liver in
thorough and regular working order, and the
digestion right, will, without doubt, prove the
most effective preventative and cure for influenza
and colds, and is also a remedy for biliousness,
indigestion, costiveness, insomnia, debility, piles,
female complaints, headache, and all kindred ail-
ments. Keep a box of Bile Beans handy, and you
need have no fears of foes of this character.

A Pitt-street Sensation.
A REMARKABLE SHOOTING CASE AND
A PROMPT ARREST.
Considerable excitement was manifested in
Pitt-street just before noon to-day by the ap-
parently strange conduct of a portly old gentle-
man, who was observed to be careering along Pitt-
street, to the imminent danger of passers-by.
With hat in hand, he continued, followed by an
alarmed crowd, until his hurried progress was
arrested by the strong arm of the law, in the
person of Constable . When asked for an
explanation of his strange behavior, the old
geutleman confided to the constable that he had
been seized with shooting pains in the head and
body, and that he was merely hastening with all
speed to purchase a box of Bile Beans for Bilious-
ness, which would promptly arrest his ills.
Now, how many persons are there who have
been seized in a similar manner, and yet
have neglected to obtain that sure and safe
remedy, Bile Beans for Biliousness. The conse-
quence is they are almost constantly afflicted with
sick headaches, constipation, indigestion, piles,
and those almost countless complaints which
spring from a disordered liver. Between the liver
and the brain more sympathy exists than between
any two other organs of the human body. There-
fore, as a necessary consequence, when the liver
fails to effectually perform its natural functions,
the brain becomes a sufferer, sick headaches are
produced, numerous ills follow, and life practi-
cally becomes a burden. Man unconsciously asks
himself, “Howam I to rid myself of my misery?”
The answer is simple. Bile Beans for Biliousness
will assist the liver to perform its duties naturally
and thoroughly, and when this has been done,
goodbye to biliousness, headaches, piles, constipa-
tion, indigestion, sleeplessness, and such kindred
ills, proving how important arrests and punish-
ment sometimes come about.

The Girl Who Blushes.
Blushes are of several varieties. There’s the
modest blush, the angry flush, the joyful blush,
and the shameful blush. Girls blush on all sorts
of occasions. Some of them are continually
blushing, while others never even so much as
have a decent colour. The girl that blushes is
generally pretty young, usually between 14 and
20, and she is then passing through possibly the
most critical part of her career. The organs
that compose her womanhood are at that time
assuming their proper proportions, and beginning
to fulfil their various functions. During this
period great care must be taken as to the
general health of the individual._ Pages upon
pages have been written upon this subject, and
yet we see everywhere pale, pasty-complexioned
girls whose looks and languid actions show
plainly the weakness of their entire system. At
this critical period the digestive organs must be
kept in thorough working order, and no waste
matter must be allowed to remain in the system.
Probably three-fourths of the derangements of
the female organs, from which so many women
suffer, are caused by the clogging, through the
presence of waste matter, of the ducts, or pipes,
immediately surrounding or connecting with the
several organs. Bile Beans for Biliousness are
essentially a medicine for the regulating of the
whole digestive system ; they clear off all waste
and impure substances, invigorate the liver,
purify the blood, and enliven the circulation.
Acting in this manner, they keep the delicate
organs of the body free from detrimental outside
influences, and consequently minimise the danger
that besets a girl just budding into womanhood.
Many a girl’s whole life has been marred by in-
sufficient care and attention at this time, and
again we would emphasise the fact that for this
sort of work there is no specific nearly so efficient
as Bile Beans for Biliousness. Young girls and
their mothers should remember this.

Every Man for Himself.
The selfisnness of human nature is so marked
that the person who devotes his energies to the
promotion of the welfare of others is almost uni-
versally regarded as a crank. Men and women
need very little advice to help themselves to most
of the good things of the world, But, strange as
it may appear, they are surprisingly apathetic in
their efforts to procure the most valuable of all
human possessions—good health. Once this has
been lost a dire misfortune befalls the loser, and
lucky, indeed, is the individual who has it restored
to him. The best way, then, to provide against
the possibility of such a misfortune occurring is to
secure the proper and thorough discharge of the
natural functions of those organs which regulate
the health of the human body. The liver,
stomach, and kidneys are those organs. If
they are in an efficient state of working,
good health will be the natural result. But they
are liable to impairment and occasionally need
assistance. In such eases Bile Beans for Bilious-
ness have been proved over and over again to be
the best and most effective assistant obtainable.
Almost all human ailments arise from an impaired
digestive organism, and thousands of valuable
lives are yearly sacrificed thereby. But the bane
ful effects of any impairment of the digestive
system are removed by Bile Beans for Biliousness,
the greatest medical discovery of the nineteenth
century.

The wreck of the Lock Sloy, and the loss of
almost the entire ship’s company has attracted
attention to the dangers of sea-faring life. The
sufferings and privations endured by the few
survivors, as related by them since their
rescue, have evoked the sympathy of the
whole Australian public. It is matter for con-
gratulation that we have but few such terrible
disasters occurring on our coasts. But unfor-
tunately we have sufferings equally as acute, en-
dured by our landsmen every day, and notwith-
standing that Bile Beans for Biliousness are at
hand to relieve the great majority of those suffer-
ings, this valuable remedy is permitted by some
misguided sufferers to pass unnoticed. When the
liver, stomach and kidneys become deranged they
inflict the greatest misery, as those organs are the
main regulators of the health of the human body.
When those organs become impaired they give
rise to indigestion, constipation, and the almost
countless kindred ills which so frequently make
life a burthen. It is for ills such as these that
Bile Beans for Biliousness have been specially
prepared, and their efficacy has been proved in
thousands of cases in our midst. Bile Beans will
cure the most chronic cases of indigestion, consti-
pation, sick and nervous headaches, and the
numerous ailments which result from an impaired
digestive organism. They are sold by all
chemists and storekeepers, and the low price of
13Jd. per box should place them within the
reach of all. They are also an undoubted specific
for colds, influenza, dizziness, female weaknesses,
irregularities, bad breath, piles, loss of energy,
lack of physical tone, and that ired feeling. The
Australian Depot is at 39 Pitt-street, Sydney. If
a course of Bile Beans be taken by any sufferer
from the ills occasioned by a deranged digestive
organism they will restore him to health and re-
move the possibility of one more being added to
the many wrecks in our midst.
The Way to Win a Lady’s Love.
A good deal has been said lately in the woman’s
columns of this paper as to the best way to win
the affection of the gentler sex. An old, experi-
enced hand at the business says that the first
essential is good nature and good spirits, coupled
with a manly physique. Bile Beaus are the
specific to use to attain this end. They put the
digestive organs in perfect condition, aid the
liver, heart, lungs’ action, act as a tonic to the
whole system ; and for such ailments as piles,
costiveness, indigestion, sour stomach, debility,
and lack of energy and physical tone they will
prove an undoubted specific. Sold at Is. lid. per
box, or six large boxes for 6s., they are within the
reach of all. They are manufactured in one of
the best-fitted and equipped laboratories in the
world, at Detroit, Michigan, U.S.A., and the
fact that they are prescribed by the first physicians
in that country proves that they are a medicine of
no ordinary merit. Obtainable at all chemists, or
the Australian depot, 39 Pitt-street, Sydney.
Oarry a few beans in your vest pocket, and if you
feel out of sorts take one. They will put you
right.

Mr. Frank Senior, who has been chemist
to the N.S. Wales Governors for many years past,
has conferred a blessing upon the citizens of his
country by discovering a wonderful cure for head-
ache. The new remedy cures instantaneously, is
very palatable, and perfectly harmless to both
ladies and gentlemen. Moreover, it is perfectly
effectual, no matter what the cause of the head-
ache may be. This cure, the result of years of
experience, can be had in 4 sizes, by post 1/4, 2/6,
3/8, 4/8, in N.S. W. only. To all the other colo-
nies 1/8, 2/8, 3/8, 5/2. Beware of imitations. Only
address: Senior, chemist, 246 George-st., Sydney.*

POLITICAL
POINTS
The anti-Federal flag—Disunion Jack.
“Who fears to speak of Ninety-nine?”
Why, the S.M. Herald.
“ The Colonists’ League, in opposition to
the Secret Conference Bill, intends to conduct a
vigorous campaign in the Riverina district,” says
Sydney D.T. Lyne, no doubt, will go down and
curse the Bill before the Hume electors. Yah !
As to the Federal Bill discouraging appeal
to the Privy Council : is Australia competent to
govern and provide justice for herself, or is she a
country occupied by people so intellectually and
morally degraded as to be unfit to exercise the
privileges enjoyed by every free nation ?
No wonder Labor was beaten at Balonne.
Every available voter was dragged to light
by Storey’s numerous friends. Coaches, buggies,
and horses were distributed freely, and one man,
William Allen, M.L.C., actually travelled 600
miles to record his vote for Storey at Bonna
Vonna.
Chamberlain, interviewed recently re the
practicability of introducing American negroes
into Queensland for plantation work, replied :
“In view of the well-known attitude of the Aus-
tralian provinces on the question of colored labor it
was impossible to entertain the suggestion.” Well-
known attitudel Good —let us stick to that.
Melb. Mayor M‘Eacharn’s announcement
that he desired Assembly experience before
standing for the Council was a virtue made of
necessity. Mac would have started for Melb.
Province had not J. M. Davies had too strong a
following. Davies’s committee had got early to
work, and every Fatman' of note in Melb. was
pledged to him.
The Federalist party certainly has no
objection to N.S.W. civil servants publicly ex-
pressing themselves on the subject of Federation.
Hie bulk of the Service will vote for Federation.
Federal statesmen will be larger-minded and more
1 1 1 -*• ral bosses than provincial politicians—and a
v i y considerable percentage of the Service will
b< come Federal employes.
The Cabbage-tree mob got “a nasty one ”
when Brisbane Federal Assn, extended its invi-
tation to Deakin as well as Barton. The stock
phrases, “Our natural ally,” “No Federation
without Queensland,” “Wait for our Northern
friends,” had been so oft repeated that the Gee-
bungs had persuaded themselves that the interests
of Q. and N.S.W. were absolutely intertwined,
and that Q. regarded Sydney as the Alpha and
Omega of Australia.
Things have sadly changed since Tom,
Dick and Harry were enfranchisedr
Before that, three-fourths of Parlia-
mentary candidates were “ gentle-
men ”; nowadays, there is hardly
one of them in the list. In the late
S.A. list, the plain, unadulterated
“laborer” was more numerous;
that was the description of one
who has been newspapering for
long past. But he has his reward ;
he is in Parliament. And the
Hon. Ben. Rounsevell was, for
this occasion, a horny-handed
“ farmer ” under which
description, too, one or
more squatters masquer-
aded. King O’Malley,
by the way, was a
“ gentleman ” ; and see
what happened to him t

THE SUCCESS OF THE TRIP IS
ASSURED.
Wardill, Trumble and Darling have been elected
to the finance committee. We can’t help thinking,
nevertheless, that a mistake has been made in over-
looking Iredale's qualifications.
Premier Kingston postpones the opening
of S.A. Parliament until Thursday, June 22
two days after N.S.W. Federal Referendum.
The monotonous brick which Premier
Kingston’s Tory detractors aim at him is not that
he is less democratic than they are, but that he is
less democratic than he pretends to be. In reply,
he points out that he has estab-
lished the State Land Bank,
the graduated land-tax on
large estates, the absentee tax,
the system of resuming large
estates for closer settlement,
created a labor bureau and
a system of village settle-
ments, abolished “the at-
tachment of wages for
debt, passed an improved
Health Act, and intro-
duced the Referendum—
not by statute, for the
Council stood in the
way, but at least by
precedent. Also, he
would have passed an
Employers’ Liability
Act but for the Coun-
cil’s obstruction ; and
he promises to try his
hand at State Fire
Insurance and the
creation of Elective
Ministries, and, if pos-
sible, to absolutely stop
the sale of State Lands.
And the unprogressive Tory
still complains that Kingston
isn’t progressive enough.
Premier Reid has now his
third Vice-President of N.S.W.
Leg. Council: W. H. Suttor—
Graham—Hughes. By the way,
where the occupant of the office
is so often changed the S.M.
Herald should, in reporting
the official, give his name with
or without his office and not
“the Vice-President of the Leg.
Council ” said this or that. Seven
readers out of ten don’t know who the V.P. is.
Provincialists yell that the atrocious
Federal Bill permits the same men to be members
of the Federal and State Parliaments, and to
draw therefore double salaries, thus making the
trade of “the professional politician” doubly
lucrative. But no man can sit either in a State or
Federal House unless he is elected by his people,
and if the people find that the double arrange-
ment is a calamity (supposing any politician tries
it), they needn’t elect the same man to both Par-
liaments. The remedy is quite in their own hands.
The announcement that ex - Premier
MTlwraith has despatched a letter to Queens-
land advising electors there to vote against the
Commonwealth Bill strikes like a voice from the
dead—or the gaol. Thomas is still comparatively
a young man—64—and his half-dozen goes at
ministerial office placed him well in the front of
Q. public opinion, but the obstinate illness which
prevented him personally going to Brisbane to
rout the enemies who lately assailed him v/ill be
certain to damage the value of his postbag forget-
me-not.
When the Westralian nigger is indentured
to his kind boss, the squatter (the police cap-
ture him, and then the squatter J.P.
gives him the alternative of being inden-
tured quite of his own free will, or doing
an endless series of gaol sentences for
continuous vagrancy), he isn’t a slave.
This point can’t be too strongly insisted
on. He gets no pay, receives any
clothes or food his boss likes to give
him, is worked any hours his boss
pleases, is flogged occasionally (boss
occasionally fined £2 if the law hap-
pens to hear of the matter), and is
gaoled if he tries to run away. But
any disreputable, lying ruffian who
says the W.A. nigger is a slave
deserves to be held up to the
scorn of all honest nigger-
dr i vers.
There is much specu-
lation as to whether the
new S.A. Assembly will
regard the decision of the
people on Household Suf-
frage as final. Darling,
now practically Opposi-
tion leader, intimated his
intention last session of
voting for the measure if
the electors favored it;
and Homburg, another
prominent Tory M.P.,
when similarly asked,
said, “Most emphati-
cally, Yes.” The people
decided in its favor by a
majority of 15,000, and if
these Oppositionists stick
to their pledges there will be a big majority for
franchise reform in the new Parliament.
Ex-Agent-General Playford, just back to
S.A. Assembly after five years’ absence, first
entered Parliament in ’GB. He .has been twice
Premier, and has had an aggregate of 3550 days
in office. The Tories are most anxious to persuade
Playford to throw in his lot with them, but King-
ston, who is always faithful to his friends, cannot
believe that Tom will “ turn dog.”
S. A.- Assembly, as re-elected last month,
consists of 12 Labor members, five Ministers, and
a Whip, 14 other Govt, supporters, three Inde-
pendents (Glynn, Playford, and Paech), and 18
Oppositionists, who, however, decline to follow
Downer, except spasmodically. The Speaker
makes the 54 members complete.. The Ministry’s
prospects of continued life are even better than
after last triennial election.
Premier Seddon’s offer of a regiment of
Maoriland troops and a steamer for service in
Samoa (regiment contemptuously declined, but
steamer accepted without effusive gratitude) isn’t
quite on a par with the great Soudan fizzle, though it
lookslikeit. TheM.L. peopiehavelonghad ayearn-
ful hope that Britain will annex Samoa and hand
it over to Maoriland, and that the latter will
ultimately become the head of a great South Sea
confederation. So Digger Dick tried to get his
oar in with a view to business rather than to grovel.
But Britain reckoned there were already sufficient
complications in Samoa.
AUSTRALIAN LANGUAGE.
“ Ratty ole bloke that, Jimmy,
surges awful if yer just shot
a few rocks at ’im ”-.
Which, being translated, means:
“Eccentric person that,
James; becomes enraged if
stones art thrown at him.”
Westebn Mail (Perth) is oi
opinion that Westralia shouldn’t
federate because if it sends eleven
good men to the Commonwealth Par-
liament there won’t be enough left be-
hind to form a decent State Govt. Which deafen-
ing cry of “ stinking fish 5 ’ is rather a surprise.
It is a remarkably modest province which is
prepared to say that it can’t raise eleven moder-
ately intelligent politicians and still have one or
two left oyer.
Interesting item from Thursday Island
Forty Japs, landed here (May 8) by the steamer Yawatu
Maru, all being provided with passports. All the afternoon
the shipping-officer was busy signing them on to Japanese-
owned boats. The white pearlers are indignant that the
Japs, can indent lab'or while they are denied the privi-
lege. Complain, further, that they are unable to get
labor locally. . . . This item simply confirms a recent
Bulletin assertion that the Queensland treaty-restric-
tions on Jap. immigration apply to unindented labor only.
HOSPITAL SATURDAY: RIVALS!!
“ Better come ’ome , Bill; weain’t got no ’ope! ”
The weekly leper begins to make a hole in
Q. Treasury. Govt, arranged with the China co’s
boat, which was taking a cargo of lepers from
Sydney to pick two up from a Northern port.
Passage-money- paid in advance—,£2so ; with
.£lO for each man when he stepped ashore. When
the boat arrived, only one man was shipped, the
others having left unobtrusively for parts un-
known. The passage-money was, of course, for-
feited.
The most specious Geebung argument is—
“ Defeat this Bill and get a better.” Better in
which way? More democratic in order to secure
the support of tho Leg. Council, or more reaction-
ary to get the Laborists on its side ? The amend-
ment must tend one way or the other. Shall we
let the two sections pull the structure to pieces in
order to re-build it, when each of them will seek
to do so in a way which, in the eyes of the other,
will make it tenfold worse ?
Ex-Treasurer W. M'Millan, on returning
from England, paid back into N.S.W. Treasury
tlie Parliamentary salary which had accrued in
his absence. This is worthy of remembrance at a
tune when nominally holding an office is con-
sidered the same as performing the duties of it.
vl ill, for instance, ex-Attorney-General Want pay
back the money which accrued as salary while he
was so far away from N.S.W. as to confessedly not
know anything about its greatest topic of politi-
cal discussion ?
Queensland enthusiasm for Federation is
on the rise in the bakbiox. The Western men, not
oaring a rap for the supremacy of Brisbane,
Rockhampton or Townsville, and regarding these
cities very often as parasites on bakbiox industry,
are not particularly concerned to see their im-
portance (real or fancied) slightly diminished by
the Federal movement. The affection of Q. bush-
men for the coast towns is a very slender thread
indeed. They have much more Australian feeling
about them than provincial.
“Is it likely," asks John Haynes, “that
the State Parliaments will reduce their numbers'?
Do politicians,’' lie triumphantly continues, “com-
mit suicide ? ” What will happen, John, ’is that
some politician sure of his own seat will, on the
establishment of the Federal Parliament, or
before, move a reduction in the members of the
State Parliament, and members who vote against
that motion will be kicked out hy the people at
the next election. Politicians don’t commit sui-
cide, but cultivate horse-sense.
After all the provincialists’ clamour about
the baseness of the N.S.W. Public Service Board
ln “gagging the civil servants ” so that they can’t
speak out their sentiments against the horrid Bill,
it is interesting to read the Board’s announcement
that it hasn’t issued any ukase or circular, or
moved in the matter at all. The Civil Service Act
prohibits civil servants publicly interfering in
politics, and has always done so ; therefore it pro-
hibits them delivering speeches either for or
against Federation. It did the same thing last
year and the year before. So in the main the tale
is only the usual parochial whopper.
The voice of Pile’s dog in S.M. Hkkau>
on the downfall of Sydney through the Federal
capital being fixed somewhere else :
What will happen in Sydney is what actually did hap-
pen in Auckland when the seat of government was re-
moved to Wellington, only on a larger scale. Auckland
has scarcely yet recovered from that blow.
And poor Auckland got such an awful whack
from that blow that it grew from a very small
place to a city of 57,616 people—actually 16,000
more than the bloated capital of Wellington—and
is now far the biggest place in Maoriland, while
the stupendous metropolis comes fourth. And the
same thing is going to happen to Sydney. Let us
weep !
In many S.A. suburban constituencies not
only are there more females than males on the
Assembly electoral-rolls, but the woman vote is
actually the stronger. At Alberton, for
instance, outside Port Adelaide, there
are 590 females and 295 males enrolled ;
the vote t,be other day was 373 females
and 203 males. And in Lefevre Penin-
sula, adjoining, of 1567 females 865
voted, and of 1048 males 645 voted.
Unfortunately, these separate returns
are kept in only a very few districts; but
those that are kept quite knock out the
ancient theory that women don’t want to
vote and wouldn’t iftliey could.

Victorian Labor Day meeting was remark-
able for nothing save that most of the speakers—
Sangster, Hancock and others—preached—albeit
unwittingly straight-out Socialism. Sangster
drew a striking parallel between the present State
of Melbourne and that of Maoriland in ’BO, attri-
buting the latter’s present prosperity to the legis-
lation of Eallance and Sedclon. The Age report
of the meeting was full of covert sneers and
underhand innuendoes. The proposed nationalis-
ation of land, tramways, and other means of dis-
tribution is gall and wormwood to David Syme.

THE UNDERMENTIONED BANKS have
agreed to charge, Half-yearly, a fee of Five
Shillings on all current accounts without excep-
tion whether debtor or creditor.
The first charge will be made on the 30th June

With an ordinary tape measure determine the width
round the joints AA, round the instep 88, round heel CO,
round the ankle DD ; next ascertain the length and width
of tread by placing the foot on a sheet of paper, and
running a lead pencil round the heel and toes. Cut the
outline thus made with a scissors, and enclose with the
other measurements, stating what size you generally take,
what are your peculiarities—a weak ankle, perhaps a pet
corn, ingrowing nails, or a blooming bunion. All these
little matters attended to. If you have a long thin foot, nr
a short thick one, with very high instep, send to ELLIS’S,"
where you are sure to get" a tit. The proprietor, with a
view of introducing his new and simple system into the
country districts where practical bootmaking is unknown,
has decided to supply his patrons at the lowest cash price.
POST FREE to any part of Australia or N.Z.

straight away with ease and comfort.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Hip Australian Eleven has got fairly to work
now ; and the results of the first three matches are mixed
cloud and sunshine. The first match, against South of
England, was the most fairly contested; since the
weather favored neither side. From t hat our hoys oame
out with 87 runs to get to win and 9 wickets to fall—an
easy victory in prospect. Scores :S. of England, 246 :
Australia, 375 ; S. of England, 222-and the time-limit
drew the match.
Second match, against Essex and the Weather
saw an Australian downfall. The wicket was bad all
through, and in the fourth (Australian) innings it wag
rotten. Essex went in first and made 199; Australia.
144 ; Essex again, 144; and Australia, all to pieces, 73
beaten by 126 runs.
Third match, against Surrey, made amends and
put lid I Howell on the pinnacle of fame. Surrey, lucky
wit l> the toss went in on a drying wicket and scored 114 ;
the only William taking all the wickets. When this
paper went to press on Tuesday, four Australian wickets
la 'J „! f, n ”: r 122, ant * hopes were rosiest. Gregory’s 36
and Hill s 29 were then the best Australian scores on the
Doard.
* • • *
Tfckvvood, who went in ninth man for South of
England v. Australia, managed to make 42 runs without
losing his wicket—a useful start for his batting average
Graces management looks rather stupidly Conservative
in this case. Surely (he man who had hit 41 (not out) in
the first innings should have been given a chance to score
next time.
* * * *
Herald on the result of the South of
England match : “It may be pointed out as being re-
markable that the Englishmen totalled 246 and 222, and
the Australians 375 and one for 7, even and odd numbers
respectively, and that the figure 9, which does not occur
above, represents the number of wickets that the Aus-
tralians had in hand.” This is a sane paper’s criticism in
its leading column ! What has the figure 1 done not to be
mentioned when it represented the number of wickets
lost. Also 0 does not appear in any of the four totals,
although it does in Noble, the best score, and Jones, the
best bowler. ’
