The Melb. football season opened on Saturday
by Fitzroy unexpectedly beating the much - vaunted
E3sendon by 47 points to 9—the same old club affected
b\ all that portion of Melb. which says “ haw” being 1 un-
able t 6 score a goal. A still greater boil-over was the
victory of Geelong over Collingwood—s4 to 27. St.
Kilda, which has not won a match for two years, main-
tained its average steadily, opening the season with a
defeat by Melb.—ll6 points to 23. S. Melb. easily beat
Carlton, Ports beat Richmond, and the new club of
Prahran lost its first match as a senior organisation
against Footscray.
* * . »
Friday night’s rain gave Sydney footballers a
slippery time on Saturday. Marrickville beat University
by 19 points to 3 ; the ex-juniors, as they play themselves
into condition as the season advances, must prove a hard
nut for the best to crack. Marrickville scored 19 points
to nil in the first half, fairly outclassing University until
their condition seemed to fail them in the second half,
and University scored 3 to 0. Pirates ran over Buc-
caneers with 22 points to nil. Randwick v. Wallaroo was
the best-contested game of the day, Randwick winning
by 6 points to s—hard luck for Wallaroo, who, if any-
thing, played the better game. The match of least interest
was between Sydney and Parramatta, the former winning
by 10 points to nil.
* » • *
The Celtic Park, Glasgow, is perhaps the finest
football-ground in the world, and next season it will
accommodate 100,000 spectators, all of whom will be able
to distinctly see the play. The club’s income last year
was €13,200, a record for Great Britain, if not the world.
On Ist Jan., ’9B, in an ordinary match with a sixpenny
admission, the takings at gates were £1470 13s. 6d.
* * * *
S.T.C. Birthday Meeting begins at Randwick
to day (Saturday). The Hurdles looks well for Ladylike
and The Barber, whilst Chesterina or Glenroy should
account for Amateur Stakes, Eastern King, Stand Off,
Dozon and Cornquist appear to have good chances in
Royal S'akes, and of these perhaps the last-named will
prove winner—though taking a line by Auld Lang Syne’s
recent performances, that filly may have a say. Of the
five entered for Steeplechase, Whitefoot 11. should be
easily first; and Loch Long or Miss West may take the
T.C. Handicap. Sweep considerations will doubtless
conduce to a large field for Birthday Cup, in which
Eastern King, Crosslake, Kallatina and Vocalist seem
especially well treated. Crosslake may win, but people
should keep an eye on April Fool.
* * • *
Strange that the most bitter opponent of Ikey
Mo in Sydney should unwittingly be his best friend.
Chipping Norton stable has provided more “turns-up”
or “skinners” for the “books” than any other half-
dozen put together—Aoa of Diamonds, last Saturday,
was the latest. Wonder if Mr. Bill Long does this
intentionally? Is he softening and endeavoring to put
the wherewithal towards a competency in the way of his
natural enemy before sending him to fresh fields per
medium of the long-threatened tote?
* • * •
“ Mr. Barnes, of New York,” is the books’ cog-
nomen for a certain Yankee punter who lias lately been
having a bin run of luck “ backing ’em " at N.S.W. race-
meetings. A slout, red-faced little chap in a felt hat, he
first showed at the recent Wagga meeting, at which he
won t2OO ; then took a hurried trip to Sydney and helped
himself to £5OO at Rosehill, and got back to Narandera in
time to gather in £l5O or thereabouts at the local meet.
That Mr. B. knows something of the game, he proved at
Narandera, where he took all available money about a
certain good thing some time before the raoe, and then,
when said good thing looked rocky, laid the jock £5O to
nothing, and collected something like £3OO from the
Hebrews.
• • * •
One of the most remarkable little bits of horse-
flesh ever seen in N.S.W. is the country horse Dunbar, a
pony by Dunlop, who has lately been carrying all before
him at Riverine meetings. He is a common-looking little
chap, with a tueked-up appearance and shaky-looking
legs, and suffers from perpetual soreness. To see him
painfully stumbling out to the post you’d swear he
couldn't gallop a yard, and probably call his owner names
for letting him start; but all soreness disappears when he
starts to gallop, and he carries thumping weights home
in splendid time, and then limps back to the paddock.
Dunbar won the chief race at Gundagai recently after
breaking out of his box and spending the night in a
lucerne-paddook, and has carried big loads to the front
at Wagga, Narandera (a double) and Hay within the last
couple of weeks. He will probably soon be seen in Syd-
ney. Had he been brought to the city a few months ago
a small fortune could have been won on him.
* • * »
Dear Bulletin.—A par. in a recent issue took
credit for “ an old-time Bulletin prediction that a pony
would yet win one of the big Australian handicaps,’’ but
the writer forgot to remark that Diffidence, the pony in
question, carried a very lightweight. The victory was
not much of an argument in favor of pony— i.e., under-
sized horse —racing as a sport. By the way, your par.
stated that Diffidence was the daughter of Etra-weenie
and the grand-daughter of “ Nellie, who gave Trenton to
the turf,” which would he a most immoral pedigree.
Etra-weenie was begotten by Trenton—Nellie. —Swjd
Book.
* * * •
Wonderful what a change of stable does fora
horse sometimes. Forest, bought as a two-year-old by
Uphill, and leased by him to S. Miller, was a rank failure
in the red-and-white—couldn’t win a trial stakes. On
expiry of the lease, Uphill handed the horse to Lynch,
who has won seven races in as many months, including
the Newmarket, the Hill Handicap, and now the Good-
wood (under 9.3). Lynch, a plucky bettor, was pretty
hard-up till he got Forest. In Jan. last, when Forest
started his winning account at Maribyrnong, Lynch told
writer that he had emptied out on his horse—to the
extent of t'3. Now he has bought a pretty villa (grate-
fully labelled “ Forest Lodge”) and is reported to have
a few thousands “ socked.”
* * * »
In the Elder Stakes at Morphettville (S.A.),
Contrast, in a field of only ten, paid a div. of £36. Her
owner, Mr. J. B. Pearson, had £2O on her. What chance
would he have had of getting £7OO to £2O off Ikey Mo ?
In Adelaide Cup, also won by Contrast, the tote paid
£3 16s. Od. or a shade over 5 to 2 ; while in Melbourne the
books were laying 2to 1. If you ask J. B. P.’s opinion,
he would say, “The tote, the only tote, and nothing but
the tote.”
• * * *
Bill Lang, the old Vic. racing identity, dropped
dead in a blacksmith’s shop last week. Lang landed in
Melb. from Tasmania 62 years back, and one of the
traditions of his career as a jockey dates back to the 40’s.
He is said to have ridden Merino to victory in a 3-mile
race at Flemington when the saddle had turned round.
As a trainer and rider Lang is credited in the Calendar
with Oriflamme’s captures of the V.R.C. Derby and Leger
in ’63. Seven years later he trained Nimblefoot—the
horse that owner Craig, of Ballarat, dreamed about, ere
his death—for the Melb. Cup, and in ’7l Lang himself
steered Nimblefoot home in the Australian Cup. Of late
years the old man was chiefly noted for sensational dis-
appointments with Pigeontoe and Rosella. Pigeontoe
was, undoubtedly, the most deformed horse that ever
won a good race. After taking a Hotham Handicap he
started favorite for Melb. Cup.
* * * *
Merloolas, the big-hearted,long-striding Queens-
lander, has at last been bought for export, and Sydney
loses the nearest approach to a really consistent cham-
pion it has seen for years. Amongst the weedy sprinters
and weak-hearted stayers of these days it is refreshing to
remember at least one horse who could go out with a big
weight up and finish after a gruelling 2 or 3 miles as
straight and game as a Carbine—beating better-trained
and faster-ridden animals by sheer pluck. Merloolas has
got nearer to the hearts of the N.S.W. sporting public
than any horse since “ Old Jack,” and many will be glad
to hear of the game old fellow often getting his well-
known “ bawly ” face in front in India.
* • • m
Multiform, the M.L. champion, now bound for
England, has started in 18 races, winning 16 and running
3 seconds—each time to a stable companion. Merloolas
also has a good record : 24 starts, 11 wins, 7 seoonds, 3
thirds.
• • • •
At Ashburton (M.L.) Harold B. won a mile-
and-a-half trotting handicap from scratch in 3min. 47sec.
Harold 8., well-known this side, trots more like Fritz
than any other horse in Australia, and but for a bad
temper would have proved himself a champion.
* * • •
Trotting stands no chance of taking hold in
N.S.W. whilst the handicapping is so haphazard. At
Hawkesbur.v Show the two-miles trot was taken in a walk
by Seldom Fed, a pony which had won more than once
before. She was placed on 30sec. behind, whilst a mare
that had never previously started in a race was put on
BOsec. behind, and there were other instances just as
glaring. The mistake made by the country men is in not
engaging a man well up in the game to prepare their han-
dicaps. The recent Dubbo Trotting Club imposts were
furnished by Harry Knight with the result that on-
lookers got the novel experience of a cleverly-contested
finish in a trotting-race.

Elying Fox, the Two Thousand Guineas win-
ner, is now an odds-on favorite for English Derby.
Captain’s Flat miner Dan O’Connell and Sydney
footballer Jack Ooilon had their much-heralded match
at Alhambra A. 0., Wednesday night, last week 1200
sports fl"ed the hall. Burs of money for O’Connell found
few takers, and then only at 6 to 4or 2 to 1 on. Four-
and-a-half-inmutes of actual fighting-time saw one man
(the miner) limp, helpless, and beaten, and the other (the
footballer) fresh and vigorous, as at the start. Hostilities
had barely commenced before they were over, for O'Con*
neH was settled in the first round, and came out
KvJl 18 . 00rner dazed. Such an unaccountable col-
lapse in so hot a pot has rarely been seen.
O Connell seemed paralysed from the call of time, and
foujfht wildly at the outset; “ stage-fright ” possibly had
a deal to do with his downfall, as it was his first public
%ht in Sydney, and he had never previously stood
before such a crowd The country pug. has found'backers
101 another go with his metropolitan adversary for half-a-
century aside. Three years back, O’Connell fought a pri-
vate match with Jack Dawson, at Seale’s Gymnasium
(Sydney), and such good judges of the game as George
Seale, Jim Barron, Tom Taylor, Jack Fuller, and many
others who witnessed that battle pronounced O’Connell a
tighter or the first order—hence the weight of money
behind him. That there was something wrong on
Wednesday week is shown by the readiness of the miner’s
supporters to risk £5O more. Writer saw O’Connell fight
the matoh with Dawson already referred to, and has no
doubt that he can do much better than his latest show-
ing.
Conlon, who licked Dan O’Connell in such short
order last vveek, promises to prove a lion in the path of
those aspiring to the middleweight championship of Aus-
tralasia. He possesses all the necessary attributes of a
fighter—height, reach, strength, youth, and that
essential, the fighting instinct, which must be inbred—it
can never be acquired. Conlon shows improvement every
time he fights. With O’Connell he refrained altogether
from sprinting round the ring, and went in and made the
best use of his extraordinary hitting powers. A match
between him and Mick Dunn is talked about ; if it hap-
pens Dunn will probably win, but he’ll have his work cut
out.
* * * *
Five couples hanged away at each other right
lustily in first round of Alhambra (Sydney) A.O.’s 87
Novice Tournament on Saturday night. All' evinced the
greatest gusto for their work, while 500 or 600 people
looked on and enjoyed the fun. Total number of persons
patronising both boxing shows same evening must have
topped 1500. Where do they all come from?
* * * *
. Featherweights Tom Hope and Tom Essler
(“ the Kaffir”) came together a third time at Golden Gate
A.C. (Sydney) last Saturday night, with the same result
—the black won, but not so quickly, as only 2min. of the
full journey (20 rounds) remained when Hope was made
oblivious per medium of two well-timed rights in the
region of the “ point.” The colored lad fought coolly
and fairly, while the white frequently lost control of him-
self and went close to fouling. Seemed that Essler in-
dulged in the cat-and-mouse business for a long time, as
he appeared capable of putting the “comether” on at
any stage after the ninth round, which was very fiercely
fought. A packed, orderly crowd witnessed the battle.
* * * *
‘‘Old Timer’’again: Referee at Golden Gate
A.C. (Sydney) still “ cautions ” fighters after the first foul
no matter how serious it may be—which, of course,
means that every combatant at that particular place has
a license to foul his adversary once—even to the extent of
doubling him up and actually disabling him. Witnessed
another glaring infringement of M.Q. rules at same place
on Saturday night. Twice at critical periods, while fight-
ing was hot, Tom Hope’s glove-strings became undone.
He was allowed to retire to his corner and sit down while
the strings were re-tied by seconds who got into the ring,
fanned, wiped and rubbed their principal down—all this
during the course of the round 1
* * * *
“C. v : Saw the latest featherweight-champion
boxer of N.S.W.—Essler (“The Kaffir”) —at work on
Saturday night. Shades of Griffo and Billy Murphy (who,
though not dead in the fl ab, are to the world). What a
man to be a champion 1 Decent enough, game enough,
and strong enough he is, no doubt, but clever enough—
oh 1 Why, any greenhorn well up in the rudiments
(“ stop ” and “ counter ”) of the game, would soon settle
“The Kaffir,” who leaves himself open from the head
down every time he leads with the left. What a pepper-
ing Griffo would have given him—and what a peppering
he will assuredly get from the first skilful man he meets.
• * * *
Dear Bulletin.—ln the matter of gray horses :
They may not be a success as racers, but the grays are
the solid workers, the hardy long-journey hacks, the
game, plodding stayers when the brumby mob’s afoot;
and out on the Western rivers, when the wool comes
rocking in by the ten-ton loads, it’s all Bourke to a bridle
that the best horse in the team is the worn old gray with
the scalded shoulders and the ragged hips. Any horse-
man will tell you of the confidence with which he gets
across a flea-bitten gray—the redder the flecks on him
the better he is. Grays for me, good luck to ’em ! —Will
H. Ooilvie.
* * * *
Nine of the “ talent ” were roped-in at Auck-
land (M.L.) lately for playing an illegal game—“ two-up,”
the orthodox “kip” (piece of wood) and two coppers
being used. “Kip” was produced in evidence, but the
beak discharged the surprised crowd on the ground that
a piece of wood wasn’t a gambling instrument?
» * * *
“Saved ” : “ Has one aspect of the Sydney Safe
Deposit occurred to you? Most people insure against
loss by fire and receive money compensation for property
destroyed. But there are some things—valuable papers,
heirlooms, etc.—which can never be replaced, and the
loss of which means a fortune. The Safe Deposit, for a
comparatively small sum, not only insures against the
loss of these things, it saves the articles themselves.
What is a guinea or two a year compared with a docu-
ment upon which thousands depend?” Safe Deposit is
in Ash-street, back of Palings, off George-street.*
The cure of piles by the new “Canalin”
treatment is fully described in the pamphlet,
“Piles, and How to Cure Them,” by Albert
Sharp. Copies, with testimonials, sent free to
any colony from the dep6t, 257 George-street,
Sydney.*
They threaten to call him Lord Bigchump.
The other morning Brisbane Courier
actually counselled the Leperland sugar-growers
to support the Commonwealth Bill on the ground
that their interests in the devastating kanaka and
the crawlsome Chow cook would be safer in the
hands of a Federal Parliament than with the
local Legislature. This looks like somebody’s
little device for scaring the democratic vote away
from k ederation. Queensland’s best chance to get
nd of its leper lies in the fact that the Democratic
Federal Parliament will have control of alien legis-
lation.
Hackett, of the West Australian, after
starting as an ardent federationist, has somer-
saulted, and now leads the forlorn hope of the old
Sandgroper-party. His partner, Charles Harper,
is a large landowner and fruit-grower. Hackett’s
latest:—
It is quite impossible to conceal the fact that a large
number of people believe with dread that in the event
of the Bill going at once to the people a big vote would
be cast in the interests, not of Western Australia, or even
of Australia itself, but of one or more of the sister
colonies.
Apples are selling at Is. per lb. in Perth shops,
but still the Sandgropers want more protection
against the Eastern growers, d n them !
Barton, and Deakin last week received a
splendid reception from the people of Brisbane.
The biggest hall in the province was densely
packed long before the business began, and clusters
of enthusiasts, unable to obtain admission, hung
on to the masonry round the lofty windows, and
the ventilators in the roof were plugged with ears.
Nothing like it has been seen in Brisbane for
years, and the speeches of the principal speakers
were fully up to the occasion and roused the
greatest enthusiasm. A show of hands proclaimed
unanimous approval of the Commonwealth Bill,
and the meetingdispersed with hurricane cheers for
‘ Barton,” “ Deakin,” and “Australia.” Federal
feeling, so long latent in Q., is bursting out with
great intensity.
Another Australian claimant to a big
British property is W. R. Bickell, of Lindfield,
Sydney, who reaches out for about half of the vast
Westminster possessions. He traces his descent
from a family which became connected by mar-
riage with the Grosvenors, and which had an
estate which it is alleged became merged in the
Westminster possessions in such a way as to make
Bicknell virtually co-heir with the present
Viscount Belgrave. If there’s anything in this—
what a carcase for the legal vultures ! But the
claimant who, like Bicknell, has to establish a
title from 119 years back, has a hard row to hoe,
and official registers were not then what they are
to-day.

The Bulletin last night dreamt that it
looked in at an anti-Federal dinner. Dibbs was
presiding, with Rosa on his right and Holman as
vice-president. In places of honor were, among
others, Pilcher, Kohen, M'Laurin, Haynes, Knox,
and Brolly Hughes. . Dibbs, raising his glass
and winking at Piloher, exclaimed —“ Here’s to
Anarchy, Socialism and Levelling Down ! ” The
sentiment was received. with great enthusiasm,
whereupon Rosa, winking at his crowd, proposed
“ Here’s to Sydney, the Federal City, and Higher
Rents ; also to Disunion and Cheap Sugar from
Fiji! ” This latter toast moved Knox to tears,
And Pilcher became so violently hysterical that
The Bulletin woke up.
Three years ago (thp event is reported in
the Sydney dailies, 29/l/ ? 96) the N.S.W. Political
Labor League resolved, among other things, that
in elections for the Federal Senate “ each colony
should vote as one constituency.” Hughes and
Watson, M’s.L.A., were among the supporters of
the idea, and it was carried with enthusiasm —
Watson, M.L.A., declaring that the resolution
“marked a clear line” between the genuine
Labor-party and the sham Democrats then in
office. Now, the principle that the Labor League
demanded on democratic grounds is in the Federal
Constitution, and the loudest guns of the party
(Holman and Brolly Hughes) go round denouncing
it as a soul-blasting engine of oppression and a
scheme by which only the richest variety of Fat
Man will get into the Federal Senate. Yah !
A year or so ago Premier Reid, being then
in the anti-Federal camp, appointed a finance
committee of two bitter Provincialists (Messrs.
MacLaurin and French—both now appear on the
anti-Federal platform) and one Federalist (Bruce
Smith) to inquire into things, so as to be sure of
getting a majority report against the Bill. The
two bitter Provincialists started the famous
twenty-two-and-sixpenny bosh, Bruce Smith dis-
senting in a very loud postscript. The point at
issue now is this: When Dr. MacLaurin and the
Telegraph and the Sunday rag quote the 225. 6d.
lie as the finding of the committee, they only tell
part of the truth—it was a finding of part of the
committee only. And when Dr. MacLaurin
refers to the committee (his own committee) as an
authority, he simply olaims that the 225. 6d. bosh
which MacLaurin tells now must be correct
because MacLaurin (as a committee) told it before.
John Norton, objecting to hired toughs
being ejected from a publio meeting which they
were disturbing
No Constitution, Federal or otherwise, that cannot
command sufficient public confidence and support to
carry it through without the aid of policemen, is worthy
of a moment's consideration at the hands of a presumably
free people.
Also : No well-stocked jeweller’s shop which can-
not command sufficient confidence and support to
carry it through without police protection is
worthy of a moment’s consideration, etc. Like-
wise, no presumably free people which cannot do
without policemen is worthy, etc. Further, any
free people which won’t allow itself to be bossed
and gagged by toughs is worthy, etc. The
question is, after all, very simple : Does freedom !
at public meetings mean the freedom of 1000 men
to hear the speakers, or the freedom of, say, 10
ruffians to say that the other 990 shan’t hear ? In
other words, does it mean freedom to curse and
yell and kick, or freedom from the coercion of the
yellers and kickers ?

The two-headed paper will now go out to get both
its hair cut.
It is claimed by the anti-Federal Party
that there will be “ almost a block-vote of N.S.W. 1
Civil Service against the Bill.” If the Service
has the faintest idea when to get in out of the
damp there will be nothing of the kind. Federa-
tion means that the postal and telegraph officials,
those of the statistician’s office, Observatory and
Mint, a good part of the Treasury crowd, and, pro-
bably, a little later on, the great Education
Department, will be removed from the control of
the Public Service Board. And the Service
somehow doesn’t like its Board. Most of it would
gladly tie 24 volumes of Encyclopedia Britan-
Nica round the Board’s neok and drop it over
South Head.
Extract from a girl’s letter from London :
How do you like the idea of Lord Beauchamp as
Governor for Sydney? Everyone here was so surprised
at his appointment, as he is so young. There are
whispers of an affair with one of his mother’s maids,
whom he is said to be educating on the Continent as the
future Lady B. But that is rather hard to believe, as he
has the reputation of having—to put it mildly— very
select ideas. One of his fads is an autograph-book which
appears to be kept exclusively for the names of duchesses
and ambassadors’wives. A little while ago he was stay-
ing in Ireland with Lord , and some girls who were
in the house found the precious book and, uninvited,
wrote their names there, with all kinds of nonsense-
verses, such as “Hi diddle diddle,” and “Dr. Foster
went to Gloster.” I believe His Lordship’s indignation
was terrible to behold.

The vituperation poured on the N.S.W. !
Freetrade Ministry by Sydney Daily Telegraph
is nearly paralleled by the paper’s adulation of
Dr. M'Laurin. The Telegraph, which aspires to
king-making, has for its present idols M'Laurin,
Heydon, Dibbs, Lyne, and Pilcher, the back-
bone of the Conservative section of the Protec- j
tionist party whom, at last general election, it
professed so utterly to abhor. If the Bill is de-
feated* N.S. Wales will be faced with another
wearying fiscal fight, and the Telegraph will
then have to battle for the discredited Reid
against its Mte noir —a powerful Protectionist
party.
On© famous phas© of the Dean case finds
points of resemblance in the 0 Callaghan (Melb.)
inquest—an inquiry which has already been ad-
journed several times for the purpose of discovering
who is the father of the deceased infant. An
agreement was drawn up during the child’s life
whereby it was to be adopted for a sum of £.)O,
one Rigby, a solicitor, acting in the matter for
the father. The latter’s name was demanded by
the coroner when Mr. Rigby entered the box, but
the lawyer pleaded privilege, and the inquest has
been many times adjourned to decide whether
Rigby is or is not bound to divulge the name of
his client. The whole of legal Melb. has been
hunting up the question, and, strange to say, not
one absolute ruling on the subject has been dis-
covered. The nearest applies to an instance
where a solicitor was held to be entitled to with-
hold his client’s address, but then only condition-
ally on there being no crime in the case and pro-
vided the address was given to him as part of
a professional confidence. The average man, who
fondly believes that his lawyer is entitled to keep
everything he knows up his sleeve, will be sur-
prised to know that the authorities rule that a
solicitor, knowing his client’s name or address
from a friend, or from living next door to him, or
from a casual remark, may be compelled to divulge
the same, and that a name oraddres3 is privileged
only when revealed to the lawyer in a definitely
professional way. As to whether Rigby is privi-
leged in the present case, the ruling will probably
be in his favor, the opposition made by the Crown I
Law Department being but half-hearted.

THE ILLUSTRATED LONDON NEWS of
Sept. 28, 1895, says:
“ If I were asked which single medicine I should
prefer to take abroad with me, as likely to be
most generally useful, to the exclusion of all
others, I should say Chlorodyne. I never travel

Demoorat Jones, a miner in a S.Q, town,
found himself boycotted by local mine-owners on
aooount of his political proclivities. Traced origin
of aforesaid boycotting to Goldbug-Gubbins, to
whom Jones, in public, administered a dose of
horsewhip, for which offence the pugnacious
Jones spent a few months in gaol. On coining
out, Jones found that his friends had kindly pro-
vided him with a few pounds by handsomely
mounting aforesaid horsewhip in silver and raffling
it among themselves—ss. a ticket. Saddler Sur-
cingle, a friend of Gubbins, and also no enemy of
Jones, had bought a ticket in above-mentioned
raffle, out of a pleasant desire to stand well with
all men. Wfien raffle eventuated, Surcingle
found, to his horror, that he himself had won the
silver-mounted horsewhip, that the occurrence was
in the local papers, and that he had lost the
custom and friendship of Gubbins — a man re-
nowned for his love of horseflesh and all that ap-
pertains thereto.

“ Ignotus ” on that much-worried question
—“ When does the century end? ”:
I notice different opinions about this, even in journals
of high standing ; but have nowhere seen a clear explana-
tion of how it is there can be more than one. Briefly, a
man’s first birthday is when he is one year old, and, con-
sequently, his hundredth when he is 100 years old. The
first century, on the other hand, begins at the beginning
of the year 1. There is no year 0. This important point
can be gathered, I presume, from any history cover-
ing the period. Dr. Schmitz, for instance, in his
“ History of Rome,” says Tiberius stayed at Rhodes
from b.c. 6 to a.d. 2, a period of nearly seven years.
This implies that the year before a.d. 1 was B.c. 1.
Hence the first century is a year old at the end of the
year 1, and is a hundred years old, or ends, at the end of
a.d. 100. Another explanation was that a child who is called
“ one year old ”is in his second year; a man “of 25” is in
his 26th year ; a man of 99 is in his 100th year, and completes
it on his hundredth birthday, at the hour of his birth.
Yet another was: If a man says “it is my hundredth
birthday,” he means he has completed 100 years,
he has actually lived through 100 in succession. But, if
we say Trajan refused to pay the Dacian tribute on the
Ist Jan , a.d. 100, w r e mean he was then commencing the
last year of the Ist century ; and when, at the end of a.d.
100, he marched against them, then the century ended.
A London writer says the Arabian chronology begins
with the year. Ours, as I have shown, does not. A
clerical friend, assuming Christ’s birth to he Dec. 25, told
me he supposed that year would be called “ the year of
the Birth of our Lord ” ; the year before it b.c. 1, the year
after a.d. 1. This introduces another difficulty, a year
which belonged to neither the first century a.d. nor the
first century b.c. But my clerical friend was in error.
Our chronology starts from a certain Ist of January, the
year before Tiberius returned from Rhodes to Rome, and
the clerical chronologists (see authorised version of Bible,
top-note), do not scruple to say Christ was born “b.c. i
or 5.”
[The French Revolutionary Tribunal adopted the
Christian method. Year one of the Republic
begins with the establishment of the Republic,
There was no year 0. The argument of Ignotus,
if one wished to be hypercritically exact, is in-
correct, however; the man who is 21 has had 22
birthdays, and the man who is one hundred has
had 101 birthdays. Each has had, respectively,
only 21 and 100 “ anniversaries ” of his birthday.
—Ed. B.]

states, on oath, how his shattered nervous system was
completely cured and restored.
Statutory Declaration.
I, TIIOS. MUDIMAN, Master Tailor, of 327 Elizabeth-
street, Sydney, in the Colony of New South Wales, do
hereby solemnly and sincerely declare as follows: —
Whereas I suffered severely from a very troublesome
affection of the NERVES, which affected me so much that
I was quite unable to attend to my business. I tried
several doctors and many remedies, but all of no avail.
Whereas I, as a last hope, obtained some of Here Rass-
mussen’s Purely Herbal ALFALINE VITALITY PILLS,
which I hereby solemnly and sincerely declare COM-
PLETELY CURED ME within a few weeks, and although
this is over five years ago I have remained well ever since.
The ALFALINE PILLS also cured my wife, and I make
this solemn declaration conscientiously believing the same
to be true.
Declared at Sydney this 29th day of April, 1898.
THOMAS MUDIMAN.

Theory of the Flaming Sword.
“ Ah, talk of blessings ! What a blessing is diges-
tion ! To digest. L>o you know what it means ?
It is to have the sun always shining and the shade
always ready for you. It is to be met by smiles
and greeted with kisses. It is to hear sweet sounds,
to sleep with pleasant dreams, to be touched ever
by gentle, soft, cool hands. It is to be in Paradise.
“There came a great indigestion upon the earth
and it was called a deluge. All the evil comes
from this. Macbeth could not' sleep ;it was the
supper, not the murder. His wife talked and
talked ;it was the supper again. Milton had a
bad digestion, and Carlyle must have had the
worst digestion in the world. Ah !to digest is to
be happy ! ”
There ! —how does that strike you for a burst of
eloquence ? I quote from Trollope. If there is any-
thing wrong about the theology you must hold him
responsible. As for its physiology and pathology
(pardon, all these “ologies ”) I can answer for the
correctness of these two. And so can millions of
people besides me. They speak of the curse of in-
digestion continually in every language ; they
groan and writhe under it in every land and
climate.
“For many years,” says one of this innumerable
army of martyrs, “I was obliged to bear as best I
could the torments of indigestion. My appetite
was practically destroyed, I ate, of course, because
one must eat or die ! but after meals I had great
pain at the chest and around the sides.
Sleep almost forsook my pillow, and naturally I
was tired and exhausted. Sometimes better and
then worse, but never free from pain and illness, I
lived on with little or no hope of getting well. It
is hardly necessary to say that I had medical
treatment, yet no real benefit resulted from it.
Happily at this time Mother Seigel’s Syrup was
brought to my notice, and so strongly commended
that I laid aside other medicines, which were doing
me no good, and began using this one only.
“ In a short time I realised a great improvement ;
food agreed with me and I gained strength. A
little later—continuing to take the Syrup regularly
as directed —the pains at the stomach, sides and
chest wholly ceased, and I have not felt them
since. My indigestion was cured at last, and I en-
joyed the blessing of health. My son, who suffered
severely from rheumatism has been relieved by
Mother Seigel’s Syrup as by nothing else he ever
tried. In gratitude I give you full permission to
publish my letter should you desire.” (Signed)
(Mrs.) Ann Barker, Field Lane, Braughing, Ware,
Herts. Oct. 7, 1898.
It was a fortunate circumstance for Mrs. Sarah
Gell, of Melchbourne, Bedfordshire, that one day
she had a personal talk with Mr. Smith, the
butcher at Rushden. He told the lady that in his
opinion if she went on suffering from indigestion
and asthma (one of its consequences) it would be
because she neglected to use Mother Seigel’s
Syrup. “ And, ” said Mr. Smith, “I speak from
knowledge.” She had been ill with this abomin-
able ailment for many years, and had spent time
and money in unavailing efforts to obtain relief.
Acting on Mr. Smith’s advice, Mrs. Gell began
using this remedy at once, and tells the outcome
in a letter of which we have room for the con-
clusion only :
“I was better almost immediately, and was
soon as well and healthy as one could wish to be.
Now I keep ‘ Mother Siegel ’ in the house, and it
never fails to help us when needed for any passing
complaint.” (Signed) Sakah Gell, Oct. sth, 1898.
Judging from the force of his comment on the
disease, I should say Mr, Trollope knew some-
thing about indigestion from experience. Most
literary people do. To them, and to all other
victims I confidently commend the best remedy
yet found —Mother Seigel’s Syrup.
No Household should be without it. To Farmers it is
invaluable. It should be found in every Stable.
All Athletes should use it. It Cures Speedily and
Permanently.
2mmn
Aches and Pains vanish instantly. A Child may use it
without fear. It has no equal as an Embrocation. Has
effected more Cures than any other specific ever offered
to the public.

house should be without it, as, taken at. the beginning, a
dose is generally sufficient, and a complete cure is certain.
“ Mr. W. G. lIEARNE.—Dear Sir,—l had a bad attack
of Bronchitis for six months, with dreadful hacking cough
day and night and great quantity of phlegm. Night
sweats came on and pains in my chest, and I got so weak

nounced to be in consumption. I had a lot of medicines
of different kinds, but nothing did me any good until,
reading in the Age of your ‘ Bronchitis Cure,’ I got a
bottle, and after taking two doses I was much improved.
After taking two bottles the cough and night sweats left

shall recommend your Medicine to all my friends.—Yours
truly, E. SMITH, Halpin Park, Nhill, Victoria.”

Death Dreaded
AND EXPECTED,
CLEMENTS TON IQ
PERFORMS ANOTHER
Great Cure.
The Case of
MRS. MARGARET BLOMFIELD.
(From the “ Marrickville Express.”)
Mr. Frederick Blomfield is the Asphalter
to the Marrickville Council, and is widely
known and much respected. He lives in
the Illawarra Road near tram line. Mrs.
Blomfield lately recovered in a most extra-
ordinary manner from what looked like her
last illness. The doctors, after efforts as
strenuous as unavailing, were at length com-
pelled to acknowledge themselves beaten,
and gave up her case as hopefess. As a
consequence of the continual reports of the
strange “ bringing back to life ” of this
lady, our reporter was sent to interview her
and her husband. The newspaper writer
was ushered into a pretty little drawing-
room by Mr. Blomfield, who, on hearing
what was required, at once called his wife,
and remained during the whole interview,
from time to time interjecting with some
detail confirmatory of his wife’s story.
Our reporter at once noticed that Mrs.
Blomfield looked in excellent health and
spirits, and said, “ Your health looks all
right now.”
“It is splendid, indeed,” replied Mrs.
Blomfield, “ and I have never been better
in my life than I have been since my cure
some months ago, nor have I ever found it
necessary to take a dose of medicine since.”
“ Now, Mrs. Blomfield,” said the press
man, “ I would like precise and unexag-
gerated details of your illness.”
“ With pleasure,” was the response. “It
was a peculiar weakness, and seemed like
disease of the heart, and came on in
such a slow, deceitful sort of way that it was
a long time before I realised that there
was really anything wrong with me. The
five doctors thab I employed one after
another were baffled, and were entirely
unable to form a satisfactory diagnosis of
my case, though they all agreed my heart
was affected. I was ill altogether for
about twelve months. I first felt a dis-
inclination to move about. This was soon
succeeded by a sickening feeling of sinking
and weakness at the heart. I was affected
with a sort of giddiness. The room would
sometimes seem to turn with me. I had a
suspiciously big appetite. That is, I ate
a great deal more than was usual with me:
but, strange to say, I got thinner and
thinner daily. My food was of no service,
and, notwithstanding all I ate, I began
to slowly fall away like a child with a
wasting disease. I gradually became
reduced to a skeleton, and my bones
stuck out plainly through my skin. I had
long enough been forced to give up my
household d uties, and I was now obliged to
lie up. I became bedridden altogether. My
hands trembled, so with nervousness that
when I took hold of anything it would fall
from my nerveless hands. I was horribly
depressed in spirits. I could stand no sort
of a noise or shock without suffering most
acutely afterwards.”
14 And your rest ? ”
“ Ah ! that was the most painful part of
my illness. Night followed night and I never
closed my eyes, nor could I find any rest in
the daytime. I nearly went mad from want
of sleep.”
“ You couldn’t last long like this.”
“ Well, you shall hear. In addition to
my already distressing syrqptoms I was
attacked with rheumatic fever. My legs
and feet swelled terribly. Every part of my
body was 'filled with agonising aches and
pains. Intense headaches added to my
misery. I was powerless to move, and had
tobe lifted aboutandtufnedfromsidetosidein
bed. I suffered from a shocking shortness
of breath, and often I had to be raised into
an upright position to breathe, or I should
have been suffocated. And, as for my
appearance, people have told me since that
I looked as though I was knocking at
Death’s door, There seemed to be nothing
left for me but to die, and, indeed, death
sometimes seemed as though it would have
been a welcome relief. Many times at night
the neighbours would say, as I have since
heard, ‘ Ah, that poor Mrs. Blomfield—we
shall see the blinds down in the
morning. She will pass away to-night.*
Day after day as I lingered on their prophecy
proved an untrue one. But they were very
nearly right. The doctors had indeed given
me up, their medicines were unable to help
me, and, worse than all, I-had given up hope
myself. The vital spark of life was already
flickering -at any moment it might have
gone out for all time. It was just at this
crisis, and wfien all seemed lost, that an un-
expected circumstance turned the balance in
my favour. A friend of my husband’s
hurried down to see him. ‘Get a bottle of
Clements Tonic at once,’he said, ‘I have
heard that that remedy has performedalarge
number of unexpected cures around this
district; it might prove the saving of your
wife’s life.’ My husband complied with the
suggestion, and before I had taken one
bottle he had the satisfaction of finding that
a marvellous difference for the better had
boci. made in my condition by that medicine.
The pains which li id. so racked my weakened
frame ceased to trouble me, and a
feeling of serenity came over my exhausted
nervous system ; then I began to get sleep
again. The swelling in my limbs went
down, and very shortly was gone entirely.
Clements Tonic acted like magic on my
spirits. After months of melancholy I now
enjoyed a cheerfulness of spirit and firmness
of nerve, which aided my general recovery
of health to an enormous extent. In four or
five weeks so great a difference had Clements
Tonic made i n my appearance that I was hardly
to be recognised as the same woman who a
few weeks previously had been helplessly
stretched on a sick bed. My food
strengthened me, and digested with ease.
I put on flesh at a most astonishing rate.
My husband was delighted with the success
of Clements Tonic, which had so
opportunely been brought under his notice.
He took good care that I took regular doses,
and very shortly I was able to get out of
bed, and was completely restored to health,
and, as I told you, I have never felt better,
and have had no need to take Clements
Tonic since. My cure was absolute and
complete. There is no doubt as to the
wonderful vitalizing power of Clements
Tonic, nor as to the permanency of its effects.
To put it plainly and shortly, ‘ My life was
saved by Clements Tonic.’ Needless to say,
I feel grateful, and that is why I am
willing, or, more correctly speaking, anxious
to make the good qualities of this potent
remedy more widely known, and may others
receive benefit from Clements Tonic as I
did.”
“ This will appear in the newspapers.”
“ Wherever and whenever you like,”
replied Mrs. Blomfield.”

“They!”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ Credulous ! ”
It was the only charge which could have spurred
the philosopher into anything like abruptness.
The turpentine splashed from his bottle as he
slapped it down upon his table and wheeled round
to glare through his glasses like a benevolent
dragon.
“ Let me put you in your proper place upon
this matter, young man—once for all,” he began,
warmly, but then, with the methodicalness which
was the essence of him, he sat down to check off
his points upon his fingers.
“ There are three attitudes towards this ques-
tion. First —that of fatuous credulity ; second —
that of not much less fatuous incredulity ”
“ Like mine ? ” Fetherstone put in.
“Like yours,” answered the uncompromising
Beckley—“ exactly.”
“ Thanks. And the third ? ”
“ The third is the only possible present attitude
of the scientific intellect —suspended judgment,
sir ; suspended judgment.”
The old man turned to his bird-stuffing again,
and Fetherstone chewed his beard for a minute
before he said,
“But science —if it means anything —means
certainty! ”
“Science never meant certainty, and never
said so !. Science means simply the best present
explanation of phenomena. The only ‘ science ’
that ever claimed certainty is the one thing that
never proved anything—theology ! ”
Fetherstone—putting on at the same time his
cartridge-belt and a less aggressive tone —said the
jibe didn’t touch him. He was no theologian.
“ I simply don’t believe in spooks because spooks
are impossible !—and there’s an end of it! ”
Says Beckley, “ Only the beginning, perhaps !
It’s generally you cock-sure people who rush from
the one extreme to the other. I shouldn’t wonder
to find you come back a true believer! ”
Says Fetherstone—
“ Shoo! ”
* * * *
“ The kapal-antul"— and the Dyak “ head-man”
clutched his hair at mention of the spirit-ship.
“No !—he wouldn’t send any of his young men
thither. In fact, they wouldn’t go, if he did !
Did not the white man know of the Burns, many
and of much wickedness, who dwelt in that ac-
cursed ship ? ”
“Yes ; the white man knew of all that woman-
talk. And the Dyaks—who called themselves
men—were but women, after all! He would go
alone! ”
Thus, Fetherstone—jobbing his rifle-butt into
the sand, as he smiled at his own assumption that
a common weakness had brought together such
extremes as Beckley the sage and Batambu the
savage. For seldom of the Sons of Logic is the
man whose argumentative clincher is an “im-
possible!” The sage had but wasted his exposi-
tion—as the savage wasted, now, his proverbial
philosophy.
Qnir? f-ho little Dvak, with a large dignity
“White man, thou art brav e because t iou ar
blinded ! When Jirong would take the me, n
jrUherstone once more, as he
walked away along the sunset-reddened beach.
“That’s the ‘Quern Dms ’ busi ness over
But I'm not likely to ‘per dev e this turn unless
H Th?' B h ur^ iU rheSed the rocky
point-was gone by the time he had clamberedup,
and—standing by the solitary palm ] ’
to where the ancient ship sat black and sol 2
above a high-water mark that had receded fr
her with the lapse of time. There wasagnm
challenge in her sinister lonehness— as in his know
ledge that not for years had any man set foot at night
within a mile of that ill-reputed relic of an ill-
reputed past. He looked back towards the Uyak
village with much the feeling of a man who shuts
a door behind him on the light. Then, with a
sudden movement, he went on. (l
“ Shoo !” he said, for the third time. Hon t
you be an old woman, too, Phil Fetherstone .
* * * *
There was a wild wind whistling when Beckley
woke —with the suddenness and completeness born
of his adventurous life. He had that instinct,
too —primary or secondary, who shall say . which
warns some men of an unwelcome presence in the
darkness.
“That you, Fetherstone?” he called, but only
wind-shrieks answered, and the old man, feeling
first for his revolver, struck a match. He was
alone, to his eye—but, to that weird sixth sense
of his, emphatically not so ! Yet steadily he lit
his lamp, before —with the long Colt ready m his
shakeless hand —he peered into the only corner
that could hide a man. And as he looked a voice
said suddenly and close behind him —
“ To the wreck ! ”
Beckley wheeled, gaped an instant, then laughed
softly at himself. “An acoustic freak !” he said.
“There’s somebody outside.”
But there wasn’t! A third-quarter moon-
driving, green and angry, through wild wisps of
scud—showed him the platform vacant; neither,
looking over and around, could he see aught upon
the pallid waste of sand, save the cocoa-palms
writhing to the wind like tortured ghosts.
He went inside and sat down. “Fetherstone
was in my mind,” he thought; “ yet the voice
didn’t strike me as his —which is a point in favor
of its objectivity. And the words? No, they
were not imagined. I’ll go ! ”
He took brandy, a bull’s-eye lantern, matches
and cartridges, belted on his pistol, and went.
* * * *
The wind, shrieking in the old man’s ears with
many-pitched mockery of voices, flicked fiercely
in his face a sand that pricked like needles, but
as he neared the gloomy ruin of a ship he felt that
he was otherwise impeded. Faint pushes from
his front!—faint clutches from behind ! —these
things; he told himself, were neither wind nor
sand—noryetimagination; for, ashe slackened pace
to look around him, the shoves became almost
blows —the clutches strong enough to move a
standing man.
But Science serves her votaries well. In
Beckley’s mind there was no room for anything
but delight at the transcendent novelty of this
experience. “ This beats the vox et preterea
nihilJ” he said; and, coolly, in that appalling
severance from human companionship, he made
experiment—pushed energetically on, and found
the forces weaken; relaxed his pace, and found
them strengthen in the inverse ratio. “Then the
human will is still a factor ! ” he said, exultantly,
as reaohing the sombre shadow of the wreck, he
felt his movements altogether unopposed.
The wide, old-fashioned main-channel was with-
in his reach. He clambered up, and, through a
gap in the rotten bulwarks, reached a deck that
had better stood the test of time. The main-
hatch, with its coamings gone, yawned cavernous
before a sturdy stump of mast; the bollards of
some strange old anchor-getting gear still held
their place forward ; save these, nothing broke the
sweep of deck between a fore-“ castle ’’ that de-
served its name and an even loftier poop.
“ Portuguese, I fancy ! ” Beckley muttered, as
he lit his lantern. “ I’ll take the forecastle first! ”
But, as ho went, a noise behind him made him
turn. “ Oh, here you are,” he said. “ I was
beginning to think ”
His words froze up. Was this the man he
sought, coming slowly from under the break of the
poop, or was it Fetherstone’s corpse galvanised
somehow into horrible post-mortem motion? The
moonlight fell upon the face—black, as from
strangulation—twisted, as by the touch of paraly-
sis. The jaw hung down —the unretained saliva
glistened on the beard. But the eyes, distended
in a petrifying glare, had sight in them. The
man, or thing, stopped opposite Beckley and
laughed—a laugh more coldly frightful than all
else, the laugh of an unseated intellect.
“What is this, in the name of Heaven?”
Beckley laid his hand upon the rigid arm
“ What have you seen ? Where’s your rifle ? ”
The other looked back towards the poop and
shuddered till his cap fell off. “ They may come
out,” he whispered. “Oh, God ! —they may
come after me ! ” With a sudden spring, he was
over the side and off along the sand.
A moment stood the philosopher, distracted
between fear for his friend and unshaken eager-
ness to search this matter to the bottom.
“ No,” he said, ruefully, “ I must follow, were
it only as the doctor.”
For six days Fetherstone lay, differentiated
only from a log by the fact—surprising to his un-
remitting watcher —that he would swallow what
was put in his mouth. But on the morning of the
seventh day the worn-out Beckley was shaken up
by bis Dyak helper. “ See, master ! —see ! ” —and
Beckley saw. The mouth had ceased to dribble ;
and the eyes had speculation in them. But the
hair was grey ! And the first words, in their ap-
parent irrelevance, dashed from the death-fighter’s
face the exultation of victory.
Said Fetherstone, slowly, “Mackenzie’s schooner
—when —is she—due ? ”
“ Let me see,’’said wondering Beckley. “About
the end of next week, I should say.”
“ She takes me back ! ”
* * * *
“But the schooner,” said Beckley, afterwards
telling me the tale, “ didn’t get down to us for a
month, because, meantime, we had the famous
hurricane of that year.”
“Fetherstone did go back north in her, though
—didn’t he ? ”
“Yes. And I’ll answer your next question in
advance. Did I ask him ? Yes! You see, I
didn’t have the chance of going to the fountain-
head, for the hurricane broke the wreck up at last
—drove the sea further in-shore than it had been
for 20 years. So, just as he was going aboard,
I said to Fetherstone, ‘You’re very nearly your-
self again, now, Phil 1 Do you think you could
stand telling me what They were that you cried
out about that night in your horror?’ And then
I cursed myself for asking 1 ”
“Why?'”
“Well, the poor fellow started away from me
as if I had touched him with fire, and looked at
me with a face like death. Then he came back,
and whispered—‘Tell you? There are no words
to do it with / ’ ’’
ALEX. MONTGOMERY.
Connie Gilchrist’s adopted godfather, the
Duke of Beaufort, died the other day at therespect-
able age of 75. He had lived long enough to
become respectable, but in his middle age Beau-
fort was known to the sporting and theatrical
worlds as the naughty mentor of young noblemen.
Thirty odd years ago he helped to make the pace
for the Marquis of Hastings, the young Duke of
Hamilton, and the Duke of Newcastle, all three
of whom got into the clutches of the Israelites.
“ The Marquis ” dissipated himself to death
shortly after his financial collapse. Newcastle
died friendless in a cheap lodging - house at
Boulogne, or somewhere. The Duke of Hamilton’s
vast estates were “ nursed ” for years, and he
came back to sassiety a somewhat reformed noble-
man. All this time Beaufort was enjoying him-
self, as usual. He was a little less imprudent
than his disciples, and he never quite came to
grief. Beaufort always had a nice little “ actress”
or two wearing his diamonds in a snug little
villa, but as he waxed in years the companionship
of sprightly lordlings led to occasional embarrass-
ment concerning the ladies. Instance the case of
a certain young peer (afterwards a jaded Australian
Governor) who became so conspicuously attentive
to one of dear old Beaufort’s “ actresses ” that his
Grace suggested a transfer of the property.
“You are always- haw !—hanging around it,”
said the now elderly sinner, “and I think you
might as well buy the whole bag of tricks.” So
the villa, furniture, equipages, &c., were valued in
an amicable way by the solicitors of the respective
peers, and the delightful lydy changed hands.
Although the late lamented Knight of the Garter
was a gentleman according to his lights, his
paternal example proved disastrous in at least one
case. The Lord Somerset (equerry to the Duke
of Clarence) who figured in the hideous Cleveland-
street scandal, and fled from England, leaving his
army commission to be cancelled, was Beaufort’s
son.
Again, on the selection of firemen :
Dear Bulletin. —“H.R.A.” talks about hook-and-
ladder, but compare the modern fire-escape ladder-a
regular staircase —with the ladders and scaffolds in use
by city painters. Also, ship-rigging, for the safety of the
ship must he strong and durable, whereas, owing to the
cut-and slash style of painting, scaffolding must be quick
and cheap. I have seen men in Sydney standing and
using both hands on swinging scaffolds merely hooked on
to a parapet ; no guard-rail — and a clothes-line as a safety-
line ! Have also seen a man work on a 50ft. ladder when
it took one man to hold it at the top and two men at the
bottom — and he had never been a sailor. Moreover, a
painter will climb over roofs with step-ladders and pots
scoot around chimneys and eaves —eight hours at a
stretch ; yet a fancy sailor-fireman considers it a feat to
carry a mate down the patent staircase called a fire-
ladder. Painters also acquire a sort of instinct as to
windows, doors, ledges, down-pipes, and the like, which
it must take a sailor long to develop. I should like to
set a few sailors to explore—say, the Hotel Metropole—
even without a fire raging.—Jack S.

ciation is the latest project thereabout.

early preceptors, gives that leading idol’s youth-
ful version of a well-known Scriptural event: —
Please, sir, Gawd said to Gideon, “ I say, just you take
a lot of pots and pans and old broken jugs, and anything
that’ll make a row, and go and frighten the Midianites,”
and please, sir, he did, and when the enemy heard the
row they all killed each other, and that was stalky of
him, I think.
Unfortunately, the dominie was also somewhat
“stalky,” and having overheard that Kipling’s
picturesque audacity depended upon a schoolboy
bet, gave Rudyard an impressive lesson upon the
difference between literary styles.

~ , T f lß question is frequently asked—Why are diseases of the kidneys so fatal? The answer is
that diseases of the kidneys are seldom detected until they reach a dangerous stage When the
kidneys become affected there are few alarming symptoms to excite fear, and the disease slowly
extends into the more vital tissues. In the majority of cases the patient does not feel as well as he
should, but he blames it on the weather, overwork, being on his feet too long, or thinks the pain in
ms back is rheumatic. His family doctor advises him to go to the country for a few weeks. He
may feel better when he comes back, but the disease in the kidneys has grown worse every day
though he does not know it. This may continue for months, or even years, before sharp pains in the
back, limbs, or head, or dropsy of the feet and ankles, develop. When that comes, it is too late in
many, cases. *

The Secrets of the Moon.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I lie at night and watch her,
As she sails across the sky
With the hidden joys and sorrows
That we know not, you and I;
And I watch her drifting onward
Over crag and dale and dune,
And I wish that I could only .
Hear the secrets of the Moon.
And she moves so brave and queenly
Where her silver clouds are curled,
You would hardly think her freighted
With the cares of all the world ;
If the fairies would be friendly,
If the gods would grant a boon,
Let them softly say me over
All the secrets of the Moon.
She has heard the clink of glasses
Ring to meet her through the stars,
She has loitered by dim gardens
Jlist to light the love-guitars ;
For the night will twine rose-garlands,
And the world’s a brave buffoon,
And we’d learn of love and laughter
In the secrets of the Moon.
She has lingered by Earth’s cradles,
She has kissed the sleeping child,
And has woven such dream-fancies
That he turned to her and smiled ;
She has led the witches’ dances,
Sewn the diamonds on their shoon—
Ah ! Our hearts would grow the younger
For the secrets of the Moon !
She has crossed by many a city,
She has smiled on mosque and tower,
She has heard from many a steeple
Clash the long nights, hour by hour,
While she kissed the wine-wild maidens
In the gilded gay saloon—
Ah ! They’d set a strong man weeping,
Would the secrets of the Moon !
She has topped the tallest mountain,
She has lit the darkest seas,
And the midnight and the morning
They have met upon her knees,
She has heard the night-wind sighing
Songs of love to the lagoon,
And the words the four winds whisper
Are the secrets of the Moon.
And until the Earth is sundered,
And the darkened seas go dry,
She will keep the silver secrets
She has won from earth and sky,
But perhaps when stars are shrouded,
And the night is as the noon,
We shall learn the joys and sorrows
Of the secrets of the Moon !
WILL H. OGILFIE.
After Many Years.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Shb met him in her teens, when life’s glamor
was upon them. She was simple and unsophis-
ticated ; he, high-spirited and manly. In her
twenties, they drifted apart in pride and mis-
understanding. The “ eternal love ” of a boy
and a girl became a thing of far-back days.
In her thirties, she one day efttered a railway-
carriage—a smiling, gracious woman of the world.
In the carriage was a single occupant. She bowed
and smiled as she recognised her fellow-traveller,
then sank into a window corner and tried to think
of nothing in particular as she watched the dis-
appearing trees and cottages. It would take two
hours to reach the next station.
How long it was since they had met, and how
dear he had been ! Even now she could feel his
lips upon her hair, and his strong hands imprison-
ing hers. She glanced towards him. What a
look of pain was on his face ! He, too, must be
thinking. Again she looked. Without a word he
held out his arms. Ah ! how sweet the time when
she could creep into them as in a sheltered fold.
Suddenly the weight of years rolled from her.
She was a girl again, and he her king. Throwing
aside her heavy cloak she swept towards him with
a sob that was almost a gasp, and the next moment
was folded close in his embrace, her face buried on
his shoulder.
O, I rank ! ” she cried, “Erank, forgive me.
I did kiss Charlie on the balcony that evening •
but I never thought such a slight sin would bring
so great a punishment.”
“My love,” he answered, “at last we under-
stand each other ! ”
“At last! ” she echoed, as she held up her red
lips for a kiss, and almost unconsciously pushed
the brown curls from off his forehead as in past
days.
“Oh, why was fate so cruel ? Why were we
parted so long ? ”
“ Nothing shall part us again, Nellie.’’
“ No, nothing in Heaven or on earth. lam
yours now, and for ever, and ever.”
Once again were passionate kisses given and re-
turned, once again was the golden head cushioned
on the heart that made so sweet a resting-place.
“ How far are you going, Erank ? ”
. “To the end of the world, pet ; to the very end
of our lives together.”
She lifted her head, and half drew herself from
his arms.
“ What do you mean ? ”
. I mean what you said just now, that nothing
in Heaven or on earth shall take you from me, or
me from you again.”
“ I was mad ! ” she answered. “ But let me
be yours for five minutes longer ; then—love must
die and life end. Kiss me again—again ! ”
The train.whistled. She tore herself from his
arms, threw on her cloak, and took her sunshade
from the rack.
He stood before her, wonder-stricken.
“ Good-bye,” she said, bowing and smiling ; and
the same gracious woman who had entered the
carriage at the last station left it at this; and,
without a trace of emotion on her fair face, joined
her husband and children on the platform.
VICTOR ZEAL.
Romeo—An Equatorial Love-
Story.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Ihe sandalwooders had christened her “ Annie
Laurie.” Why, Heaven alone knows— unless it
was, as Annie herself used often say, “Them
sailors is contrarious brutes.” She was over 50
with short grey hair, small eyes (like a pig’s, ac-
cording to the natives), a flat nose, no neck, and
as fat as a hippopotamus. Where she came from
was a, mystery. Women such as she were occa-
sionally found in the more settled parts of Poly-
nesia, but how did she get down to the New
Hebrides ?
Swum,” was the laconic reply to a delicately-
worded question on this subject. For my part,
I believe she was one of Sydney’s “ nymphs of the
pavement ” carried away by a whaling crew and
left stranded somewhere in Eastern Polynesia,
whence she had worked gradually back, from
island to island, by favor of passing whalers and
traders.
She dealt in sandalwood—then in copra when the
wood became scarce. She also entertained visiting
sailors of course at their own expense. And
when I tell you that her loud, strident voice and
skilful handling of a yam-pole kept her rough
visitors in exemplary order—you will have some
dim idea of the awe with which she was regarded
by the islanders.
A fair-sized block of land surrounded her house
—bought, so she said, from the natives. “ Didn’t
I give yez twenty dollars trade for this bit o’
dirt ? she would demand, and the terror-stricken
creatures would nod vehemently.
Annie had a lover! A passionate, faithful
being- the very model of a lover ! Despite her
frowns, her scorn, and her oft-repeated threats to
knock his brains out (which, by the way, she often
nearly succeeded in doing), he still remained true.
Hung about her house, sitting at the doorway
until ordered to her taro patch. Here he would
work all day and return to his old seat in the
evening. When Annie expected visitors she
kicked him off the step. He would submit with-
out a murmur until his mistress was out of hear-
ing—then the air would become thick with curses
Komeo s past history was as much a puzzle as
that of the object of his affections. He had been
born in Queensland, and, though of what exact
parentage it would be hard to say, he had pro-
bably in him, besides kanaka and Chinese blood
a strain of the white Queenslander.
However that might be, he was quite different
from the natives of the island, and was considered
by them an outcast—in fact, he owed his existence
to the patronage of Annie.
His devotion and Annie’s perverseness were
the joke of the sandalwood-trade, and the sailors
kicked and cuffed the poor brute unmercifully
when they ran across him at the “ Chapel ”—as
Annie’s residence was called. Once, a shellback
meeting him on the beach, kicked him as usual,
but a knife between the ribs convinced this man
and others that JEtomeo was only fair game for
brutality when under the jurisdiction of Annie.
I Beveral times ventured to expostulate with Annie
about her coyness, but always received the same
reply : “ Rot! Raney a woman of my age taking
up with a dirty black ! ” Then I would go to
Romeo, and try to impress upon him the hope-
lessness of his suit, and persuade him to ship for a
cruise with us. But no—he would shake his head,
and Wander back to the doorstep again.
It was “ Big Mitchell ” who cut this romance
short. It’s not a nice story.
Mitchell had onoe been first harpooner on a
whaler, but the long cruises prevented him enjoy-
ing as much women’s society as he wished, so he
was now mate of a sandalwooder. A hulking
great blackguard he was, and even Annie would
not have him in her house unless a couple of others
were there to assist in keeping order. One day
he came ashore drunk and set out for the
Chapel. I was on board at the time, and only
heard of the matter afterwards. Annie met him
at the door.
“ Yez can’t come in,” she said.
“ Well, bring a bottle out here, you hag ! ”
“Not me! Gro an’ask yer skipper.” With an
oath Mitchell rushed at the woman, and, catching
her by the throat, had almost choked her before he
was forced to turn and defend himself from the
furious onslaught of Romeo. There was no fight
—Mitchell’s arm was gashed—but in a moment
the black was whirling in the air, and then, crash-
ing against the bamboo walls, fell in a heap on the
ground.
We patched up the poor fellow as well as we
could, but after all our handiwork a sorrier-look-
ing Romeo never lived. Worse still—he was an
idiot. But he was still the same quiet creature as
of old, except when he saw Annie—then he would
spit at her, gnash his teeth, and hobble away.
